The author of this book says: When I have completed for you, O my sons, this book, which is a part of medical science in its entirety, and have reached the utmost clarity and explanation in it, I saw fit to complete it for you with this chapter, which is a part of practical work.
Because practical work is completely lacking in our country and in our time, so much so that its knowledge is almost extinct and its effect has ceased. And there remain only a few traces of it in the books of the ancients, which have been copied by hands and marred by mistakes and confusion, until its meanings have become obscure and its benefit has been lost.
Therefore, I saw fit to revive it and compose this chapter on it in a way that is explanatory, clear, and concise, and to include pictures of cauterizing irons and other surgical instruments, since this adds to clarity and is essential to what is needed.
The reason why there is no skilled craftsman in our time is that the craft of medicine is lengthy, and its practitioner must first study anatomy, as described by Galen. so that he may understand the functions, forms, temperaments, connections, and separations of the organs, and the knowledge of the bones, nerves, muscles, their number, and their origins, the arteries and veins, and the locations of their origins.
Therefore, Hippocrates said that physicians are many in name but few in deed, especially in the craft of surgery, and we have mentioned a part of this in the introduction to this book.
For he who is not knowledgeable in the anatomy we have mentioned will inevitably make mistakes that kill people, as I have often witnessed among those who have imagined themselves skilled in this science and claimed it without knowledge or training.
For I saw an ignorant physician who incised a scrofulous tumor in a woman's neck, and he severed some of the arteries of the neck, so the woman bled to death in his hands.
And I saw another physician who proceeded to remove a stone from a man who had a fistula, and the stone was large, so he acted rashly and removed it with a piece of the substance of the bladder. And the man died after about three days, and I had been summoned to remove it, and I saw from the size of the stone and the state of the patient that I could have done so.
And I saw another physician who earned his living practicing medicine among some of the brothel keepers of our city.
Then a black boy came to him with a fracture in his leg near the heel, together with a wound.  The physician, in his ignorance, hastened to bind the fracture over the wound with bandages and splints very tightly, and he did not leave the wound any ventilation.
Then he gave free rein to his desires, then he left him for days and ordered him not to untie the bandage until his leg and foot swelled and he was near death.
Then I was summoned to him, and I hastened to untie the bandage, so he found relief and was freed from his pains, except that the corruption had become firmly established in the limb, and I could not prevent it.
And the corruption continued to spread in the limb until he died.
And I saw another physician cauterize a cancerous tumor, and it ulcerated after days until the misfortune of its owner increased.  That is because if a cancer is purely of a black humor, then it is not at all proper to apply iron to it.
Unless it is in a limb that can be entirely excised.
Therefore, my son, you should know that manual work is divided into two parts: work accompanied by safety, and work accompanied by harm in most instances.
And I have warned in every place this book comes to, of the work which contains danger and fear, so you should avoid it and refuse it.
Lest the ignorant find a way to speak and criticize, so take for yourselves firmness and caution, and for your patients gentleness and deliberation.
And use the best method leading to safety and a commendable outcome, and avoid dangerous diseases difficult to cure.
And purify yourselves from that which you fear might cast suspicion on your religion and your worldly affairs, for this is more lasting for your reputation and higher in this world and the next for your standing.
For Galen said in some of his precepts, "Do not treat a bad disease, lest you be called bad physicians.
This chapter has been divided into three sections.
The first section.
On cauterization with fire and cauterization with strong medicine, arranged systematically from head to foot, with illustrations of the instruments and cautery irons, and everything needed for the work by hand.
And the second section.
On incision, perforation, bloodletting, cupping, wounds, and the extraction of arrows and the like, all arranged systematically, with illustrations of the instruments.
And the third section.
On setting (bones), dislocations, and the treatment of sprains and the like, arranged systematically from head to foot, with illustrations of the instruments.
The first section.
On cautery.
And before we mention its use, it is necessary to mention its benefits and harms, and in what temperament it is used.
And I say that discussion of the benefit and harm of cautery is lengthy discourse, a precise science, and a hidden secret, and a number of wise men have spoken about it.
And they differed in it, and I have shortened their words for fear of lengthening.
And I say that cautery generally benefits every bad temperament that is with a substance and without a substance, except for two temperaments, namely the hot temperament without a substance and the dry temperament without a substance.
As for the dry temperament with a substance, they differed in it. Some said that cautery is beneficial in it, and others said the opposite, that cautery is not suitable in a disease that is from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
And it is impossible to be cured of a hot, dry disease with a hot, dry medicine. And he who says the opposite said that cautery with fire may benefit from a hot, dry disease that occurs in the bodies of people. Because when you add the body of man and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you make the body of man cold.
And I say as he says, because experience has revealed that to me many times, except that one should not imagine that matter except one who has trained and practiced in the field of cautery to a great extent. And he stopped at the difference in the temperaments of people and the state of diseases in themselves, their causes, their symptoms, and their duration.
But as for the other temperaments, there is no fear for you from them, especially cold, moist diseases.  All physicians have agreed upon them and have not differed in the benefit of cautery in them.
And know, O my son, that among the secrets of treatment is cautery with fire and its superiority over cautery with burning medicine, because fire is a simple element whose effect does not exceed the member that was cauterized, and it does not harm another connected member except slightly.
And cautery with burning medicine may exceed its effect to what is beyond the members, and may perhaps cause in the member a disease difficult to treat, and may perhaps kill. Fire, because of its nobility and the excellence of its essence, does not do that except if one exaggerates.
And this has become clear to us through experience, long service, attention to the craft, and understanding of the realities of the matter.
Therefore, I have dispensed with lengthy discourse.  Were it not unbecoming of this book of mine, I would have presented to you concerning fire a hidden secret and the manner of its action in bodies and its expulsion of disease with philosophical, demonstrative discourse too subtle for your understanding.
And know, O my sons, that they have differed concerning the time suitable for cautery, and they considered the best time to be springtime.
And I say that cautery is suitable at every time, because the harm resulting from the time is absorbed in the benefit which is brought about by cautery. Especially if the cautery is for strong, necessary, urgent pains which do not tolerate delay, because of the fear that what follows from them may be a calamity greater than the slight harm which comes from the time.
And let it not occur to you, O my sons, what the common people and ignorant physicians imagine, that cautery which cures a disease will never return, and that you make it a prohibition.
And the matter is not as they suppose, because cautery is only like a medicine which alters the temperament and dries the humors which are the cause of the pains.
Except that cautery is preferred to medicine in the speed of its success, the strength of its action, and the intensity of its power.
And it is possible that the disease may return after a time, according to the temperament of the patient, the strength of his disease, and what may be prepared in his body from the gathering of humors in it, and his neglect of himself in acquiring them from foods and similar causes.
Except that the disease in which cautery is used is a mild disease and in a member with few humors and moistures. Like cauterizing a tooth for pain and the like; it is possible that that pain may not return, and that is in the least.
And as for the common people's saying also that cautery is the last of medicine, it is a true saying, not to what they go to, for they believe that no treatment benefits with medicine or without it after the occurrence of cautery, and the matter is otherwise.
And the meaning of cautery being the last of medicine is only that when we use types of treatment in a disease and those medicines do not work, then we use cautery as the last thing, and it works; from here it occurred that cautery is the last of medicine. Not in the sense to which the common people and many ignorant physicians went.
The ancients mentioned that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they said that because of the moderation of gold and the nobility of its essence, and they said that the place of cautery does not suppurate; and that is not absolutely the case.
Because I have tried that and found that it only does that in some bodies and not others, and cautery with it is better and superior to iron, as they said, except that if you heat the cautery iron in the fire, made of gold, you will not know when it is heated to the degree you want because of the redness of the gold.
And because cold quickly affects it, and if you increase the heat, it melts in the fire and is poured, and the artisan falls into trouble because of that; therefore, cautery with iron is, for us, quicker and nearer to correctness in practice, God willing.
And I have arranged this chapter on cautery into sections that I have organized from the head to the foot, to make it easier for the student what he wants from it, God willing.
The first chapter.
In cauterizing the head, one cauterization.
This cauterization benefits from the predominance of moisture and coldness on the brain, which are the cause of headache and frequent colds from the head to the area of the eyes and ears, excessive sleep, and toothache and throat pain. And in general, for every disease that occurs from cold, such as paralysis, epilepsy, and catalepsy, and similar diseases.
The image of this cauterization is that you first order the patient to vomit with a purgative medicine that cleanses the head for three or four nights, according to the strength, age, and habit of the patient.
Then you order him to shave his head with a razor, then you seat him before you cross-legged, having placed his hands on his chest.
Then place the base of your palm on the base of his nose between his eyes; where your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive-shaped cautery, whose image is this.
Expose the bone the amount of a clove or a carob seed; then lift your hand, or if not, replace your hand with the same or another instrument if it cools. Until you see of the bone what I mentioned to you, then take some salt, dissolve it in water, and soak a piece of cotton in it, and place it on the spot, and leave it for three days.
Then apply a cotton swab soaked in butter and leave it on until the scab from the cautery is gone, then treat it with the four-ingredient ointment until it heals, God willing.
And they have said that the more a wound remains open, the more pus it discharges, and that is better and more beneficial.
Some have mentioned cauterizing the skin to the bone, holding the cautery until some of the thickness of the bone is burned, then removing what was burned from the bone afterwards, then treating it.
And others said it is necessary to exaggerate with the cautery until it strongly affects the bone, until it falls from the bone like a small carat or disc, and they claimed that it vents from that spot the vapors of the head, and they leave the wound open for a long time.
Then treat it until it heals. I do not see these two types of cautery at all except in some people and by way of risk, and in my opinion leaving it is better and safer. If it is done, the head weakens when its natural connection is broken, as we have seen in other members.
Especially if the patient's head is naturally weak. The first type of cautery is safer and better in my opinion, and I use it, so use it and be safe, God willing.
Chapter Two.
Also on cauterizing the head.
If a chronic pain occurs in the head and lasts for the patient, and poultices, collyria, snuffs, ointments, and dressings have been used, especially if he has been cauterized with the single cautery that we described and nothing of that has benefited him, then look. If the patient's head is naturally strong and not weak, and he feels intense cold, cauterize him again a little above that. Then cauterize each temple of his head until the thickness of the skin is gone and the amount of bone we described is revealed. And cauterize him in the back of his head in the place known as the hatchet, and lighten your hand in this and do not uncover the bone, for the patient feels a severe pain from it, unlike the pain of all the other cauterizations of the head, and I will mention this cautery in its place.
The cautery with which the temples and the back of the head are cauterized should be gentler than the cautery with which the middle of the head is cauterized, and this is its image.
Chapter Three.
On cauterizing non-chronic migraine.
If pain occurs in a fissure of the head with headache, and the pain extends to the eye, then let the patient vomit with purgative medicines for the head, and use all the treatment that I mentioned in the divisions of diseases. If that does not work, then cauterization is done in two ways. Either cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine or with iron.
As for cauterization with a burning medicine, take one clove of garlic, peel it, and cut its ends from both sides. Then, incise the place of pain in the temple with a wide scalpel until there is a place under the skin that will contain the clove.
Then insert it under the skin until it disappears. Then, bind it tightly with bandages, and leave it for about fifteen hours. Then, unbind it, remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, then dress it.
Apply cotton soaked in butter until the place suppurates, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.  And if you wish, do this with some of the burning medicines that I mentioned in the eighteenth chapter on burning medicines.
As for cauterization with iron, it is in this manner: Heat the cautery iron, which is depicted as follows:
It is called the nail cautery because its head is like the head of a nail; it has some perforation and a small projection in its middle.
Then place it on the painful area and hold your hand while you rotate the iron little by little; the amount of skin thickness you burn should be about half. And lift your hand so as not to burn the artery underneath and cause bleeding, then soak cotton in salt water and place it on the area and leave it for three days. Then dress the cotton with butter, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize this migraine with the knife-like end of the cautery, and be careful not to cut the artery in this chronic migraine in particular.
Chapter Four.
Chronic migraine.
If you treat the migraine with the aforementioned treatments and what we mentioned in the classifications of diseases, and the treatment is not effective, and you see a cause for which the first cauterization with medicine or cauterization with fire is not sufficient. Then you should heat the knife-like cautery until it turns white, after you have marked the painful area with a line half a finger-length or so, and you should leave your hand once while pulling it, until you cut the artery and reach toward the bone.
Except that you should be careful not to touch the moving jaw during chewing, lest you burn the muscle or its motor nerve and cause spasms.
And be cautious and watchful of the bleeding from the artery which you cut, for there is danger in cutting it. Especially for one who is ignorant of what he does and is not an experienced practitioner; and leaving the work is better.
And the treatment of bleeding which occurs from the artery will be mentioned in its proper place in the book, God willing; if you see from the ailment something which this cautery does not overcome.
And you see the patient's body capable, cauterize in the middle of the head as we described, and treat the wound until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, use the cautery which we mentioned in the chapter on the incision of the artery with the two-bladed cautery, for it is a better and more effective cautery than this.
Chapter Five.
In cauterizing the pains of the ears.
If pain occurs in the ear due to cold, and it is treated with purgatives and all the treatment which we mentioned in the section, and the pain does not go away, then heat the cautery which is called the point, whose image is this.
Then, after heating it, apply it in points around the whole ear, or around both if the pain is in both. And keep the cauterization a little away from the root of the ear, after marking the place with ink.  The cauterization should be about ten points in each ear, or thereabouts. Then treat the areas until they heal, God willing.
Chapter Six.
On cauterizing paralysis.
The paralysis treated by cauterization is that type which arises from phlegm, as I mentioned in the classification of diseases.
One should avoid cauterizing the type which arises from dryness and nerve tension.
When you have treated this type of paralysis with applications, snuffs, and gargles, and your treatment has been ineffective, then the patient should be cauterized with three cauterizations. One at the root of the ear, the second a little below his temple, and the third at the juncture of the lips.
Make your cauterization on the side opposite the diseased side, for the relaxation occurs on the side which appears healthy.
The image of the cauterization is that you cauterize with a cauterization opposite the upper tip of the ear, a little below the horn of the head, and another in the temple, its length being the length of the thumb.
Lower your hand with the cautery until you burn about half the thickness of the skin. This is the image of the cautery; it is a type of scalpel, the image of which has preceded, except that it is a little gentler than it, as you see. The scalpel should have a little more thickness. Then treat the area as previously mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
Cauterization of chronic apoplexy.
If the apoplexy becomes chronic and you treat it with what we have mentioned, and your treatment is not effective, and the patient does not have a fever, cauterize four times. One cauterization on each temple, one cauterization in the middle of the head as we have mentioned, and one cauterization at the back of the head as previously described.
The application of the cautery is as previously described.  It may also be cauterized on the stomach, which will be more effective. Then treat as previously described.
Chapter Eight.
On cauterization for forgetfulness.
If it is due to phlegm, the patient should first be given strong purgatives and brain-cleansing pills.
Then his entire head should be shaved and a mustard plaster, as described in the chapter on plasters, should be applied to the back of his head repeatedly, for this is a type of cauterization.
Do this in the same manner as described there. If he is cured by this, well and good; otherwise, perform three cauterizations on the back of his head, arranged in a row from the top of the head to the nape of the neck.
Make the distance between each cauterization the width of a finger. Then treat the cauterization as described before. If you wish to increase [the number of cauterizations] and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the middle one.
If you wish to increase [the number of cauterizations], cauterize the temples, then treat it until it heals. The cautery should be olive-shaped, as described before.
Chapter Nine.
On cauterizing paralysis and general body relaxation.
It is necessary that you proceed to purify the head with purgatives and what we have mentioned. Then shave the patient's head, then cauterize: one cauterization in the middle of the head, one on each temple, and one on the back of the head.
And three on the vertebrae of the neck. If, in a case of body relaxation, you need more than that, and the patient can tolerate it, and the disease is strong and intractable, then cauterize four times on the vertebrae of his back. And apply the cautery until you burn most of the skin, then lift your hand, then treat him as previously mentioned until he is healed, God willing. The cautery should be olive-shaped.
Chapter Ten.
On cauterizing epilepsy.
Only the epileptic whose epilepsy is caused by phlegm is cauterized.  It is necessary to first purify his brain with major purges and all other treatments which we mentioned in the classification, if the patient is an adult and able to take medicine.
But if he is a child who cannot tolerate medicines, then let him use gargles and masticatories that purify the brain for many days beforehand, along with improving his diet.
Then his head is shaved, then one cauterization is performed in the middle of the head as previously described, and another cauterization at the back of the head. And one cauterization on each temple of his head. If the patient is strong and able to withstand it, then perform the cauterizations which were mentioned for the patient with paralysis and body relaxation, on the vertebrae of the neck and vertebrae of the back. The cautery should be olive-shaped, as previously described.
If the patient is a child, make the cautery gentle in this manner.
Chapter Eleven.
On cauterizing melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is corrupt humors and thick phlegm, then perform the cauterizations which we mentioned for the paralytic patient.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess tending towards black bile and the patient's body is moist, then purge him so as to cleanse his brain, as was previously explained in the classification.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a tightly-woven linen cake in the shape of a circle, then place it in the middle of his head while the patient sits cross-legged holding it on all sides, then take one pound of old sheep fat.
Then heat it over a fire to a moderate temperature, such that the finger can tolerate it when inserted, then pour it onto the middle of his head within the circle and leave it until it cools. Do this for the patient once a week, along with other good management, until he is cured, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize it with many small punctures, without holding your hand to the cautery iron but rather by waving it; for this type of cauterization moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply a cotton wool pad soaked in sheep fat or chicken fat to it.
Chapter Twelve.
On cauterizing a cataract in the eye.
If the onset of a cataract in the eye becomes apparent to you through the signs mentioned in the classification, then act quickly and purge the patient of what will purify his head and protect him from all humors, and sweat him in the bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave his head and apply a cautery to the middle of the head, and then two cauteries to the temples if the onset of the cataract is in both eyes, or to one side if it is in one eye.
Then cut with the cautery all the veins and arteries under the skin, and let the cauterizations be lengthwise across the width of the temples, and guard against bleeding.
If you see any bleeding, stop it immediately with any treatment available to you.  We will discuss the wisdom of cauterizing and cutting arteries, and preventing bleeding.
And he may be cauterized at the nape of the neck, under the two bones, with two deep cauterizations.
Chapter Thirteen.
On cauterizing chronic weeping sores.
If the watering of the eye is chronic and persistent, and originates from the veins and arteries on the surface of the head, outside the head, and you are certain that it is caused by a cold, thick, phlegmatic excess, then cauterize it in the manner described at the beginning of the treatment for running eyes. One cauterization in the middle of the head, and two cauterizations on the temples, and two cauterizations at the nape of the neck under the two bones; and if more is needed, cauterize one on each side of the tail of the eye at the edge of the eyebrow with a small cautery iron.
Chapter Fourteen.
On cauterizing the foul smell of the nose.
If you treat it with what we mentioned in the classification and the treatment is not effective, then immediately purge the patient with colocynth for three nights, then shave his head and cauterize the middle area with an olive-shaped cautery iron. Then cauterize with the nail-shaped cautery iron, two cauterizations above the eyebrows, a little below the hair, and be careful of the artery, do not cut it; and this is the image of the nail-shaped cautery iron.
Chapter Fifteen.
On cauterizing the relaxation of the eyelid.
If the eyelid relaxes due to illness or moisture, cauterize the eyelid with one cauterization using this crescent-shaped cautery iron.
And if you wish, cauterize a little above the eyebrows, two cauterizations on each side, and keep a distance from the temples; and the length of each cauterization should be the length of the eyebrow.
And do not exaggerate with the cauterization, but only to the extent that a third of the skin is burnt, and the shape of the cautery iron is like this.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cauterizing the eyelid.
If its margins are inverted inwards so that it pricks the eye, then cauterization is of two types. Either cauterization with fire or cauterization with a burning medicine. As for cauterization with fire, you should order the patient beforehand to leave his eyelashes, if he is one who plucks them, until they grow long and even.
If it pricks him while they are growing, then tie his eyes with a bandage so that they do not move until they grow.  Then, when they have grown and become even, place the patient's head in your lap, and then mark on the eyelid with ink a sign in the shape of an Asian leaf.
The beginning of the mark should be near the margins, then place under the eyelid cotton soaked in egg white or in the saliva of a lizard. Then heat a cautery iron, this is its image.
Then cauterize according to the marked shape little by little, many times, until the surface of the skin, which is in the shape of the Asian leaf, is all burnt, especially its surface. A sign of the success of your work is that you see the eyelid curled and the hair raised from the flesh of the eye.
Then lift your hand and leave it for three days, then apply cotton with butter to it until the scab falls off, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If some hair returns after a while and the eyelid relaxes,
repeat the cauterization in that place as you did before. If the hair is in the lower eyelid, pull it out until it returns to its natural place and the hair does not prick the eye. As for cauterization with a burning medication, it is that you instruct the patient to leave the margins until they lengthen and become even.
Then make from paper the shape of an Asian leaf, then take from the usual soap and from unslaked lime, of each one a drachm weight or so, and crush them both well together, and do that quickly lest it dry. Then spread some of it on the paper that you made in the shape of the Asian leaf and place it on one or both eyelids, and place under the eye cotton soaked in egg white, and the patient's head in your lap.
And place your index finger on top of the medication and press it slightly, and you move it whenever the patient feels the sting of the medication, for he will feel a burning sensation like fire. As long as he feels the sting, leave the medication and move it with your finger. When the stinging stops, remove the medication and wash the eye with water.
And look, if you see that the eyelid has risen as it rises when cauterized with fire or by cutting; otherwise, apply more of the medication to the place where the medication did not affect and did not blacken, until you complete your work and the eyelid is cauterized.  Then apply cotton with fat to it until the burning subsides.
Then treat it with palm ointment or otherwise until it heals.  And it is necessary for you during the procedure to be extremely careful that nothing of the medication falls into the eye. If the eyelid relaxes after days, and you fear something in the eye, remove the hair.
Then reapply the medication to that relaxed part of the eyelid, specifically as you did initially, then treat it until it heals.  This is an image of the Asian leaf.
Know that people's eyes differ in size, so your cauterization should be according to that. The correct method is not hidden from someone experienced in this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cauterizing the ulcer that appears in the canthus of the eye.
If you treat the fistula with what we mentioned in the classification of diseases and your treatment is not effective, then it should be cauterized in this manner.
Order the patient to place his head in your lap, and let a servant hold his head in your hands firmly so that he does not move or shake his head.
Then, place a cotton ball moistened with egg white or saliva on his eyes, then heat the cautery which is of this form.
It should be hollow like the quill of an eagle's feather on one end, which is the cauterizing end; and if you wish, it can be perforated on the other end, or if you wish, it can be solid like an awl, but this hollow one is better for your work, God willing.
Then, squeeze the fistula if it is open and remove its contents and dry it; or if it is not open, open it and remove its pus. Then, place the cautery on it while it is very hot, holding it with your hand until you reach the bone.
And move your hand slightly away during cauterization from the eye towards the nose, lest your hand should err or the patient should become anxious and the cautery should fall on the conjunctiva and damage it. If you reach the bone on the first cauterization, that is sufficient; otherwise, repeat the cauterization a second time if necessary.
Leave it for three days, then apply a cotton ball with butter and treat it with drying ointment until it heals. If forty days pass and it does not heal, apply a strong, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and denude it as will be mentioned in its chapter, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing a fistula, mentioned by some of the ancients:  You aim at the location of the fistula, incise it, then place a thin cone in the incision itself; this is its image.
Pour into it the weight of a dirham of molten lead, and hold your hand firmly on the cone, and the patient should remain completely still, lest the molten lead flow into his eye; and it is necessary to place on the patient's eye cotton soaked in egg white or in water.
For the lead burns the fistula and heals it wonderfully. If the fistula heals with the cauterization and treatment mentioned, that is sufficient; otherwise, it is necessary to use nasal perforation and to return the fistula to the nasal passage, as will be described in its appropriate place.
Chapter Eighteen.
On cauterizing a cleft lip.
A cleft often occurs in the lip, which is called a hair, especially in the lips of boys; it occurs frequently. If you treat this cleft with what we mentioned in the section and the treatment is not effective, then heat a small, knife-like cautery of this shape.
Its cavity should be as thin as a knife. Then apply it, hot and quickly, to the cleft itself, until the cauterization reaches the depth of the cleft; then treat it with cerate until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Nineteen.
On cauterizing a fistula occurring in the mouth.
If a swelling appears at the root of the gums, or in the palate, or at the roots of the molars, then it suppurates and bursts, and from the running pus a fistula results, and you treat it but the treatment is not effective, then you should heat a cautery to the extent that it fits in the fistula.
Then insert it, hot, into the opening of the fistula, and hold your hand until the heated iron reaches its bottom and end.  Do this once or twice, then treat it afterwards with what we have mentioned of treatment, until it heals, God willing.
If the discharge ceases and it heals, well and good; otherwise, it is necessary to examine the place, and remove the diseased bone according to what will come in its chapter, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
Concerning the cauterization of loose teeth and gums.
If the gums are relaxed from moisture and the teeth move, and you have treated them with medicines without success, then place the patient's head in your lap, and then heat the cautery iron, whose image comes after this, after you have placed the tube on the tooth.
And quickly insert the hot cautery iron into it, and hold your hand for a little while until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth, then lift your hand, then return the cautery iron several times as you wish, then let the patient fill his mouth with salt water.
And let him hold it for an hour and then spit it out; for the loose tooth will become firm, and the relaxed gum will tighten, and the foul moisture will dry up.
Chapter twenty-one.
On cauterizing a toothache.
If the toothache is caused by cold, or if there are worms in it, and treatment with medicines has been ineffective, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with butter, or cauterization with fire.
As for cauterization with butter, you take cow's butter and boil it in an iron spoon or in a shell, then take a piece of cotton, wrap it around the end of a probe, then dip it in the boiled butter and quickly apply it to the aching tooth, and hold it until it cools.
Then repeat this several times until the heat reaches the root of the tooth.  If you wish, you can dip wool or cotton in cold butter and place it on the aching tooth, and place a heated iron on top of it until the heat reaches the bottom of the tooth.
As for cauterization with fire, take a copper or iron tube, somewhat thick in its body so that the heat of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth, then heat the cautery which is shaped as shown and place it on the tooth itself, holding your hand until the cautery cools; do this several times.
Then the pain will go away, either that same day or the next. After the cauterization, the patient should fill his mouth with good butter and hold it for an hour, then spit it out. This is the image of the cautery.
Cauterize with whichever end you wish, and as is possible. This is the image of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-two.
On cauterizing fistulas.
If the fistulas are from phlegm and cold humors and do not respond to ripening medications, and you desire rapid ripening, heat a hollow cautery iron, which is pictured here, perforated at both ends.
So that the smoke exits at the other end during cauterization, place it cautiously on the tumor once and twice if needed, until you reach the depth of the tumor. If the tumor is small, make the cautery iron to the size of the tumor.
Then leave it for three days and apply cotton soaked in butter over it until what was burnt by fire is gone. Then treat it with ointment and tents until it heals, God willing.
Chapter twenty-three.
On cauterization for hoarseness and shortness of breath.
If humors prevail over the windpipe, especially if that is accompanied by coldness of temperament, then the patient should first be purged with laxative medicines, then cauterize with a cautery iron in the hollow of the neck at the root of the throat in the lower place.
Beware of reaching the throat with the cautery and do not burn more than half of the skin, then cauterize another place at the joint of the neck at the end of its last vertebra, profoundly, and let the cautery be nail-shaped in the manner previously described.
Then treat him with what has been mentioned until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter twenty-four.
On cauterizing lung disease and cough.
If the cough and lung disease are due to cold humors, and the patient has no fever or consumption, and the disease is chronic, cauterize two places above the clavicles in the low, soft, empty places, and another place in the middle of the chest between the breasts.
The cautery should be nail-shaped, as previously described. If you wish, let your cauterization be punctate with the cautery called the "nuqta," the image of which has been shown previously in punctate cauterization for earache.  The points should number around thirty.
Then treat him with what has been previously mentioned until he is cured. You may also make a cautery with three branches in this manner, and it hastens the cauterization because you cauterize three places at once. This is the image of the cautery.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On cauterizing the armpit.
If the head of the humerus is dislocated because of slippery humors, or if it is not fixed when it is reduced from its dislocation so that it becomes habitual, then it is dislocated at the slightest movement, as we have seen. Then the joint should be reduced first. Then the patient lies on his back or on his sound side, and then the skin inside the armpit is raised upwards with the fingers of your left hand if the joint is dislocated inwards.
Then heat the cautery iron with two heads, which is of this form.
Then cauterize the skin with it until it penetrates to the other side. The shape of the cauterization is four cauterizations. It may be cauterized with a cautery iron with three heads; then the shape of the cauterization is six cauterizations, and the heads are of the fineness of a rose thorn.
And this is the image of the cautery iron with three heads.
And this number may be increased by one, so that the cauterizations are eight. Then, apply crushed balls with salt to the cauterization. Then the patient must remain still and not move the limb for a time until it strengthens.  And if the dislocation is upwards, which is rarely the case.
Then cauterize above the shoulder, one good nail-shaped cauterization, or many cauterizations in a dotted pattern.  For the joint will become strong, the moisture will depart, and the patient will heal.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
In cauterizing the stomach.
If cold and excessive moisture occur in the stomach, to the point that it is disordered, and frequent discharges occur there, and it has been treated with various treatments without success, then the patient should lie on his back, extend his legs and arms, and then be cauterized with three cauterizations. One cauterization below the xiphoid process, to the extent of a finger's breadth, with a nail-shaped cautery, and two cauterizations below it on either side of the first cauterization, so that the cauterizations form a triangle, and sufficiently separated so that they do not join if they suppurate.  The depth of the cauterization should be about two-thirds the thickness of the skin. This should be the shape of the cauterizations.
In this manner and to this extent, no more.
And if you wish, cauterize it with one large cauterization in the middle of the stomach, in this manner.
And the cautery with which you perform this single cauterization should be in the shape of a circle, in this manner.
And you may cauterize the stomach by punctation for someone who is apprehensive of this cauterization. This is done by marking points on the stomach to the desired extent with ink, then cauterizing them with a pointed cautery, then treating it with the treatment previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the cold cauterization of the liver.
If there appears in the liver pain from coldness and dampness, or from a subtle wind, so that it has departed excessively from its natural temperament, and the patient has been treated with what we mentioned in the division, and that has not availed. Then it is necessary that the patient lie on his back, and three cauterizations should be marked with ink in this shape and this exact amount on the liver, below the diaphragm, where the elbow of a person ends.
And after each cauterization, there should be a cauterization the thickness of a finger, and the cauterization should be straight along the body, and do not press down firmly with the cautery.
And let the amount of thickness of skin that you burn be half of it, not more. And let the patient stand on his feet, and if he is not able to stand, then let him lie down with his legs extended and his arms raised. And this is the image of the cautery.
And you may be able to cauterize these cauterizations with the knife-cautery, if you have gentleness and skill in the craft, and take care lest you go too far in the cauterization and burn all the skin, and burn the abdomen and reach the intestines, for the skin there is thin, so know this.
Chapter twenty-eight.
In cauterizing a swelling of the liver.
If an abscess appears in the liver, and you wish to know if that swelling is in the flesh of the liver or in its peritoneum, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient will feel heaviness and pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the peritoneum of the liver, there will be intense sharpness with the pain, and you see that physicians have been exhausted treating it, then it is necessary that the patient lie on his back, then you should mark the swollen area with ink.
Then heat the cautery in the fire; it is the cautery that resembles a hoe, and this is its image. And cauterize it with one cauterization until you burn all the skin and the cauterization reaches the peritoneum, until all the matter comes out, then treat it with the treatment of abscesses until it heals.
And this type of cauterization should not be used except by one whose training in the art of medicine has been long and who has repeatedly experienced these diseases; then he may undertake such work, but leaving it is better in my opinion.
Chapter twenty-nine.
On cauterizing the fistula.
The ancients mentioned cauterization with the roots of rhubarb for a cold fistula in this way: you take one long, dry root of rhubarb, the longest you can find, about the thickness of a finger, then soak it in oil and heat it in the fire. Then you cauterize with one cauterization between the connection of the clavicle and the neck, and two small cauterizations a little below the nape of the neck, inclined towards the side under the chin, and two large cauterizations above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
And two cauterizations also between the fifth and sixth ribs, slightly inclined backwards, and another cauterization in the middle of the chest and another above the stomach, and three cauterizations on the back: one between the shoulder blades and two on the sides of the spine, below the cauterization which is between the shoulder blades.
You should not deepen your hand with the cautery, but it should be a superficial burning of the skin.  Some of the ancients mentioned that some people used a curved iron cautery, which they heated and inserted between the ribs until it reached the tumor itself.
And the pus comes out as we mentioned in liver tumors.  In this method of cauterization there is a great danger; either the patient dies immediately, or a fistula forms in the place which will not heal.
Chapter Thirty.
On cauterizing the spleen.
If you treat a spleen ailment with the treatments mentioned in the classifications and your treatment is not effective, then cauterization is done in three ways, all correct. One is to cauterize it three or four times in a row along the length of the spleen.
In the shape of the liver cauterizations whose form we have described before, and between each cauterization and the next should be the width of a finger or a little more. The nature of the cautery should be as we described in the cauterization of the liver, and you should not deepen your hand in cauterizing, and the patient's position should be lying on his back.
The other way is in the left flank, and you raise the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left flank ends, and you raise the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left flank ends, and your raising of the skin should be across the body so that the cauterizations fall along the body.
Then you insert two cauteries very carefully until you pierce the skin on the other side, then you remove the cautery, and the cauterizations will be four. And if you wish, you may cauterize with the other cautery with three points.
Then you treat the cauterized area after leaving it to discharge pus for many days, for this is more effective than all the treatments mentioned previously.
Chapter thirty-one.
On cauterization for dropsy.
Cauterization is only beneficial in particular for ascitic dropsy. If you treat the dropsical patient with the various treatments that we mentioned in the classification and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize him with four cauterizations around the navel and one cauterization on the stomach.
Another cauterization on the liver, and a cauterization on the spleen, and two cauterizations on his back between the vertebrae, one opposite his chest and another opposite his stomach. The depth of the cauterization should be approximately the thickness of the skin. Then you leave the cauterization open to discharge pus for a long time.
And do not deprive the patient of treatment after cauterization with what is necessary to combine the two purposes for him, so that healing will hasten to him. The shape of the cautery instrument with which the back is cauterized is nail-shaped, as its image has preceded, and that with which the abdomen is cauterized is olive-shaped.
Chapter thirty-two.
On cauterizing the feet and legs.
If they swell in dropsy and fill with yellow water, then it is necessary to cauterize on the top of the foot in the hollow between the little finger and the ring finger.
And place your hand with the cautery iron and do not bend it, then raise your hand and do not return it at all, for the yellow water will ooze out, and the cautery iron is in this form.
Then cauterize on the legs, two cauterizations on each leg, and the cauterization should be with the sharp end of the cautery iron, and the cauterization should be along the length of the leg, one below the knee and another below that towards the middle of the leg, and on each thigh two cauterizations.
And leave the cauterization open without treatment for a long time, so that the water may ooze out, then treat it with all that we have mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
Concerning the cauterization of diarrhea.
If the diarrhea is from cold and humors, to the point of weakening the retentive and digestive powers in the stomach and intestines, and this has been treated with various treatments and it has not healed, and you see the patient able to withstand cauterization and strong, then cauterize him with a large cauterization on his stomach. As preceded in the cauterization of the stomach with the circular cautery, and four cauterizations around the navel, gently with the mild nail-shaped cautery, and a cauterization on the cotton above the coccyx, large or two cauterizations.
If you see the humors abundant and the patient able to withstand it, then cauterize him with a cauterization on the pubis and a cauterization on each loin, and perhaps add two small cauterizations on the stomach near the large one, for it is a successful treatment whose benefit does not err, God willing.
Chapter thirty-four.
On cauterizing the hemorrhoids of the anus.
If there are in the anus chronic hemorrhoids, many or one, and they are from thick, cold humors or putrid humors, and they were treated with what we mentioned in the division, and the treatment did not work, then cauterize the patient with three cauterizations on the lower part of the sacrum, a little below the table, triangular.
And a cauterization below the navel by the width of two fingers. And if you estimate that his stomach has become cold and his food does not digest, and you see his face swollen, then cauterize him on the stomach with a large cauterization as mentioned before, and a cauterization on his liver. And another on his spleen with a nail-like cautery, and leave the cautery open for a while, then treat him until he heals, God willing.
Chapter thirty-five.
In cauterizing warts.
After cutting it, if the wart is cut, heat the cautery which resembles a nail, then insert it hot into the same cut wart, and persevere with your hand until the cautery reaches the mouth of the vein from which the blood flows; do this once or twice.
If the warts are numerous, cauterize each one as we have described, then treat them with ointments suitable for them until they heal. And if you cauterize it with a large cautery on cotton, it will be more effective.
Chapter thirty-six.
In cauterizing the fistula which is in the anus and its surroundings.
If the patient does not consent to the incision and the procedure which we described in its place and shrinks from that, then perhaps he will be healed by cauterization. If a fistula occurs and pus and corrupt humors flow from it for a long time, then the first thing you should do is measure it with a thin probe.
Then know the extent of its depth with the probe, then heat the cautery which resembles a needle, then insert it hot into the fistula itself, along the course of the fistula and the extent to which the probe entered, and repeat the cauterization until all those corrupt substances are burned, once or twice or three times, according to your need.
And be careful not to burn a nerve if there is one, or a large vein, and if the fistula leads to the body of the bladder or to the body of the intestine.
Guard against all these places.  You do this only if the fistula is in a fleshy location and you estimate that it is not penetrating. Then treat the area until it heals. If the area is sealed and the flow ceases and remains so for a time, then know that it has completely healed.
And if the flow does not cease, then know that it is penetrating, or that in its depth there is a decayed bone or something similar, which will be mentioned in its place.
Chapter thirty-seven.
On cauterizing the kidneys.
If pain occurs in the kidneys from cold or a strong wind, and the patient's sexual function is thereby diminished, then it should be cauterized on the loins, over the kidneys themselves.  Cauterize each kidney with the nail-shaped cautery previously mentioned.  And perhaps cauterize it a third time in the same place, resulting in three successive cauterizations.  This will be more effective.
Chapter thirty-eight.
On cauterizing the bladder.
If weakness and relaxation occur in the bladder due to cold and dampness, so that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize it with one cauterization below the navel, over the bladder where the pubic hair begins, and one cauterization to the right of the navel and another to the left.
The cauterization on each side should be the size of a thumb joint.  And cauterize one or two times in the lower back, if necessary.  The cautery should be nail-shaped, as previously mentioned.
Chapter thirty-nine.
On cauterizing the uterus.
If a substance forms in the uterus due to cold and dampness, preventing conception and corrupting its humors, making menstruation difficult or causing pain during its arrival, then three cauterizations should be made around the navel, as mentioned in the cauterization of the bladder. One or two cauterizations on the cotton in the lower back; the cautery should be nail-shaped.
Chapter forty.
On cauterizing a dislocated hip.
Mucous humors may settle in the hip joint, causing it to dislocate.  The sign is that one leg will appear longer than the other when compared, and an empty space will be found at the site of the dislocation.  Therefore, a semi-circular cauterization should be applied to the affected area of the hip joint itself.
After outlining the joint with ink in a circle so that the joint itself falls in the center of the circle, use the cautery iron whose image is shown in the cauterization of the stomach. If you do not have this cautery iron, perform three cauterizations with a large olive-shaped cautery iron.
Make the cauterization deep enough to reach the entire thickness of the skin, then treat him until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter forty-one.
On cauterizing sciatica.
If pain occurs in the hip joint, and the cause is cold and dampness, and you have treated the patient with what we mentioned in the division, and the treatment has not been effective, and this has lasted for a long time, then it is necessary to evacuate the thick humors from the patient with the foul-smelling bean or the prickly pear bean, or the like.
Then shave it and apply cautery in two ways: either cautery with burning medicines or cautery with fire. Cautery with fire can be done in many ways, one of which is to cauterize the hip joint itself with three triangular cauterizations in this manner.
And make them of a suitable depth. The distance between each cauterization should be the width of a finger. The cautery iron should be olive-shaped.  You may also cauterize one in the middle on the head of the hip itself, making four cauterizations.  If you wish, cauterize it with the circle previously mentioned in cauterizing a dislocated hip, a single cauterization.
So that the circle encompasses the entire hip and burns through the entire thickness of the skin. This is the method of cauterization for sciatica if the pain is confined to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
If it extends to the thigh and leg, cauterize two areas on the thigh where the patient indicates pain, and one above the heel, four finger-widths towards the outer side slightly. The cautery iron should be knife-shaped, and the depth of the cauterization should be only the thickness of the skin.
If he indicates that the pain extends towards the toes, cauterize where he indicates with a pointed cautery iron, three or four times, or more if necessary. If he indicates pain below the knee towards the leg, cauterize there with one knife-shaped cauterization.
And be careful in all your cauterizations not to burn a nerve or a large artery, lest you cause the patient a serious or long-lasting injury.
I have witnessed one and another who were cauterized above the heel, and the cauterization was excessive; the leg became gangrenous, the gangrene reached the foot, the whole foot was perforated and decayed, then diarrhea and death followed. If the pain is in both sides, cauterize both in the same manner.
Some learned physicians have mentioned a cauterization of the hip of this kind:  A cup-shaped iron instrument is made, half a span in diameter, and its mouth is the thickness of a date pit or slightly less, and inside this cup is another cup, and a third.
The distance between each cup is the width of a thumb joint; the cups are open on both sides; their height is about one or two finger joints; and a handle of well-secured iron is attached to them.  This is its image.
Then it is heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks, then it is placed on the hip, the patient leaning on the sound side, and three round cauterizations are performed at once; then it is left for three days, and dressed with butter, and the wound is left open for days.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  The author of this book says: We have rarely used this type of cauterization because of its ugliness and the horror of its appearance, and because few are found who can endure it; however, it is a good cauterization for those who endure it and in whom it hits its mark.
As for cauterization with burning drugs, it is done by making two cup-like rings, similar to those through which the pulp of the pomegranate passes, of copper or iron.
Their rims are about two finger joints high, and the distance between them is about the thickness of a finger. They are open at the bottom and open at the top, and they are held together in this manner.
Then lower it onto the hip joint, with the patient lying on his sound side, and firmly hold your hand, then pour between the two circles some strong water, which has been slightly warmed by the fire, and hold it for the time of a sandglass. The patient must endure its stinging and burning, for he will feel a burning like fire.
Until the stinging subsides, then raise your hand with the instrument and wipe the strong water from the entire hip with fresh water. Then leave it for three days, applying butter to it until the blackness of the burn disappears. Leave it for a few days, so that pus may drain from it, for this is more effective.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals. If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make a mold for it that holds the water, in the same way that you did for the hip. This is the description of the strong water, and I have described it in the chapter on the preparation of medicines.
Take one part of alkali salt and one part of unslaked lime, crush them, and put them in a new pot which has a small hole in its bottom, just big enough for a probe to enter.  Place another glazed pot underneath the bottom of the pot.
Pour fresh water over the alkali and lime to cover them by a finger's breadth, after you have pressed them well with your hand. Leave the pot until the strong water drips into the glazed pot below, then collect all that water and pour it onto more fresh lime and alkali.
Then it will be very strong and sharp, useful in many medical procedures and in cauterizing various members, for it acts exactly like fire.  Among the substances that cauterize the hip are also thapsia, linseed oil, and lime with soap, well mixed.
Galen recounts a story from the ancients about a treatment for hip pain and sciatica, greatly extolling its virtues and claiming that no other treatment is needed and that it cures with a single application.
To the extent that one might enter the bath carried and leave cured.  This is done by taking green verdigrisif green is unavailable, take fresh dry verdigrisand finely grinding it with some fat, then applying it to the hip where the pain is, or to the leg or thigh.
Then bind it on and leave it for three hours, or however long the patient feels the burning subside. Then have him enter the bath; when his body is moist, have him enter the pool. The pain will then cease, and he will be cured, God willing. If not cured, reapply the poultice after ten days.
Then he will be cured, God willing. Know that one should not cauterize with these medications except after purging the body. Dioscorides mentioned that goat hair, when used to cauterize sciatica, is beneficial.
Cauterization is performed in this manner: take wool, soak it in old oil, and place it on the deep place between the thumb of the hand and the forearm, closer to the forearm. Then take dry goat hair.
Heat it in the fire until it becomes a coal, then place it on the wool and leave it until it goes out. Then take another and continue to do this until sensation reaches, via the humerus, to the hip, and the pain subsides, God willing. This type of cauterization is called cauterization with goat hair.
Chapter forty-two.
On cauterizing back pain.
Pain in the back may occur from many causes, either from a fall, a blow, excessive vomiting, and the like. It is caused by the effusion of a cold, moist substance, and cauterization is only applied in this type.
That which is caused by the effusion of a cold substance. After the patient has vomited with hellebore and the like, three rows should be cauterized on his back where the pain is, across the breadth of the table itself, after marking the spot with ink.  In each row, five cauterizations or more, according to the patient's tolerance and strength.
The cauterization should be done with a needle cautery; or if you wish, cauterize three or four times with a medium-sized nail cautery, in this manner.
Chapter seventy-three.
On cauterizing the beginning of a hump.
This ailment frequently affects young children.  Its initial sign in a child is shortness of breath upon standing and movement, and you will find at the end of his spinal vertebrae a bead that has protruded and stands out from the other beads.
If you see this and wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery iron in this manner. Apply the cautery evenly to each side of the vertebra; and if you wish, cauterize around the vertebra with a pointed cautery iron in two or three rows, letting the points be close to one another.  Then treat the area until it heals as we have mentioned.
And be careful not to use cautery in a hump that is due to a nerve spasm.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On cauterizing gout and joint pains.
If joint pains are due to cold humors that settle in any member of the body, and if the pain occurs in the legs, physicians usually call this gout in particular.
If the gout, which is cold in origin, is treated with the types of treatment that we mentioned in the classification, and the pain does not go away, then cautery will remove it.  This is done by cauterizing around the joints of the legs with many cauterizations after vomiting. The cautery iron should be olive-shaped and medium-sized, in this manner.
And if you need to cauterize the surface of the leg, do so with a pointed cautery iron. If the pain ascends to the knees or to other joints, and this often happens, cauterize each knee three or four times on each side with this same olive-shaped cautery iron.
If you need more cautery than this, cauterize, but do not deepen your cauterization, but rather let it be to the thickness of the skin only. If the pain ascends to the hips or to the back, use what we mentioned regarding cautery in its chapter.
If the pain is only in the hands, cauterize around the forearms as if making two rows. If the pain remains in the fingers, cauterize each knuckle and the metacarpus. If the pain ascends after days to the elbows or shoulders,
cauterize them on each side. The patient should not be without good management and taking medicines, for if he improves his diet and expels phlegm, he will be cured with this cautery, God willing.
Chapter Forty-Five.
On the cauterization of hernias.
If a hernia appears in the groin and some intestine and fat descends to the testicle, and this is recent and beginning, then you should order the patient to abstain from food that day and use laxatives to move the stool from his bowels.
Then he should lie on his back in front of you, and you should tell him to hold himself so that the fat or intestine protrudes, then you should return it with your finger. Then mark with ink below the hernia on the pubic bone a mark resembling a semicircle, its extremities towards the upper part of the body.
Then heat a cautery of this shape.
Until it becomes white and throws off sparks. Then return the intestine or fat to the abdomen. Then a servant should place his hand on the place lest the intestine protrude.  And you have spread the patient's legs apart and placed a cushion under him, and another servant ties his legs.
And another on his chest holds his hands. Then lower the cautery onto the same mark, and your hand with the cautery should be upright and straight, and hold it until it reaches the bone, or repeat it again if it did not reach the bone the first time.
And take care to prevent the protrusion of the intestine while cauterizing, lest you burn it, which would cause the patient either death or a great calamity. And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your work will not succeed. And the cautery should be gentle for children according to their size, and for adults according to their size.
Then treat the cauterized area after three days with fat until the scab from the fire disappears. Then treat it with other ointments until it heals. And let the patient lie on his back for forty days until the wound is closed.
And you should make his diet during his treatment such that it softens his bowels, lest the intestine protrude during defecation. Then, if he wants to get up after forty days, use a tight bandage and keep it on for another forty days.  And he should reduce exertion, overfilling himself with food and drink, and loud crying.
For if this treatment is used in this way, he will recover completely, God willing. And I will mention the treatment of hernias by incision in its chapter, God willing. As for a hernia that occurs in the rest of the abdomen and is recent, if you wish to prevent it from increasing, cauterize a round mark around it. Let what you burn of the skin be about two-thirds of it, then treat it as we have mentioned, for it will not increase, God willing. And some of the ancients mentioned cauterizing the hernia with a triangular cautery in this manner.
After you mark the hernia with ink, making the horizontal line at the top of the hernia and the other line downwards, and you apply a single nail-like cautery in the middle. The first cautery is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter forty-six.
On cauterizing fistulas.
If pain occurs in some member from a fall or a blow, and that pain persists for a long time and medication does not relieve it, then it should be cauterized with a pointed cautery.  The cauterization of each member should be according to its size, whether large or small, and its weakness or strength.
And if the pain and fistula persist, then if it heals from the first cautery, well and good; otherwise repeat the cautery, because it is the nature of these pains to move from one member to what is near it; therefore it is necessary to follow them with cautery until the patient is cured.
Chapter forty-seven.
On cauterizing leprosy.
As for lepers, they may benefit greatly from cautery, especially the type of leprosy which comes from the corruption of phlegm and black bile.
If you wish to cauterize, then look: if the leprosy is beginning, and you have treated it with what we mentioned in the classification, and it has not subsided, nor stopped, and you fear that corruption will take over the whole temperament of the patient, then cauterize him on the head with five cauterizations. One in the middle of the head, as is usual; the second below it towards the forehead at the end of the hair; two on the temples; and one at the back on the nape of the neck.  And press your hand with the cautery a little, until it affects the bone slightly.
And scales will come off from it, so that the thick vapors may more easily escape from it.  And cauterize it also on the same spleen as before. But if the leprosy has continued on the patient and appeared clearly, then it is necessary to cauterize it with these cauterizations which we have mentioned on the head. And a cauterization on the tip of the nose; two cauterizations on the cheeks; two cauterizations on the vertebrae of the neck; six on the vertebrae of the back; one large one on the coccyx at the base of the spine; another above it in the same place; and two on the hips, one on each hip. And two on the knees, one on each knee; and two on the shoulders; and two on the elbows; and two on the breasts; and cauterize on each joint of the joints of his fingers and toes with one cauterization; and on each heel of his feet and forearms.
And be careful of the nerve which is on the back of the heels, lest you burn them. And it may be cauterized with one cauterization on the pubic bone, and another on the stomach, and another on the liver. And know that the more cauterizations you do, the more useful and effective it will be.
And know that the patient does not feel the pain of the cautery as much as the healthy person, because his body is numb.  And the cauteries should be of varying sizes, according to the limbs and joints, as mentioned before concerning the descriptions of the cauteries.
Then treat the cauterization with finely ground  cress mixed with honey and other treatments until it heals, God willing.
Chapter forty-eight.
Concerning the cauterization of numbness: when a limb becomes numb and is treated with medication, ointments, and bandages, but does not heal, then cauterize the numb limb according to the size of the limb, whether large or small. Let your cauterization be deep, penetrating the thickness of the skin slightly.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  And some numbness that occurs in the hand and foot, in the vertebrae of the back at the exit of the nerve that moves that limb, may be cauterized, and the numbness will go away.
And let none undertake this except one who is knowledgeable in the anatomy of the limbs and the exits of the nerves that move the body.
Chapter forty-nine.
Concerning the cauterization of leprosy.
If leprosy is longstanding and no medical remedy is effective, cauterize it slightly, to the depth of the thickness of the skin, until the whiteness disappears and its color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour, rose oil,  plantain leaves, and pigeon or dove blood; one part of each. Mix everything together, apply it to a cloth, and bind it to the place until it heals.
Chapter fifty.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
If the cancer is incipient and you wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, as it grows. Some scholars have mentioned that a thorough cauterization should be done in its middle, but I do not consider this, because I expect that it will ulcerate, and I have witnessed this many times.
Therefore, it is correct to cauterize around it in a circle, as we said, or with many cauterizations.
Chapter fifty-one.
Concerning the cauterization of hemorrhoids.
If a hemorrhoid occurs in someone, and it is slow to mature, either because of the potency of its cause, or because of the age of the patient if he is old and anemic, or because of time, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the hemorrhoid, cauterize around it with many small cauterizations, punctually.
Then leave it, for it will hasten maturation. If you wish to slow it with cauterization, then take the cautery whose image is this.
And place it in the middle of the hemorrhoid until it penetrates the skin, and let the cauterization be from what is below, so that the flow of pus is easy, then treat it as is appropriate until it heals.
Chapter fifty-two.
On cauterizing the eating sore.
The eating sore is a corruption that spreads in the member and eats it, just as fire eats dry wood. If you see the eating sore in a place that can tolerate cauterization with fire, then take many small and large, nail-shaped cauteries according to what suits that place where the eating sore is.
Then cauterize it from every side until all the corruption is eradicated and nothing of it remains at all, then leave it for three days and apply to the cauterized areas ground sulfur with oil until all the eschar and all the corruption are removed.
Then treat it with ointments that grow flesh. If you see after three days that the flesh grows healthy growth with no corruption in it, otherwise repeat the cauterization on what remains of the corrupted areas.  And the eating sore may be treated with a sharp medicine.
For it acts in place of fire, more quickly beneficial. And I have mentioned its treatment with the sharp medicine in the classification, so take it from there when you need it.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On the cauterization of inverted and non-inverted nails.
This condition often occurs in the soles of the feet; it is a rough thing that pains the man, and its cauterization is in two ways: either cauterization with fire or cauterization with a sharp liquid.
As for cauterization with fire, it is that you heat a hollow cautery similar to an eagle's feather, made of iron to the extent that it surrounds the nail from every side, and it should be thin-edged; then you lower it, hot, onto the nail, then you turn your hand with the cautery around the nail until the cautery reaches the depth of the nail.
And you leave it three days, it will suppurate with pus, then you dress it with crushed wild bread mixed with salt, and you leave the dressing on it overnight; then it will be extracted from its roots. Then you treat the wound site with a flesh-growing ointment until it heals, if the nails are not inverted.
And it often occurs on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet.  Then you should take a tube of copper or iron or eagle's feather and lower it onto the nail or wart, then you pour a small amount of sharp liquid into the tube.
And you hold your hand while you turn it, with a slight twitching of your hand, so that the edge of the tube affects the root of the nail, and the sharp liquid finds the way to penetrate to the root of the nail. And the patient endures a little the stinging of the sharp liquid for a while.
Then you leave it, and the nail will be extracted by its roots.  You do this one by one until you have treated all of them in the body; then you treat their locations after they are extracted with ointments that grow flesh.
Chapter fifty-four.
On cauterizing the falling sickness.
If someone suffering from the falling sickness is afflicted by cold in the nerves, or by a quartan fever or something else, then it is necessary to cauterize him with four or five cauterizations on the vertebrae of the back, between each vertebra a cauterization, and a cauterization on his chest. And on his stomach a cauterization with the olive cautery, for the falling sickness is calmed and the cold illness is hastened to ripen.
Chapter fifty-five.
On cauterizing pustules occurring in the body.
Ugly pustules may erupt in the body, being caused by cold, thick, and corrupt materials.  At their first appearance, it is necessary to cauterize each pustule delicately with a cautery-iron whose end has been heated in the fire, or with the long root of the ziraund, or with a lenticular cautery.
And boils may be cauterized at their first eruption in this manner, so that the active cause does not increase, and the ailment disappears, except that this should be done after the patient has been bled.
Chapter fifty-six.
On cauterizing hemorrhage occurring from a severed artery.
Often, bleeding occurs from a severed artery during a wound inflicted from the outside, or when an abscess is incised, or a member is cauterized, and so forth, making it difficult to stop.
If this happens to someone, quickly place your index finger on the mouth of the artery and press it firmly until the blood is contained under your finger and nothing comes out.  Then place several small and large olive-shaped cauteries in the fire and heat them until they are very hot.
Then take one, either small or large, depending on the wound and the location where the artery is severed.  Place the cautery on the same vessel after quickly removing your finger, and hold the cautery until the bleeding stops.
If, upon removing your finger from the mouth of the artery, the bleeding resumes and the cautery is extinguished, quickly take another cautery from those prepared in the fire, and continue to do this, one after another, until the bleeding stops.
Be careful not to burn a nerve that is there, lest you cause the patient another affliction.  Know that if an artery bleeds, it cannot be stopped, especially if it is a large artery, except in one of four ways: either by cauterization, as we have said; or by amputation, if it has not already been severed; for if it is severed, its ends contract and the bleeding stops; or by tightly tying it with threads; or by applying medications that stop the bleeding, and tightly binding it with bandages.
And as for anyone who attempts to stop it with a ligature, or by tightening with cloths, or applying cauterizing agents, and so forth, it will not be stopped at all except rarely.  If this happens to someone and a physician or medication is not available, he should immediately place his index finger on the mouth of the wound itself, as we described.
He should press it very firmly until the blood is contained and flows from above the wound and onto the artery; and the finger should not be removed, always applying very cold water to it until the blood coagulates, thickens, and stops. During this time, consider what further cauterization or medication is needed.
Chapter Two.
Concerning Incisions, Punctures, Bloodletting, Wounds, and the Like.
Khalaf said: In the first chapter we mentioned every illness for which cauterization with fire and burning medication is suitable, its causes, its reasons, its instruments, and the forms of cauteries; and I made that into sections from the head to the foot.
And I follow in this chapter the same method, to make it easier for the student to obtain what he seeks.
Before I begin that, you should know, my sons, that this chapter contains more subtleties than the first chapter on cauterization.
And because of that, caution should be greater in it, for the work in this chapter often involves the evacuation of blood upon which life depends, when opening a vein or making an incision on a tumor, or puncturing an abscess, or treating a wound, or removing an arrow, or incising a stone, and the like; all of these are accompanied by danger and fear, and death frequently results.
And I advise you against undertaking what is doubtful to you, for in this craft various kinds of people will come to you with various kinds of illnesses; and among them will be some who are weary of their illness and to whom death is insignificant.
Because of the severity of his illness and the length of his affliction, and because the illness shows signs of death, and because some will give you their money and enrich you with it in hope of health, and their illness is fatal, you should absolutely not assist anyone who comes to you with these characteristics.
Let your caution be greater than your desire and eagerness, and do not undertake anything of this sort unless after certain knowledge that assures you of a favorable outcome. And in all the treatment of your patients, use foresight and warning of what leads to safety.
For in that you will have help in acquiring praise, glory, mention, and thanks. May God inspire you, my sons, with His guidance, and may He not deprive you of correctness and success, for that is in His hand; there is no god but He.
And I have arranged the chapters of this section in the same order as the previous section on cautery, from head to foot, so that what you seek from it may be easier for you, God willing.
Chapter One.
On the treatment of the water which collects in the heads of infants.
