This ailment frequently occurs to infants at birth if the midwife presses the infant's head without gentleness, and it may also occur from a hidden cause which is not known.
And I have not seen this ailment in anyone other than infants, and all those whom I have seen afflicted with it have died quickly; therefore I considered abandoning treatment of it.  And I saw an infant whose head was filled with water and whose head grew larger every day. Until the infant could not sit up by himself because of the size of his head, and the moisture increased until he perished.
And this moisture either collects between the skin and the bone, or it collects under the bone on the dura mater.  The procedure in this case: if the moisture is between the skin and bone, and the swelling is small, then it is necessary to make one transverse incision in the middle of the head, and the length of the incision should be about two fingerbreadths, so that the moisture flows out.  This is the image of the scalpel.
If the moisture is greater and the swelling is larger, then make two intersecting incisions in this manner:
And if the moisture is under the boneand its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on every side, and the water recedes if you press it inward with your hand, and this is not hidden from youthen it is necessary to make three incisions in the middle of the head in this manner.
After the incision, all the moisture comes out, then you tighten the incisions with rags and bandages. Then you bathe it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day, then you untie the bandage and treat the wound with lint and ointments. Do not neglect to bind the head moderately, and feed the patient with all dry food, little moisture.
Until the member strengthens and heals. Another description of the incision is that you look where the bone of the swelling and the collection of water appear, for it may be in the back of the head more, or in its front, or on the right, or on the left.
Then you aim the incision where the swelling and the fullness of water appear to you, and you incise it as much as you can, and be careful not to cut an artery, lest bleeding occur and the patient die from that bleeding, along with the evacuation of the moisture.
Chapter Two.
Cutting the two arteries behind the ears, known as the  hassisayn.
When someone experiences severe colds in the eyes or chest, and this persists and medication does not work, then the best treatment is to cut these two arteries.
So if you want to cut them, you should shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth to reveal the artery, then tighten the patient's neck with the excess of his cloth, then look where the pulse beats and their locations, the two low-lying places behind the ears, and they rarely hide except in some people.
Then mark them with ink, then cut them with a sharp scalpel, cutting to the bone. This should be done across the head.  If you wish, insert the scalpel under the artery and cut upwards; the length of the cut should be about two fingers breadth.
If the artery is severed, the blood will come out in a pulsating flow, leaping forward repeatedly. If the artery is not palpable, you should measure three fingers breadth from the ear, then mark it with ink and incise to the bone.  What should be...
...sent from the blood six ounces on average, and perhaps more or less, all this depending on the strength of the patient and the fullness of his arteries. Then look at the wound; if there remains anything of the peritoneum on the bone, cut it away.
Lest a hot swelling appear. Then bind the wound with a linen thread, and treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
In the ligation of the two arteries in the temples.
If a person suffers from chronic migraine or acute attacks caused by sharp humors and heat in the muscles of the temples, or severe chronic headache and the like, and he has been treated with various medical treatments without success, then we have tried in these diseases the ligation of the arteries of the temples, or their cauterization as we have described.
And the method of ligating them is that the patient's hair in the temples should be shaved, and the artery apparent in the temple should be sought, for it is apparent to you from its pulsation, and it is rarely hidden except in a few people.
Or, in case of intense cold, if it is hidden from you, let the patient tighten his neck with the excess of his garment, then you rub the area with a cloth or compress the area with hot water until the artery appears clearly and obviously. Then take the scalpel, whose image is this. Then you incise the skin gently with it until you reach the artery, then you place a hook in it and pull it upwards until you bring it out of the skin and free it from the membranes underneath it on every side; and if the artery is thin, then twist it with the end of the hook. Then cut off a portion of it to the extent that its ends separate and contract, and do not cause bleeding, for if it is not severed and cut, the blood will not clot at all; then the blood is evacuated from six ounces to three.
If the artery is large, then it is necessary to tie it in two places with strong doubled thread; and let the thread be either of silk or of lute strings, lest corruption quickly reach it before the wound heals and bleeding occurs.
Then cut the portion between the ligatures; do this at that time or after another time; and if you wish to cauterize it with a knife-like cautery to the bone until its ends are severed, this will suffice for this same work or be better, as we have said.
Unless the patient has a fever or a hot temperament, because cauterization is something that helps in destroying humors and will thus be more beneficial. After tying off the arteries, the area should be packed with old cotton and firm bandages applied. After the healing, it is treated with drying medicines that promote flesh growth and with bandaging until it heals, God willing.
If bleeding from the artery occurs during your work, immediately cut it, either by cauterization or by filling the area with vitriol and compressing with your hand until the bleeding stops. If you do not have any of that, place your finger there until the blood clots.
And wash the area with very cold water until the sharpness subsides, and bind it as necessary.  Easier and simpler than ligating the artery is to cauterize the vein with this double-bladed cautery after marking the two locations with ink.
Apply it very hot until it reaches the bone and the vein is severed in two places so that its ends are separated, for it will not reunite with this cauterization at all. This is the image of the cautery.
The blades should be sharp, resembling small lancets, but much less sharp than a scalpel; for if they are as sharp as a scalpel, they cool more quickly and the flesh is not severed rapidly. If they have some thickness, the heat of the fire will remain in them.
And the flesh is severed quickly. This method is better than any other, and is lighter and easier. Let the distance between the blades be the width of a finger.
Chapter Four.
In the treatment of a constant flow of hot tears into the eyes.
If the tears are constant and their flow is from the veins on the outside of the skull, and nothing from medical treatment with drugs has been beneficial, and you see the patient's face is red. And he feels a crawling sensation on his forehead like the crawling of ants, and his eyes are sunken and moist, their rims eroded and his eyelids clinging together from the severity of the tears.  Therefore, those who first treated this condition resorted to this procedure, which they call treatment with the sword. It is to instruct the patient to shave the hair from his forehead.
Then make three parallel incisions in the forehead along its length.  The length of each incision should be about two fingers' breadth. One incision is parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second a little distance from the movement of the muscle in the temple, and the third on the other side.
And be careful not to cut the arteries on either side, and keep your hand away from the joining of the jaws. Let the distance between each incision be the width of three closed fingers.  Have ready pieces of prepared sponge or many dry cloths to wipe away the blood.
Then insert the double-edged scalpel, whose image is this. From the incision near the temple to the middle incision, and with it flay away all the skin between the incisions, together with the membrane on the bone. Then do the same from the middle incision to the third incision. Then remove this scalpel. Then insert into the first incision another instrument called a scalpel, sharp on one side and smooth, not sharp, on the other; and this is its image:.
Place its sharp side upwards, towards the flesh attached to the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone; and push it until it reaches the middle incision, cutting through all the vessels which descend from the head to the eyes without the cut reaching the surface of the skin.
Then do the same in the middle incision to the other incision. After a moderate amount of blood has flowed, squeeze the places of the clotted blood. Then place in each incision a pledget of cotton, and place over it a compress moistened with a drink and oil, or vinegar and oil.
Lest a hot inflammation occur. On the third day, remove the bandage and use plenty of irrigation with lukewarm water. Then treat it with basilicon ointment with rose oil and whatever else you treat wounds with, until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Five.
On the treatment of tears and discharges to the eyes from inside the head.
When someone experiences frequent, sharp, severe, persistent discharges, and you see their eyes weak, small, their vision weakened, and their eyelids ulcerated, with the eyelashes falling out. And there is severe, painful pain deep in the head and continuous sneezing. Know from these symptoms that those materials and discharges come from many deep vessels. The best treatment is this: order the patient to have his forehead shaved, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there is severe, painful pain deep in the head and continuous sneezing. Know from these symptoms that those materials and discharges come from many deep vessels. The best treatment is this: order the patient to have his forehead shaved, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead. Or a little higher transversely, beginning the incision from the left temple to the right temple.  The incision should be to the bone, and keep your hand away from the temporal muscles that move when chewing. Then, when the bone is exposed and all the blood is dried with a sponge,
separate the edges of the incision with old cotton or linen threads. Then bind it from above with pledgets, and moisten the pledgets with a potion and oil, lest a hot swelling occur. When you remove them and see that the hot swelling has subsided, you should scrape the bone until the growth of flesh begins.
Then treat it with a drying preparation that makes flesh grow, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony, preparing an ointment from them, and using it for flesh growth in such wounds.
Chapter Six.
In the treatment of what falls into the ear.
Whatever falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic stone, or something like stone such as iron and glass; or a plant seed such as chickpeas and kernels and the like; or a flowing thing such as water, vinegar, and the like.
Or an animal. When a stone or a kind of stone falls into the ear, which does not grow in the ear, then expose the ear to the sun.  If you see the stone, then instill some violet oil or cerate into it, then try to remove it by moving the head or sneezing with a sternutatory. And close the nostrils when sneezing comes, after you have placed a ring of rags or wool around the ear and extend the ear upwards. Often it comes out with this treatment. If it does not come out otherwise, then try to remove it with a gentle probe, whose image is this.
If it comes out with the probe, otherwise try to remove it with a gentle, slightly curved, blind probe. If it does not come out with that, then make a tube of copper and insert the end of the tube into the hole of the ear gently.
Seal what is around the tube with pitch softened with oil, lest there be a way for the wind other than the tube. Then draw it with your breath strongly, and often it comes out with that. If it does not come out with what we have described, then take a little bit of gum arabic or of prepared gum with which birds are caught, and place it on the end of the probe.
After you have wrapped cotton tightly around it, then insert it into the hole of the ear gently after you have dried the ear of moisture. If it does not come out by all that we have described, then hasten to incision before hot swelling or spasm occurs.
The description of the incision is that you first bleed the patient in the cephalic vein and take out blood from him according to his strength. Then seat the patient before you, and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the base of the ear near its lobe, in the lower part of it.
And the incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone, then remove it with whatever instruments you can. Then suture the incision immediately and treat it until it heals.
And if the thing that has fallen into the ear is one of the seeds that grow and swell, then try to remove it with what we have mentioned.  If it does not respond to removal, then take a thin, delicate scalpel of this shape.
And try with it to cut that type of seed that has fallen into the ear. But you do this only if you are certain that that seed has become moistened by the vapor of the ear until it becomes many small pieces, then remove them with the blind probe or with a soft sponge or by suction.
As we mentioned, then it is easy to remove it. As for water entering the ear, the patient should first use sneezing with a snuff, having filled his ear with some old cotton while lying on the ear in which the water is. If it comes out with that,
otherwise, take many small, smooth, flat stones the length of the fingers, warm them slightly by the fire, and let the patient insert one into the hole of his ear. He should then hop on one foot on that side and strike the stone in the ear with another stone.
He should continue doing this, stone by stone, until all the water comes out.  The water may also be removed by taking a piece of papyrus or a feather, inserting one end into the ear, and applying fire to the other end until most of it burns, then repeating with another feather.
Do this repeatedly until all the water comes out or draw it out with the tube as previously mentioned for the stone. As for removing an animal that has entered, see if it is small, like a flea, etc., treat it as mentioned in the classification, but...
If its body is large and perceptible to the touch, try removing it with lint and hooks; this is easier than anything else that gets into the ear. As for removing worms that develop in the ear, if you treat it with what we mentioned in the classification in the chapter on instillations and your treatment is not effective,
you should look into the ear in the sun. If you see anything of the worms, remove them with lint or fine hooks. If you do not see anything, take a tube of this shape.
narrow at the bottom and wide at the top, and insert the thin end into the ear as far as the patient can tolerate. Then suck strongly with it; do this repeatedly until all the worms come out. If they do not come out, seal around the tube with wax, as I mentioned to you for the stone.
If they do not come out with what we mentioned, use the instillations that I have mentioned, which the ancients have tried in killing worms; you will find this in the chapter on instillations.  Pouring the ointments and medicines into the ear is done with this instrument, and this is its shape:
As you see, make it of silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, and wide at the top. If you wish, the nozzle inside the tube can be of tightly fitting copper; if you wish, take a reed and wrap cotton tightly around its end.
Then pour the ointment or juice or whatever medicine you want into the tube while it is in the ear, then insert the reed with the cotton from above and squeeze it moderately with your hand until the ointment is propelled into the interior of the ear and is felt.
The patient feels it going in.  Whatever you pour into the ear should be warmed a little in the fire.  Be careful that what is instilled is neither very cold nor very hot, for the ear cannot tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
On the treatment of accidental blockage of the ear.
Some children may be born with their ear canals not perforated.
It may also happen that some people experience a blockage in the ear canal due to a wound or growing flesh; and this blockage may be deep within the ear canal, invisible to the eye.
It may also appear slightly outside the opening and be visible to the eye. That which appears deep within the ear and is not visible to the eye is, in most cases, difficult to cure; but that which is visible to the eye should have the patient's ear exposed to the sun. And examine it; if you see the blockage outwardly, open it with a delicate scalpel, which is of this shape:
Its tip should be slightly broad, its edge limited, and the rest of the scalpel smooth on both sides, lest it injure the ear. If the blockage is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a delicate hook and cut it gently and carefully until all the flesh is removed.
If the blockage is deep within the ear, take a smooth, delicate probe, warm it slightly in the fire, and insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the blockage, and the patient feels a heaviness in hearing, remove it gently.
Take care not to injure the membrane of the ear. Then insert into the ear a wick to fit the size of the opening, having coated it with Egyptian ointment. Do this for several days until you are sure the wound will not close up again, or take a wick, moisten it in water, and sprinkle powdered vitriol on it.
Use this. If you see that the ear has swollen with a hot inflammation, you should remove the wick and replace it with another wick coated with an ointment made with rose oil until the hot swelling subsides; then treat it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear. Use the other treatments for stopping bleeding mentioned in the chapter on haemostatics.
Chapter Eight.
On the treatment of warts which appear on the eyelids.
These warts which appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry.  You should grasp them with forceps or a hook and cut them from their roots with a scalpel; then apply powdered verdigris to the area.
If it bleeds, cauterize it with a gentle lentil-shaped cautery.  Cauterization is best after cutting.  They often return if anything of their roots remains; but if cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and it does not return.
Chapter Nine.
On the treatment of the cold affecting the eyelids.
Something resembling a cold in its intensity and hardness often occurs in the eyelids.
Therefore it is called a cold, which is a gathering of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids.  The treatment is to look: if the cold swelling is prominent on the surface of the eyelid and moves to every direction, then the matter is easy; make a transverse incision upon it.
Then gently peel it from every side until it is released, then suspend it with a hook and cut it. If you are not able to cut it except after it has penetrated the eyelid by the cut, this will not harm the patient. If the incision is large, close it with stitches and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, there is no harm from it, for the ointment will heal and join it. If the cold swelling is inclined towards the inside of the eyelid, towards the inner surface, then turn the eyelid back and suspend the cold swelling with a hook without needing an incision, and excise it from every side.
If it penetrates the eyelid with the cut, that will not harm. Then wash the eye after cutting the cold swelling with salt water and treat the area with that which heals until the patient recovers.
Chapter Ten.
In the treatment of the sharnq which appears in the eyelid.
Sharnq is a fleshy growth which forms in the layers of the upper eyelid.  This most often occurs in children, and it weighs down their eyes, causing them to suffer from colds in the mornings, and they are unable to look at the sunlight. Because the tears flow quickly to them.
Therefore you see them always leaning on their faces, and on them they sleep, and their eyelids under the eyebrows are moist, and a swelling has arisen over them, and the sharnq is clearly visible.  When you press the area with your fingers, you feel the sharnq between them. The method of treatment is that the patient should place his head in your lap.
Then you take a linen cloth and prepare a wick from it, and you make a circle from that wick, the size of which encompasses the sharnq from every side, then you place it on it and press with your fingers from every side to gather the sharnq in the middle of the circle.
Then you make an incision in the middle of that moisture with a lancet, a transverse incision, and the incision should not be larger than the incision which is made in bloodletting, and as for the depth, you should incise all the skin until you reach the sharnq.
Then, in most cases, the sharnq protrudes from the incision in the form of a piece of white fat.  You draw it out with a cloth that you have wrapped between your fingers, while you turn your hand to the right and left until it heals. And beware of increasing the incision, lest the lancet reach the eye and harm it.
If the sharnq does not appear to you in the first incision, then you should increase the incision a little gently until the sharnq appears, then you draw it out as we said, then you dip cloths in vinegar and water and place them on the area and bandage it tightly. And some people crush salt.
And they place it inside the incision so that what remains of that moisture dissolves, then you treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs in the area, then treat it with soothing poultices until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On types of eye injuries.
If superfluous membranes grow on the eyelid, contrary to the natural course, under the natural membranes and eyelashes, then they harm the eye and cause various diseases, such as constant tearing, relaxation of the eyelids, whiteness, and thickening, until this becomes a cause of blindness.
Eye injuries are of four types: either cauterization with fire, or with a caustic medicine as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, or the injury may be treated by cutting and suturing, or with a reed, as I will describe.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand. If it does not turn, insert a needle with a thread from beneath the eyelid and pass the needle with the thread from above, close to the hair itself, and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid.
Turn it with a probe, then incise the inside of the eyelid, avoiding the superfluous hair, with a sharp scalpel from the larger canthus to the smaller canthus. Then remove the thread and place a small pledget of cotton or cloth under the eyelid. Then mark the eyelid with ink.
Like the shape of a laurel leaf, except that the shape should be according to how much you want to raise the eyelid, because this varies among people. Some may need a portion of the eyelid to be cut according to how much the eyelid is relaxed.
Some may need less to be cut; all of this depends on the relaxation of the eyelid. Then incise with the scalpel along the two lines that you marked, beginning from the larger canthus to the smaller canthus, and each incision should be near the natural hair, about the thickness of a probe.
Then insert the hook into one corner of the skin and remove it all. Then join the edges with stitches using a fine wool needle and thread. Wipe away the blood and stick the remaining threads to the eyebrows with some adhesive substance.
If you wish to do so, otherwise it does not matter. Then leave the stitches and threads for about three or four days, then treat it. If you wish, you can leave the wound without stitching and treat it with things that dry and constrict, for the eyelid will rise when the wound is closed and healed; stitching is better.
This method of blepharoplasty was mentioned by the ancients, although it is burdensome for the patient. It is a good procedure and there is no danger in it. Another method of blepharoplasty is to mark on the eyelid a shape like a laurel leaf, as we described. Then raise the eyelid with three hooks, either separate or grouped in this manner:
Then cut away the excess eyelid with small scissors in this manner, cutting moderately.
If you cannot hold the hooks and they do not stay in place, take a needle with thread and insert it into the middle of the shape, and insert another thread near the larger canthus and a third thread near the smaller canthus, and gather the threads together with your fingers moderately, then lift it with a moderate lift.
Lift the marked skin entirely as we described, then bring the edges of the wound together with sutures and treat it until it heals. If hot swelling appears when you cut or incise, then calm that swelling with qirt and similar soothing ointments.
It may also happen that the lower eyelid inverts its edges, so you should use what we mentioned of cutting, suturing, cauterization with fire, and with strong medication; cauterization with fire or with strong medication is more effective and gentler on the patient than cutting and suturing, and you should do the cutting and suturing in both.
Cauterization with a reed is done in this manner: you invert the eyelid and make an incision on the inside as described, then you make two thin reeds or pieces of wood, their length equal to the length of the eyelid and their width less than the width of a scalpel.
And you carve notches at their ends on both sides where the threads will be attached, then gently bring them together on what remains of the eyelid and tightly pull the reeds from both sides and leave it for days; the tightly drawn skin will die, blacken, and decay.
Until it falls off by itself; if it is slow, cut it with scissors, then treat it until it heals.  When it unites, the eyelid will be raised and the edges of the eyelids will not be inverted. This is an image of the two reeds; make them in this shape and this exact size in length and width.
Chapter Twelve.
On raising ingrown eyelashes from the eye with a needle.
If it is one or two hairs, the procedure is to take a thin needle, thread it with a smooth, fine silk thread, then bring the two ends together and tie them in a very gentle knot, and let the length of the thread be about a span.
Then attach another fine thread to the needle, shorter than the first, and tie its ends if you wish. Then have the patient put his head in your lap, and let it be near the sun so that the work may be clearly seen, for it is delicate work. Then insert the needle with the thread into the root of the ingrown hair.
And quickly pass it through the eyelid until you bring it out above the natural hair. Then pull the thread upwards on the needle and draw into the needle that hair, whether it is one, two, or three, but no more.
Then pull it with your hand until you extract the hair with the needle from the eyelid along with the natural hair. If you pull the needle and the hair does not come out with it, pull the needle downwards with the thread that you had attached to it until you bring the needle out from the upper opening.
Then return the hair to it and pull both until it rises to the upper opening and you see it clearly. Then remove the needle and thread, and pull and bind the eyelid, leaving it bound for two or three days until the hair settles in place and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, either add to it a long hair from the natural hair so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows longer, then raise it.
Chapter Thirteen.
In the treatment of the stye which occurs in the upper eyelid.
The stye that affects this area is called arnabiyya.  This stye is either natural or accidental. The accidental one results from the healing of a wound, incision, cauterization, or the like.
The method of treatment is to incise that adhesion and separate its edges, then place a linen wick between them and tie it until it heals.  It is not advisable to use things that dry and constrict in its treatment.
For if you do that, the stye will return more severely than it was.  Rather, use things that relax, such as fenugreek and a poultice with water in which mallow and flaxseed have been boiled, and a diachylon ointment dissolved with some fat, then apply it to the wick and treat it with this.
The key to its treatment is to try by every means to prevent it from adhering in its original state. This treatment that we have mentioned is only a partial repair of the stye, not a restoration of the area to its original appearance.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the treatment of a stye which forms in the lower eyelid.
This stye which forms in the lower eyelid is what is truly called a stye. It can be natural or accidental.  The accidental type results from a wound, incision, cauterization, or something similar.
The method of treatment is to take a needle with doubled thread and insert it into the flesh, passing it from the left canthus to the right canthus, so that the thread is at both ends of the flesh. Then stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with a wide scalpel.
If the eyelid returns to its proper shape, that is sufficient. Otherwise, take a probe and place it on the site of the incision, using it to turn the eyelid inside out. Then make two incisions on the inner side of the eyelid. The ends of these incisions should meet at the corners of the previous cut, forming an angle. Until, when they meet, their shape resembles this shape:
This is the Greek letter lambda. Then remove that flesh, so that the sharp edge is below what lies next to the eyelid.  Then bring together the separated parts with two stitches, sewing it with a wool thread. Then treat it with the emollient and astringent medications previously mentioned until it heals.
If the fistula resulted from an incision, suture, or cauterization, it is necessary to make a simple incision without affecting the eyelashes, as previously described, then separate the lips with a probe, as mentioned.
In short, in treating a fistula, whether above or below, you should proceed according to the nature of the fistula, for they vary greatly in appearance.  The skilled surgeon devises a solution by whatever means possible.
Until the shape returns to its natural form or approximates it.  The disease itself, in most cases, will indicate the appropriate treatment and instrument.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may occur in many people, whereby the upper eyelid adheres to the white of the eye, preventing easy movement and hindering its natural functions.  It is necessary to insert the tip of a probe under the eyelid.
And lift it upwards or stretch it with a hook, then cut the adhesion with a delicate scalpel, not sharp like the cutting scalpels, but rather somewhat blunt, lest the patient be disturbed during the procedure and the scalpel slips, injuring the eye with its sharpness.  Your cutting should be as if you are peeling off that adhesion.
Wait until the eyelid returns to its natural state and the adhesion is completely healed, then pour saline water into the eye to wash it, or instill a solution of vitriol in water, then separate the eyelid from the eye with a linen wick.
And place on top of the eye wool moistened with egg white, and after the third day use the astringent eye drops until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cutting the pterygium and the fleshy excrescences.
The pterygium is of two kinds: either nervous, resembling a thin, hard membrane; or non-nervous, resembling a thick, white moisture; if touched with iron or attempted to be removed with a hook, it breaks up.
And a hook does not become fixed in it. Both kinds begin from the greater canthus, gradually extending until they cover the pupil and obstruct the light and the movement of the eye. The method of cutting them is that the patient should place his head in your lap.
Then he opens his eyes, and you raise the eyelid with your hand; then you seize the pterygium with a slightly curved hook and stretch it upwards; then you take a needle and thread it with a horsehair or oxhair and strong thread, and bend the point of the needle slightly and pierce it into the middle of the pterygium.
Then you pass it through with the needle and tie the pterygium with the thread, and stretch it upwards, and dissect with the hair the side of the pterygium next to the pupil, as if you were spreading it with the hair to its end; then you cut the remainder at the root of the canthus with a fine scalpel or small scissors, and leave the natural flesh of the canthus.
So that there may not result from its cutting a constant flow of tears.  The pterygium may be stretched with the hook alone, or with the thread as we have said, then the smooth scalpel dissects the end, the picture of which is this:
And be careful that the scalpel does not touch the cornea, lest it cause a wound and injure the pupil. Then, after the cutting, throw into the eye some powdered salt or  zink sulphate powder and leave it until the next day, then treat it as is appropriate until it heals.
If the pterygium is not vascular and you are unable to insert a needle into it or to fix a hook in it, this type of pterygium is often treated with medicine and heals as described in the classification.  If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a fine, smooth scalpel with a weak edge, in this manner: And dissect the pterygium from above gently with it. If you see that it is loosening and breaking up and the dissection has affected it, then instill into the eye immediately zinc sulphate powder or red sulphate powder or powdered salt and bandage the eye until the next day.
Then resume work on it until it is all gone, unless a hot inflammation intervenes in the eye; then leave it and treat the hot inflammation until it heals, then resume working on it by dissection until it heals. As for the fleshy excrescence of the caruncle
If the excrescence harms the eye excessively, then hook that excrescence with a hook and cut some of it, but do not overdo the cutting lest it cause a flow of tears; then instill into the caruncle red or zinc sulphate powder until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the excision of the pterygium and what grows of excessive flesh in the eye.
In the eyes of some people, a red, fleshy growth may develop, covering the pupil, or approaching it, or overflowing onto the eyelids, and sometimes the eyelids turn outward, resembling a rose flower.
The procedure is that the patient should place his head in your lap, then open his eyes, and you should remove all that red flesh with suitable hooks.
Or grasp it with a scalpel or forceps, then cut the flesh little by little until you have removed it all by cutting, being careful not to injure the eye during the operation.  Your cutting should be done either with the scalpel we described for cutting the membrane, or with small scissors. Like those with which one plucks hairs, as their image follows in the chapter after this one.
When you have finished cutting and removed all the growth, fill the eye with crushed salt, or instill into it some red vitriol or similar caustic medicine, and apply a cotton pledget soaked in egg white to the outside of the eye to prevent inflammation.
If some of the growth remains and the bleeding is excessive, and you fear inflammation, leave the eye and treat it with remedies that alleviate inflammation, then resume the procedure until it is healed.  Do the same with excessive flesh of this type that appears in the eye.
However, you should avoid treating any eye that is weak by nature or by circumstance with instruments of iron, and do not undertake any of these procedures until you are sure that the organ can tolerate the operation.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the removal of pterygia from the eye.
Pterygia are red vessels that grow over the eye, obstructing vision and weakening the eye over time.  First, you should examine; if the eye with pterygia is strong and has no other disease besides the pterygia,
then removal is appropriate. You should instruct the patient to place his head in your lap, then hook the vessels with one or two hooks, depending on your skill. The hooks should be delicately curved in this manner:
Or they may be two double hooks in a single piece, in this manner:
Then, delicately, cut those vessels with fine scissors, wiping away the blood from time to time until you see that the vessels have been removed from the eye and the bleeding has stopped. Be careful not to injure the eye with the tips of the scissors, and perform this procedure during midday, facing the sun.
Be very careful in your work so as not to cut anything other than those vessels.  When finished, instill green or red vitriol into the eye; its sharpness will destroy any remaining pterygium. If you cannot remove it all at once, bandage the eye with something that soothes the pain.
Leave it for a few days until the pain subsides and the inflammation is gone. Then repeat the procedure in the same manner until it is healed. This is an image of the appropriate scissors.
Leave it [and let the patient's head rest on the pupil's lap, while another wipes away tears and checks the front and back of the eye, and you]. The basic procedure is to open the eye with fixed or triangular openers that the pupil holds.
Sharp, and let the patient's head rest on the pupil's lap, while another wipes away the tears and checks the front and back of the eye, and you have the choice to begin cutting from the top or bottom of the eye.  It is preferable to cut two strands on the upper side, one at the end of the crown and the other at its beginning.
Grasp it between the middle and index fingers, then hook it from below opposite them.  Then grasp it between the little and ring fingers, and lift your hand gently until you see the whole pterygium raised. Cut it from the smaller side if you wish, with scissors or with a delicate scalpel.
Then thread the needle and lift the entire pterygium until it is raised above the conjunctiva. Do not cut the strands, for the tears and blood will obscure your vision.
Then cut with scissors whatever extends beyond the cornea from above and below, so that nothing remains attached except to the cornea. If you can lift it from the cornea with a needle, that is best; this may be possible in some people.
And when you have cut it, let the wound heal for several days until the pain subsides and the inflammation disappears, then resume the operation.
Chapter Nineteen.
On replacing a deviated nasal bone.
Physicians call a deviated nasal bone a "nasal polyp". If you treat it with cauterization or a strong, burning medicine as described above, and it does not heal, then there is no remedy except to incise the swelling when it is ripe and to evacuate all the moisture or pus within it.
Until the bone is exposed.  If the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, scrape it with an instrument of this shape:
The rasp head, made of Indian iron, is called *khushnah*. Its head is round like a button, finely engraved with the pattern of a file or rasp. You place it on the corrupted part of the bone and then rotate it between your fingers, applying gentle pressure, until you know that the corruption has been removed.
Do this repeatedly, then dress the wound with drying, astringent medications. If the wound closes and flesh grows over it, and the discharge ceases, and forty days pass without recurrence, suppuration, or further incident,
know that it is healed. Otherwise, there is no remedy except to return the fistula to the nasal cavity in this manner:  Expose the bone again with an instrument or strong medicine.  Once the bone is exposed, take a probe of this shape:
The tip of the instrument is triangular, and its shaft is conical, tapering to the tip, as you see. Then place it on the bone itself, near the ethmoid bone, but slightly away from the eye. Then rotate the hand with the probe until it penetrates the bone and the patient feels it.
He will feel air escaping from it if he holds his hand over his nose. Then dress the wound with the drying astringent medications, as I have taught you. If flesh grows and the area hardens, the matter which used to flow outwards will return to the nose, which will be easier for the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
On returning a protruding eye.
If the whole eye protrudes, and no harm or defect occurs in the vision, then the patient should be relaxed, and then cupped on the temples. Then apply a cupping glass to the nape of the neck without restriction, and gently suck.
Then apply a plaster made from acacia, aloe, frankincense, and mastic. Then bind the eye from above over the dressing with many pledgets.  The method for making the pledgets is to take many soft lint pieces and fold each one into four layers, making many of them.
Then place them one on top of the other as much as the eye needs, then bind it firmly with a strong cloth. Repeat the cupping with suction without restriction. Then remove the cupping glass and leave the eye bound for a day and a night.
If the bandage loosens during that time, it should be tightened gently. Then remove the bandage after a day and a night, as we said. If you see that the eye has returned, well and good. Otherwise, repeat the application of the dressing, pledgets, binding, and cupping until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On cutting the uvula.
If a hernia occurs in the uvular layer, then it protrudes and emerges outside the eyelids like a grape, and the person's appearance is disfigured because of it, and you wish to cut it, then the procedure is as I describe.
It is that you insert a needle into the base of the uvula from below upwards, then insert another needle with a doubled thread from the side of the uvula, and pass it through, and leave the first needle as it is.
Then you cut the place where the doubled thread bends and tie some of the uvula around the needle with it and pull it gently, then you remove the needle and place on the eye wool moistened with egg white and leave it until the threads fall out with the uvula, then you treat the eye with what strengthens it.
Until the wound heals.  You may also pull the uvula from each side with a circle of cloth until the eye is further protruded gently, then you cut with a fine scalpel reaching to the depth of the eye, so the white moisture flows and the eye descends and wilts in place, then you pull it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-two.
In the treatment of coma.
This ailment which is called coma is a fluid that gathers in the eye resembling descending water, but it is not. The method of treatment is that you seat the patient on a chair upright, then you take his head in your hands from both sides.
And move it until you see the fluid go to the bottom with your eye, then it becomes fixed and does not move, and the light departs and the patient sees things as he used to see them. And if it does not descend to the bottom, we know that it is water. If the descent of the fluid is not achieved by what we have mentioned,
otherwise, seat the patient before you, then take a thin scalpel and make a small incision with it above the cornea at the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the ciliary region until the material comes out.
Then, when it has come out, instill into the eye warm water in which honey has been mixed, or water in which fenugreek and honey have been boiled, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have already mentioned the types of water in the classification and which of them is suitable for cupping, in a detailed and explained statement, so take it from there correctly. Then, you should seat the patient before you, sitting squarely facing the light near the sun.
And you bind his healthy eye and bind it tightly, then you lift the eyelid of his eye with your left hand if the eye in which the water is the left eye, or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then you place the tip of the cupping instrument near the cornea, with the thickness of a probe, in the same white of the eye on the side of the smaller canthus, then you push the cupping instrument forcefully while rotating it with your hand until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel with the cupping instrument that it has reached something empty.
And it should be that the extent to which the cupping instrument goes into the depth is the distance that is from the pupil to the end of the blackness, which is the cornea. For the copper is seen by the observer himself in the eye due to the clarity of the corneal membrane. Then you move the cupping instrument upwards to the place where the water is.
Then you press it downwards again and again. If the water descends immediately, then the patient will see what his sight has been opened to immediately, with the cupping instrument in his eye. Then it rests a little. If the water rises, then lower it again without removing the cupping instrument.
If it settles and does not rise, then remove the cupping instrument gently, while twisting it with your hand little by little. Then dissolve some clean Andarani salt in water and wash the eye with it from the inside, then place outside the whole eye a compress or wet wool.
With rose oil and egg white, and you bind it with the healthy eye. As for us and the people of our time, we place crushed cumin with egg white on it. If the cupping instrument does not enter the eye due to its hardnessfor some people have very hard eyes Then you should take the scalpel called the *bard*, whose image is this:
Then pierce with it only the adherent part and do not overdo the piercing.  It is only that you make a slight opening for the cupping instrument, then insert the cupping instrument as we mentioned. When your work is complete, prepare for the patient a firm lying-down position in which he lies on his back in a dark room.
And he must abstain from all movements and from coughing, and you make his food whatever softens his nature, and he does not move his head to the right or left at all. The bandage remains in place until the third day, then you untie it in that dark room and test his vision.
And you show him things, then you reapply the bandage until the seventh day.  And it is not appropriate to do that during treatment or after cupping at your time, but you should avoid that before the water rises quickly with intense vision.
If a hot swelling appears, then it is necessary to untie the eye before the seventh [day] and treat that with what calms the swelling, until when it calms then at that time you release his vision and place a kerchief on his face so that his sight may be trained from under it for days while he is in that dark room, then...
He gradually leaves the house and manages his causes. And know that in cupping, the learner cannot do without observation many times, then afterwards he proceeds to the work. And it has reached me from some Iraqis that he mentioned that they make in Iraq a cupping instrument that is perforated which draws out the water with it.
And I have not seen anyone in our country make that, nor have I read it in any book of the ancients. And it is possible that this is recent. And this is an image of types of cupping instruments for one who is ignorant of them to look at:
It is made of copper especially, and its end is with this very thinness, triangular-ended, sharp.
Chapter twenty-four.
In the treatment of the flesh that grows in the nose.
Various superfluous flesh may grow in the nose; some of it resembles a scorpion with many legs, and some is cancerous flesh, hardened and dark in color, and some is soft flesh, not dark in color.
If any of this flesh is soft, not malignant nor cancerous, then the patient should sit before you facing the sun, and you should open his nostril and insert the snare into that flesh, then draw it outwards, and then cut what you have seized of it with a sharp, delicate scalpel from one side.
Until you know that all the flesh has gone; if any remains that you could not cut, then remove it gently with one of the delicate instruments, so that nothing remains. If the bleeding overcomes you, or a hot inflammation appears,
then treat it accordingly.  If it is one of the malignant tumors, then act quickly to cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is gone. Then, after cutting, place vinegar and water, or a potion, in the nose. If the nose opens and the moisture passes into the throat,
know that he is healed. If the moisture does not flow as it should, know that inside there is flesh growing on the weakened bones that the instrument did not reach in cutting. Then you should take a linen thread of some thickness.
and tie many knots in it, making the space between each knot the width of a finger or less. The patient should then carefully insert one end of the thread into his nose with a probe, or with whatever is available after making it like a button, and draw it with his breath until it reaches the pharynx.
and emerges from his throat.  Boys often do something similar in school, and it is easy for anyone who wants to do it. Then gather together the two ends of the thread, the one that emerged from the mouth and the other that remained in the nose.
Then use the knots in the thread to saw at the flesh, doing this until you know that the flesh has been cut by the knots of the thread. Then remove the thread and, after wiping away the blood, place a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment in the nose. Do this for three days or more.
until the ointment consumes all the remaining flesh. Then, as a final step, place a lead tube in the nose for a few days until it heals. If it needs drying treatment, use that.
This is an image of the instrument with which ointments and medicines are dropped into the nose:
It is made of silver or copper, similar to a small, open oil lamp, and its spout is also open.
And if you wish, make the tube closed like a reed, and the spout of the instrument flat.
It has a handle at its end, as you see, to hold it when you heat oil or whatever you wish of juices or liquids in it.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On warts growing on the tip of the nose.
Often a wart grows on the tip of the nose, and it increases and grows larger with the days until its appearance becomes unsightly.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut it off at its first appearance and to excise all of it, then apply to the place either cautery or a burning medicine that acts in place of cautery. If you fail to cut it off until it becomes large, then look: if it is hardened, solid, dark in color and insensitive,
then do not apply iron to it, for it is a cancerous tumor, and I have often seen the cutting of this tumor followed by great harm to its sufferer.  But if the tumor is soft to the touch, not dark in color, and you see that complete excision is possible, then empty the patient's bowels and cut it off without hesitation or delay, and treat the place with what dries and constricts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On suturing the nose, lip, and ear. If their connection is severed by a wound or something similar, know that when a severance of connection occurs in one of these cartilages, the treatment rarely works except in some people.
Then, if any of this happens to someone, look: if the wound is fresh with its blood, bring together the edges of the wound with sutures and then treat it until it heals. And if the separation of the connection has separated its edges and each part is whole, then you should scrape each part to the thickness of its outer skin until it bleeds.
Then bring together the edges with sutures, tighten them, and sprinkle on them mastic and frankincense, powdered, and place on top of the sutures a plaster of walnut ointment or another of the consolidating ointments. Leave it bandaged for two or three days, then unbind it.
And change the medication and leave it until the threads separate by themselves, then treat it with ointment until it heals.  The method of suturing is to bring together the separated connection either with needles, as we described in suturing the abdomen, or with thread, as I explained there.
Chapter twenty-seven.
On removing the knots which appear in the lips.
Many people develop in the inside of their lips small, hard tumors; some resemble a lentil, some are smaller, and some larger.  It is necessary to turn back the lip and incise over each knot and hang it with a hook.
And cut it from each side, then pack the area after cutting with powdered alum until the bleeding stops, then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the area with something astringent until the wounds heal.
Chapter twenty-eight.
On cutting away excess flesh in the gums.
Often, excess flesh grows on the gums, which the ancients called epulis.  It is necessary to hang it with a hook or grasp it with forceps and cut it at its base, and leave it a while to drain or bleed, then apply powdered alum to the area.
Or one of the astringent drying powders. If that flesh returns after treatmentfor it often doescut it again and cauterize it, for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter twenty-nine.
On extracting teeth with iron.
Rough, unsightly scales may gather on the surfaces of the teeth, inside and out, and between the gums.  They may blacken, yellow, or green, even causing damage to the gums and making the teeth unsightly.
The patient should sit before you with his head in your lap.  Clean the tooth or teeth showing scales and anything resembling sand, removing it all completely. Do the same with blackness, greenness, yellowness, and anything else until clean.
If the scaling is removed at the first attempt, well and good. Otherwise repeat the scaling on another day, and then a second and third, until you achieve your goal.  Know that different shaped and numerous tools are needed for scaling teeth, depending on what is required.
For the instrument used to scale a tooth from the inside is not the same as that used to scale it from the outside, nor that used to scale between the teeth, which is of another form.  These are several forms of scaling instruments, all of which should be ready at hand:
Chapter Thirty.
On the Extraction of Teeth.
You should treat a painful tooth by every means possible and delay its extraction, for there is no replacement if it is extracted, because it is a noble substance, except when its extraction is unavoidable.
If the patient is determined to have it extracted, you should ascertain that the tooth is the source of the pain. For often the patient is deceived by the pain and thinks that it is in the healthy tooth, and he extracts it, but the pain does not go away until the diseased tooth is extracted.  We have seen this done repeatedly by barbers.
If you ascertain which tooth is causing the pain, then you should incise around the tooth with a moderately strong scalpel, so as to separate the gum from all sides, then move it gently with your fingers or with delicate forceps little by little until you loosen it.
Then firmly grasp it with the large forceps, and hold the patient's head between your knees, having steadied it so that it does not move. Then pull the tooth straight out so as not to break it. If it does not come out, then take one of those instruments and gently insert it under it from every side, and move it as you did before.
And if the tooth is perforated or decayed, you should fill that hole with a piece of cloth and pack it tightly with the end of a thin probe, lest it crumble when you pull on it with the forceps.  You should also thoroughly incise around the gum on all sides.
And take care not to break it, for if some of it remains, it will cause the patient a greater affliction than his original pain. And beware of doing what the ignorant barbers do, in their haste and rashness in extracting it without using what we have described.
For they often inflict great harm on people, the least of which is that the tooth breaks and all or part of its roots remain. Or that it is extracted with part of the jawbone, as we have witnessed many times. Then, after its extraction, let him rinse his mouth with a potion or vinegar and salt. If bleeding occurs, which often happens, then crush some alum and pack the site with it.
Otherwise, cauterize it if the alum does not help. The image of the delicate forceps with which you first move the tooth:  they should have long ends, short handles, and be thick so that they do not bend when you grip the tooth with them. And this is the image of the large forceps:
They should be, as you see, thick in the handles so that when you grip them, they do not yield or bend, and short in the ends.  Let them be of Indian iron or of well-tempered steel, with polished ends, and at the end, teeth that interlock to provide a firm and secure grip.
And the ends may be made in the shape of a file, which will also provide a strong grip.
Chapter Thirty-one.
On the extraction of the roots of molars and the removal of fractured jaw bones.
If, when extracting a molar, a root remains broken, you should place cotton soaked in butter on the area for a day or two until the area relaxes, then insert the forceps or hooks. whose ends resemble the beak of the bird called the balaraja.  This is an image of the hooks:
They are made like a file on the inside or like a scalpel. If you are unable to remove it with these hooks, you should drill into the root and expose all the flesh with a scalpel, then insert an instrument that resembles a small lever, which is depicted here:
short and somewhat thick at the end, and not too slender, lest it break. If the root comes out with this, well and good; otherwise, use these other instruments, which are depicted here: the first has a triangular end and is somewhat thick.
Another fine triangular image:
And we may also use this instrument with two branches, which is also depicted here:
and other instruments and tools previously mentioned in the extraction of molars.  We may also use this instrument which resembles a large hook, which is depicted here:
triangular, with a curved end, somewhat thick, so that it does not break, and not too slender. Know that the instruments for molars are numerous, as are all the other instruments; they are almost innumerable, and a skillful and clever craftsman may invent for himself instruments according to what the work and the diseases themselves indicate.
For there are diseases for which the ancients did not mention instruments, due to the variety of their types.  If a bone of the jaw or one of the bones of the mouth is broken or decays, then search for it in its place with one of these instruments and hooks mentioned for extracting roots.
And we use a curette, its image is this:
It should be somewhat thick to control the bone so that it does not slip until the bone is removed. And we treat the area with appropriate medicines. If the bone has slight decay, remove its decay and blackness until it is clean, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter thirty-two.
On the extraction of molars that grow upon others.
If molars grow other than in their natural course, they are unsightly, especially if this happens in women and slaves.  It should be examined; if the molar has grown behind another molar,
and its extraction or pushing back is impossible, extract it. And if it is attached to another molar, cut it with this instrument, whose image is this:
It resembles a small beak and should be made of sharp Indian iron, very sharp at the point.  Your cutting should be done over many days because of the hardness of the tooth, lest you shake other teeth. But if it is protruding and you are able to file it down, file it with an Indian file, whose image is this:
It should be entirely of Indian steel, and its point very finely engraved, like the file with which needles are made. File the tooth little by little over many days gently, lest you shake the tooth and it falls out. Then smooth it further and remove it with some forceps.
And if a tooth is broken, and it hurts the tongue when speaking, then it should also be filed until the roughness of that fracture is removed, it becomes smooth and even, and does not hurt the tongue or impair speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On binding loose teeth with silver or gold threads.
If the front teeth become loosened and move as a result of a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on anything to eat for fear that they will fall out, and you have treated them with astringent medicines but the treatment has not worked.
The solution is to tie them with a gold or silver thread; gold is better because silver tarnishes and rots after a few days, while gold remains unchanged forever and is not subject to this. The thread should be of moderate thickness, according to what fits between the teeth.
The method of binding is to take the thread and insert its loop between the healthy teeth, then weave the ends of the thread between the loose teeth, whether one or more, until you reach a healthy tooth on the other side by weaving.
Then weave back to the side where you began and gently but firmly tighten the thread so that it does not move at all.  The tightening should be at the roots of the teeth so that it does not come loose. Then cut the excess ends of the thread with scissors, gather them together, twist them, and hide them between the healthy tooth and the loose tooth.
So that it does not injure the tongue. Then leave it tied as it is for as long as it remains. If it loosens or breaks, tie it again with another thread. In this way, he will enjoy it his whole life. This is a picture of the teeth and the method of binding between two healthy teeth and two loose teeth, as you see.
One or two teeth, after falling out, may be returned to their place and bound as described, and they will remain.  A skilled dentist does this. He might carve bone from cattle bone to make a tooth-like shape and place it where the tooth has gone. And it is tightened as we said, and it remains, and he enjoys it.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
Concerning cutting the ligament which appears under the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament which appears under the tongue may be either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, from a healed wound.
And the procedure is that you open the mouth of the patient and place his head in your lap and raise his tongue, then cut that nervous ligament transversely until the tongue is released from its restraint.  If there is some stiffness and complexity, and this is from the healing of a wound, then place a hook in it and split it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knot is undone.
And beware that the incision is not deep in the flesh, lest you cut an artery there and bleeding results. Then let him rinse his mouth after the cutting with rose water or with vinegar and cold water, then place under the tongue a pledget of linen which the patient holds in each night.
Lest it unite again.  If bleeding occurs, place powdered alum on the place. If the bleeding overcomes you, cauterize the place with a lentil-shaped cautery suitable for this. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On the removal of a frog generated under the tongue.
A swelling resembling a small frog sometimes occurs under the tongue, preventing the tongue from its natural function, and it may grow large enough to fill the mouth. The procedure is to open the patient's mouth toward the sun and examine the swelling.
If you see it dark in color or black, hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it, do not interfere with it, for it is a cancer. But if it is whitish and moist, insert a hook into it and incise it with a fine scalpel, freeing it from all sides.
If the bleeding is excessive during the procedure, apply powdered vitriol until the bleeding stops, then resume the procedure until you have completely removed it. Then rinse with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with other appropriate remedies until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
In the treatment of tonsil tumors.
And what grows in the throat from other tumors; sometimes glands appear inside the throat, similar to those that appear outside, called tonsils. If you treat them with what was mentioned in the section and they do not heal,
then look: if the tumor is hard, dark in color, and insensitive, do not use the cautery; and if it is red in color and its base is thick, do not use the cautery either, for fear of hemorrhage, but leave it until it matures; then either you will incise it or it will burst on its own.
And if it is white in color, round, and its base is thin, this is what should be cut.  The procedure is that you should look before operating whether its hot inflammation has completely subsided or has somewhat diminished.
Then seat the patient facing the sun, his head in your lap, and open his mouth.  Have an assistant hold his tongue down with an instrument of this shape:
Make it of silver or copper, thin like a knife. When you depress the tongue with it and the tumor is visible, take a hook, pierce the tonsil with it, and pull it outward as much as possible, without pulling along any of the membranes.
Then cut it with an instrument of this shape, resembling scissors except that its two ends are curved, each end very sharp and close to the other, made of Indian iron or Damascus steel:
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with a scalpel of this shape:
sharp on one side and blunt on the other. After cutting one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner. Then, after cutting, have the patient gargle with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water in which pomegranate peel or myrtle leaves, or similar astringents, have been boiled until the bleeding stops. Then treat it until it heals.  Other tumors besides the tonsils may grow in the throat; cut them as described for the tonsils.
I treated a woman with a tumor that had grown inside her throat, resembling a polyp, somewhat insensitive to the touch, and which almost blocked her throat. The woman breathed through a narrow passage and was prevented from eating and drinking water.
She was near death if she remained a day or two. Two branches extended from the tumor, emerging from her nostrils. I acted quickly, inserted a hook into one of them, and pulled; a suitable piece was extracted.
Then I cut it where I reached it through the nostril. I did the same with what protruded from the other nostril. Then I opened her mouth, depressed her tongue, inserted the hook into the tumor itself, and cut some of it; only a little blood flowed.
The woman's throat opened, and she immediately began to drink water and eat. We continued to cut away parts of the tumor repeatedly over a long period; the tumor replaced what was cut, until it lasted a long time for both of us.
Then I devised a plan and cauterized the tumor inside the throat with a cautery iron; it stopped growing. Then she traveled away, and I do not know what God did with her afterwards.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On the excision of a uvular tumor.
That which is called an `anba' if it descends as a growth to the uvula and swells, and is white and oblong, is called a pillar; and if it is thicker at the bottom and round, it is called an `anba'. If it is treated with what we mentioned in the classification and the treatment is not effective,
and I saw that the hot tumor had subsided from it and it was thin, then it should be cut, and whatever is gathered together and round, and does not have length, and is bloody or the color of pus, or black, or insensitive, then it should be avoided cutting it.
For there is danger to the patient.  Therefore, if you see it in the way described, of its whiteness and length, you should seat the patient facing the sun, and press down his tongue with the instrument which we described previously, then insert the hook into the `anba'.
And pull it downwards and cut it with one of the two instruments which we mentioned in cutting the tonsils.  And you should not cut from it except that which exceeds the natural state without excess, for if you cut more from it, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after cutting, use what we described in cutting the tonsils and treat it until it heals. If the patient is afraid of cutting it, then you should use a way to cauterize it without fear or caution.  The way of cauterization in it is with a strong medicine, and that is that you place the patient's head in your lap.
Then press down his tongue with the instrument we mentioned, then take from the strong water which we mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and mix it with unquenched lime, and make it neither thick nor thin, and fill the concavity of this instrument with it. And this is its image:
Its end, in which you place the medicine, has a concavity like the concavity of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula itself, and the patient lying on his side, so that the saliva flows from his mouth from inside his throat, lest something descend from it into his throat and harm him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine while you press it on the uvula for about half an hour until you see that it has blackened and the sting of the medicine has subsided. And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the end of a probe and wet the cotton in the medicine.
And insert the probe with the cotton into the tube from above until the cotton adheres to the uvula. Do this repeatedly until you achieve what you want from cauterizing the uvula, then leave it. For it will wither and fall after three or four days. If you need to reapply the medicine, reapply it.
And after cauterization, wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in butter and dry the medicine around it, then rinse with cold water and treat from the outside with poultices and from the inside with gargles until it heals. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and further from fear.
The uvula may also be treated with something gentler than cauterization and cutting; it is treated with fumigation in this manner: take  fenugreek, hyssop, thyme, savory, wormwood, chamomile, and gum tragacanth, and similar herbs. Gather them all or some of them in a pot and immerse them in vinegar.
And boil it, the pot being coated with philosopher's clay, and there should be a hole in the middle of the lid through which a hollow instrument is fitted, made in this manner, of silver or copper.
And insert the end containing the pomegranate into the patient's mouth until the steam rises to the uvula through the tube, until the uvula is well moistened. Then repeat it several times until it withers. And beware of performing this treatment at the first onset of the swelling.
For it often increases the swelling.  Rather, it should be done when its hot swelling subsides. If you do not have this instrument, take a reed and fit an eggshell to its end, lest the patient's mouth be burned.
For the eggshell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth, and this is a good and safe treatment.
Chapter thirty-eight.
On removing thorns and other things that lodge in the throat.
Often a bone, fishbone, or something else lodges in the throat.  You should remove what is visible to the eye after you have depressed the tongue with the instrument, towards the sun.
So that you may see what is in the throat.  And if it does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, you should make the patient vomit before his food is digested in his stomach, for perhaps the lodged object will come out with the vomit. Or the patient should swallow a piece of beet or a stalk of lettuce.
Or he should swallow a mouthful of dry bread, or you should take a piece of soft sea sponge, tie it to a string, and then have him swallow it. When it reaches the place of the thorn, pull the string quickly.  Do this several times.
Often the thorn or bone will stick to it and come out. If it does not come out by these means, then use a lead instrument of this shape:
It should be slightly thicker than a probe, and at its tip a hook. The patient inserts it into his throat gently, raising his head upwards, and being careful not to touch his throat, lest he cause coughing and thereby push the bone or thorn further in. Or the physician inserts it with his hand.
And it is better for the patient to insert it himself, as he knows the location of the lodged object.  He pushes downwards or pulls upwards with the instrument, doing all this as much as he is able, until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On removing leeches lodged in the throat.
If you treat the leech with what we mentioned in the section on medicinal treatment and it is not effective, then look into the patient's throat in the sunlight, after you have depressed his tongue with the instrument I described to you.
If you see the leech, pull it out with a small hook or a gentle, firm probe. If you cannot do so, take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the leech, then insert a heated iron into the tube's interior.
Do this several times, and the patient should abstain from water for the whole day.  Then he should take a jug full of cold water, open his mouth into it, and rinse his mouth with it without swallowing a drop, moving the water from time to time with his hand.
The leech will fall out when it feels the water. If it does not come out as described, fumigate the throat with urine and asafoetida using the instrument described for fumigating the tonsils; do this several times, and it will fall out.
The method of fumigation is as follows: Take a pot containing hot coals and cover it with a lid having a hole in the middle.  Fit the end of the instrument into this hole, then add the fumigant. The patient places his mouth on the end of the tube and closes his mouth tightly so that the fumes do not escape, until it is known that the fumes have reached his throat.
Then the leech will fall out. If it does not, repeat the fumigation several times. The patient should endure thirst, eat salty food and garlic, and not drink water; the leech must come out with this treatment.  This is an image of an instrument used to extract a leech from the throat when it is visible.
It resembles forceps, as you see, except that it has this curve which enters the throat, and its tips resemble a bird's beak; they are as rough as a file, so that when they grip something, they do not let go.
Another instrument is used to compress the tongue. It is a wide iron ring, large enough to fit over the tongue in the mouth.  It is inserted into the mouth standing on its side, so the mouth remains open without the patient's will, and he cannot remove it at all until it is removed.
Description of the instrument with which the mouth is opened in this very way.
Chapter Forty.
It will speak of the cauterization of tumors and their incision.
Tumors are numerous and varied in kind, as will be described one by one in this book.  They differ in their cauterization and incision in two ways: one, from the type of tumor itself and what it contains of humors.
The second type is due to the locations in the body where they occur, for a tumor occurring in the head is not like a tumor occurring in the anus, and a tumor occurring in a fleshy place is not like a tumor occurring in a joint; each has its own treatment.
Some tumors should not be cauterized except after the pus in them has matured and is complete; others should be cauterized while they are raw and have not fully matured, such as tumors that are close to the joints.
Because if a tumor occurs near a joint and its condition persists until it putrefies what is around it, it may corrupt the ligaments or nerves of that joint, thus causing the crippling of that limb, or the tumor may be near a vital organ.
For if you delay its cauterization until it matures, you will harm that vital organ; or it may be near the anus, so cauterize it raw.  It is necessary to cauterize a raw tumor, not fully matured, which is near the anus, lest it putrefy the depths and penetrate into the interior of the anus, becoming a fistula.
And it becomes to a degree incurable.  And you should know the time for cauterizing tumors that have fully matured; it is when the pain of the tumor subsides, the fever goes away, the redness and throbbing decrease, the head of the tumor softens, and all other signs appear.
And you should apply the cauterization to the lowest part of the tumor if that is possible, so that the flow of pus may be easier downwards, or in the thinnest and most prominent part of the tumor.  And let the cauterization extend along the length of the body if the tumors are in the hands or feet.
And the places of muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries, and in general all flat places which do not bend. But as for those which bend, let the cauterization be done according to that place.  And if the tumor is in fleshy places it is best to leave it uncauterized.
Until its maturation is complete, as we have said. For if you cauterize it before that, the flow of pus from it will be prolonged, and it will be very harmful and dirty, and perhaps its lips and depths will harden.  And some tumors may be cauterized across the body, if necessary, or according to the needs of the member.
And you should use a single cauterization for small tumors, and for large tumors a wide cauterization or many incisions, according to the size of the tumor.  And there may be tumors which should have their skin scraped and cut if they have become like a rag.
And it has reached the point where it has died, as happens in many abscesses and collections. And some of them are incised with a three-cornered incision, and some have a piece cut from it in the shape of an ivy leaf, like the inguinal tumor.
And some use a circular incision and a crescent-shaped incision and similar incisions. And if it does not have a head, like smooth flat tumors, then it should only be cauterized simply.  And if the tumor is large and has been collected for a long time, and you cauterize it, you should not rush to remove all the pus at that time.
Rather, remove some of it, then dress the tumor until another day, then remove some of the pus also. Do this repeatedly gradually until you remove all of it.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old man, for the vital spirit often dissolves with the sudden exit of pus, so the patient may die and you are unaware. Therefore, be extremely cautious in this matter.
After cauterizing these tumors, you should wipe the wound and look. If the wound of the tumor is small, or the incision is single and simple, then use a thread of linen or old cotton. And if the tumor is large and the incisions of the cauterization are many
