then you should insert into each incision a tent, until some of them reach each other. And if some of the skin of the tumor has been cut or eroded, then you should pack it with old cotton or linen lint without moisture, and dress it until the third day.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar after soaking a linen cloth in them and applying it to the bleeding site repeatedly.
If the bleeding persists, you should use the procedures, treatments, and styptics that we have described in many places in this book and in the division. If you are in winter and the location of the tumor is very sensitive, you should moisten the bandages with a warm syrup and oil.
And place them on the site. And if you are in summer and the locations are fleshy, you should apply the bandages soaked in water and oil or in syrup and oil, all of it cold, until the third day, as we said. Then you should unwrap the tumor and wipe it.
And use similar treatments until it heals. This is what you need to know about the general treatment of tumors. As for the detailed method, I have described the treatment of each tumor in a categorized and summarized way.
Chapter forty-one.
On the incision of tumors that appear on the scalp.
Small tumors appear on the scalp; they are a type of boil contained in sacs, similar in appearance to a chicken's crop.  There are many types, some fatty, and some containing a moisture resembling mud.
Some contain a moisture resembling pus and broth, and so on, and some are hardened and solid.  There is no danger in incising and removing all of them, unless you encounter an artery during the incision.
The procedure for incising them is to first probe them with the instrument whose image follows, which is called the *muddass*, so that you know what they contain. If what they contain is moisture, incise them longitudinally with a simple incision in this manner:
Begin the incision from point B to point C. When the moisture is emptied, remove the sac that contained the moisture and cut it completely; do not leave any of it at all, for it often recurs if any remains.
Then, dip cotton in yellow ointment, if available, otherwise in salt water, and fill the wound with it. Leave it until the next day, for it will consume what remains of the sac. Then, apply the cotton with the yellow ointment again and a third time if necessary.
Until you are certain that nothing of the sac remains. Then treat the wound with ointments until it heals.  If the swelling contains fatty matter, incise it in this manner:
Make a firm incision and place hooks in the edges of the wound, and peel it from every side.  Make every effort to remove the membrane which contains it. If an artery obstructs you, do as I described before; and likewise do if the swelling is hardened from the incision.
The treatment is the same as I have described. The incision on a hardened swelling is easier because it has little blood and moisture. Some of these swellings in the head in some people may have no moisture at all.  For I incised a swelling in...
the head of an old woman, and I found the swelling like hard, rough, white stone, impossible to break; if anyone were to throw it, it would cut him.  It was unlike other swellings occurring in the head, except for those which appear in the heads of children.
And at the roots of the ears, incise them all with a simple incision, and make their bottoms from their lower parts so that the flow of matter to the bottom is easy.  Then treat them with whatever treatment suits them.
Chapter forty-two.
Concerning the incision on the swellings which frequently appear in the neck. These swellings frequently appear in the neck, under the armpits, and in the groins; they may appear in the rest of the body. That which appears in the neck may be single or multiple; some may develop from others.
And each swelling is within a special membrane of its own, as is the case with the abscesses and the swellings of the head as we described. And the types of these swellings are many: some are calcified, some contain fluids, and some are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
I have not seen any of them in good condition to the touch, and their appearance was close to the color of the skin; they moved in every direction and were not attached to a nerve of the neck, nor to a vein, nor to an artery, nor were they deep-seated. It should be incised with a simple incision from above to below the body, in this manner, from line J to line B.
And peel it from every side and stretch the edges of the skin with a hook or two hooks, or many hooks if needed, as we said in the case of the swellings of the head. Remove it little by little, and be careful not to cut a vein or nerve. And let the scalpel not be very sharp.
Lest your hand exceed in cutting, or the patient be disturbed, and you cut what you do not need to cut.  If you cut a vein or artery and it hinders you, place in the wound powdered vitriol or some styptics which stop the blood.
And tighten the wound and leave it until the intensity of the swelling subsides and the wound relaxes and begins to suppurate; for the bleeding then stops. Then return to your work until you finish. Then search with your index finger if anything remains, then other small worms; cut them and clean them out.
If at the root of the worm there is a large vein, then it should not be cut from its root, but it should be tied with a doubled tight thread and left until it falls off by itself without harm. Then stuff the wound with old cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, and then treat it.
And if you cut the whole worm, then you should bring together the edges of the wound and stitch it immediately after you ascertain that no part remains.  If you see that a part of the worm's body remains,
then you should aim to cut to its root and base, and use sutures and what we have mentioned.  And as for worms containing fluids, cauterize them slightly where their ripeness appears, and make the cauterization from below, as we said.
Then, after cauterization, use a dressing with Egyptian ointment or similar to consume what remains of the corruption, until you see that the wound has been cleansed. Then treat it with ointments that promote flesh growth until it heals.
Chapter Forty-Three.
Concerning the incision of the throat for a tumor that occurs inside the throat.
The ancients mentioned this incision in the throat, but I have not seen anyone in our country perform it. This is the account of their words. As for those with angina, they should avoid incision of the throat, for they will not benefit from it.
Because all the veins and the lung will be weak.  But those with a hot tumor in the mouth, throat, or tonsils, if there is no ailment in the windpipe, then it is necessary to use incision of the throat to escape the harm that results from suffocation.
Then, if we wish to do this, we should incise the throat below three or four rings of the windpipe, a small transverse incision between two rings, to the extent of the incision in the peritoneum, not in the cartilage. This location is suitable for the incision.
Because it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels are distant from it. If the physician is timid, he should extend the skin of the throat with a hook, then incise the skin until he reaches the windpipe, avoiding the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should incise the peritoneum which we have described.  The incision of the windpipe is indicated by the phlegm which comes out of it along with what is excised, and by the interruption of the voice.  The wound is left open for a time. When the time in which strangulation was feared has passed, the edges of the wound are brought together from the skin.
And its outline alone, without the cartilage. Then use medicines which cause flesh to grow until it heals. The author of this book said, the explanation of this statement which we have related is only if they see that the patient's throat is obstructed by one of these tumors which they have mentioned.
And the patient was close to death and his life was about to end, they went to incise the throat so that the patient might breathe somewhat through the site of the wound and be saved from death. They were ordered to leave the wound open until the crisis of the illness is over, and its crisis is three days or so.
Then they ordered the wound to be sutured and treated until it healed. What I myself witnessed is that a maidservant took a knife and ran it across her throat, cutting some of the windpipe. I was summoned to treat her, and I found her gasping like one who is slaughtered.  So I uncovered the wound.
Then I found the blood which came out of the wound to be slight, and I was certain that she had not cut a vein nor an artery. Breath was coming out of the wound, so I proceeded, I sutured the wound, and treated it until it healed, and nothing happened to the maidservant except for a slight hoarseness in the voice.
And after days she returned to her best condition. From this, we say that an incision of the throat is not dangerous.
Chapter forty-four.
Concerning the incision upon the tumor which appears in the throat from the outside and is called the elephantiasis of the throat.
This tumor which is called elephantiasis of the throat is a large tumor of the color of the body, and it is frequent in women, and it is of two kinds.
Either it is natural or accidental. As for the natural, there is no remedy for it; as for the accidental, it is of two kinds: one resembling fatty swellings, and the other resembling the tumor which arises from the complication of the artery, and in its incision there is danger. Therefore, one should not at all use the iron upon it, except that which is small of it, if you probe it and examine it with the probe and you find that it resembles a fatty swelling and is not attached to anything of the veins, then incise it as you incise upon swellings. And remove it with what contains it of the cyst if it is in a cyst, and otherwise, remove it completely, then treat the place with what is appropriate of treatment.
Chapter forty-five.
Concerning the incision of various swellings.
Swellings are numerous in types, and I have mentioned all their types in the classification. It is necessary that we explain here the difference between a swelling and an abscess, since it is difficult.  So I say that with an abscess there is heat and fever and frightening pains until the boiling of the matter subsides. And the putrefaction is completed, then the fever and intensity subside.  A swelling has neither heat, nor fever, nor pain, and it is contained by a serous cyst which is its special covering, and it is the color of the body, and its beginning is like a lentil, and it becomes like a blister, larger and smaller.
And it is of two types: either fatty, or it contains moisture. The colors of the moisture are many, as I mentioned in the classification.  Therefore, if you come to treat a swelling, you should probe and examine it first with the instrument called the probe, as its image comes in the chapter after this.
Assess the size of the swelling, then remove the probe and look at what comes out after it. If fluid of any color comes out, then incise it with a simple incision as I mentioned in the case of other swellings. If no fluid comes out after the probe, then know that it is fatty.
Then make a firm incision on it in this manner as I have taught you, and attach it with hooks, and peel the skin from every side gently, and preserve the cyst if you are able to remove it intact with the swelling.
If the cyst is ruptured during the procedure and you are unable to remove it intactand this often happensthen remove it piece by piece until nothing remains. For if any part of it, however little or much, remains, the swelling will return in most cases.
If it overcomes you and a little remains, cauterize the wound at the end with some sharp cautery irons, and tighten the wound and put on it something that cools hot swelling and treat it with other treatments until it heals. If the swelling is large, then stitch its lips and treat it with that which heals.
If you encounter a bleeding or non-bleeding vein and bleeding occurs, immediately cauterize the area with powdered vitriol and leave it constricted for two or three days until the wound becomes infected and the flow of blood ceases. Then return to removing what remains of the swelling.

Chapter Forty-Seven.
On treating the breasts of men.
The breasts of some people may swell at puberty, resembling women's breasts, remaining swollen and unsightly.  Whoever dislikes this should make a crescent-shaped incision on the breast, in this manner.
From line B to line C. Then remove all the fat, then fill the wound with cauterizing medication, then bring the edges of the wound together with sutures and treat it until it heals. If the breast droops and relaxes because of its size, as happens with women,
then it should be incised in its upper sides with two crescent-shaped incisions, each connecting to the other at their ends so that the larger line encircles the smaller, in this manner: From line B to line C, then remove the skin between the incisions and remove the fat, and use what we mentioned of sutures and dressings and whatever treatment is needed until it heals.
If you cannot remove all that needs to be removed because of the patient's anxiety or because of bleeding, then you should pack the wound with cotton soaked in caustic ointment and leave it until it consumes the remaining fat, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter forty-eight.
On the treatment of tumors that appear under the armpit.
These tumors which appear under the armpit are of the kind of hard swellings contained in a sac, and some contain fluids.  Those containing fluids should be incised with a crescent-shaped incision in this manner.
From line B to line C.  If all the fluid in the tumor is evacuated, pack it with used cotton and leave it until the next day, then remove the cotton.
And treat it with a suitable ointment until it heals. If the tumor is of the kind of hard swellings, incise it as we mentioned in the incision for swellings. If the wound progresses to the point where pus forms, cauterize it as we mentioned previously.
Chapter forty-nine.
On incising a tumor that appears near an artery or vein, called an aneurysm.
If an artery is injured and the skin above it heals, a tumor often results from this; similarly, a vein may also develop a swelling and tumor.
The signs by which one knows whether the tumor and swelling are from an artery or from a vein: if the tumor is from an artery, it is oblong and deep-seated in the body; and if you press the tumor with your finger, you feel as if it has a creaking sound.
That which is from a vein, the tumor is round on the surface of the body, and incision of these tumors is dangerous, especially those in the armpit, groin, and neck, and in many other places in the body where they are very large; therefore, one should avoid treating them with the iron.
And those that are also in the extremities or in the head should be avoided. As for those that are from a swelling of the mouth of an artery, make a longitudinal incision on the skin, then open the incision with hooks, then dissect the artery and free it from the membranes.
Until it is exposed, then insert a needle underneath and pass it to the other side, and tie the artery with a doubled thread in two places, as I have shown you in ligating the two arteries in the temples; then incise with a scalpel the area between the ligatures until all the blood in it is removed.
Then the tumor will resolve, and you use the treatment that produces pus until the ligatures fall off, then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals.
If the tumor is from a severed vein, then you should grasp with your hand as much of the tumor as possible together with the skin, then insert a needle below the place you grasped with your hand and pass it through with a doubled thread until you bring it out on the other side. Then tie the tumor tightly with it, as I have described to you in tying the aneurysm from both sides with the ends of the thread. If you fear that the threads will slip, then insert another needle with another thread under the whole tumor at the intersection of the first needle.
And tighten your threads in four places, then incise the tumor in its middle until what is inside comes out. Then cut away the excess skin and leave what was tied. Then place on it a dressing that you have soaked in a syrup and oil. Then use treatment with twisting and ointments until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
On the tumor which occurs from the twisting of a nerve.
Just as a tumor appears in an artery and vein, so a tumor appears in a nerve if a blow occurs to it or it occurs from excessive exertion or the like.  This is more common in the wrists and heels.
And in every place where the joints move, it is a  firm tumor, its color resembling the rest of the body. In most cases it is painless, and if pressed hard the patient feels something like numbness. The tumor is not deep within the body.
Rather, it is under the skin and moves in every direction, neither forward nor backward. As for that which is in the joints, it should not be treated with iron, for it may cause gangrene. As for that which is in the head or forehead...
then incise the skin with a scalpel. If the tumor is small, grasp it with forceps and cut it from its root. If it is large, suspend it with a hook and peel it off, then remove it and close the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals.
Chapter fifty-one.
Concerning the excision of warts which appear on the abdomen.
Many people develop warts on their abdomens and on the rest of their bodies. These are called fungal warts because of their resemblance to mushrooms. Their base is thin and their head is thick; their edges may be transformed, and some are small while others become very large.
I once saw a man who had two warts on his abdomen that were very much like mushrooms, white and thin at the base, their edges transformed and cracked, with moisture constantly flowing from them. I excised them; one weighed about eighteen ounces, the other about six.
In excising them, observe that if the patient is moist and the wart is white, moist, and thin at its base, then excise it with a broad scalpel. Have cautery irons ready in the fire, for often much blood flows when they are cut.
If the bleeding overwhelms you, cauterize it immediately. If you see that the patient is cowardly and fears being cut with iron, then take a strong lead thread and tie it tightly around the wart of this description, leaving it for two days, then increase the tightness of the lead, continuing to tighten it.
Do this until it affects the wart, until it is severed and falls off by itself without trouble. If the wart is thick at its base, there is some fear and risk in cutting it, especially if it is in the abdomen, but it may be possible to cut off half of it or a part of it.
Then cauterize it so that it does not return, and then treat it until the wound heals. Beware of attempting to cut a wart that is dark in color, insensitive, and unsightly, for it is a cancerous tumor, and we shall discuss cancer later.
Chapter fifty-two.
In the treatment of umbilical hernias.
An umbilical hernia has many causes: either from a rupture of the peritoneum of the abdomen, from which fat or intestine protrudes, as occurs in other hernias; or from blood emanating from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned; or from gas accumulating in it.
If it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum and protrusion of fat, then the color of the swelling will be similar to the color of the body, it will be soft without pain, and will appear in various positions. If it is due to the protrusion of the intestine, its position will be, as we have described, more variable. If you press it with your fingers, it disappears, then returns; and sometimes there is a gurgling sound. It increases greatly with the entry into the bath and with severe exertion. If it is due to moisture, it will be soft.
It does not disappear when you press it with your hand, nor does it increase or decrease. If it is due to blood, then, along with these signs, the swelling appears dark. If it is due to a growing flesh, then the swelling is firm, hard, and remains at a constant size.
If it is due to wind, its touch will be soft. The procedure in this case is that you should look: if the umbilical hernia is due to blood from an artery or vein, or wind, then you should refrain from treating it, for there is danger and deception in that, as I have informed you in the chapter where I mentioned the swellings which arise from the artery and vein.
If the umbilical hernia is due to the intestine or fat, then you should instruct the patient to restrain himself and stand erect, then you should draw a line with ink around the entire umbilicus. Then you should order him to lie on his back before you, then you should incise with a wide scalpel around the umbilicus at the place which you marked with ink, then you should extend the center of the swelling upwards with a large probe, then you should tie the incised area with a strong thread or silk tendon, tightly.
The knot of the ligature should be a slip-knot. Then you open the constricted center of the swelling above the ligature and insert your index finger, and seek the intestine. If you find that the ligature has grasped it, then loosen the slip-knot and push the intestine into the abdomen. If you find fat, then extend it with a probe.
And cut its excess. If an artery or vein obstructs you, then ligate it well and return to your work. Take two needles and thread them with strong threads, and insert the needles into the incision which you made around the swelling, crosswise, having passed them through. Then tighten the swelling in four places on the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the constricted flesh mortifies and falls off by itself, or cut it if it becomes thin and mortified. Then treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If the umbilical hernia is from flesh that has grown there,
or from moisture, then it is necessary that you constrict the swelling as we said, and remove the blood or moisture that you find in it, then treat it with what will unite the area.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of cancer and how to treat it with medication, and the warning against treating it with iron, lest it ulcerate, and we mentioned the cancer that develops in the uterus and the warning against treating it.
And the ancients mentioned that when the cancer is in a place that can be completely excised, like the cancer that is in the breast or in the thigh and similar easily removed organs, especially if it is small and beginning, but when it is old and large, then you should not approach it.
For I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me achieve that, and the procedure, if it is possible as we said, is that you should first purge the patient of black bile several times, then you should bleed him if there is obvious fullness in the veins.
Then you should position the patient in a way that allows you to work, then you should apply to the cancer the appropriate hooks, then you should scrape it from every side with the skin completely, until nothing of its roots remains, and let the blood flow and do not stop it quickly.
Rather, squeeze the areas and drain all the thick blood with your hand or with whatever instruments you can, and if during the procedure you encounter great bleeding from a severed artery or vein, then cauterize the vessel until the bleeding stops, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
Chapter fifty-four.
In the treatment of the bubo.
We have explained in the classification the types of dropsy, how the accumulation of water occurs, and the signs of each type and its treatment with medications.  That which is treated with iron is only the ascitic type, and the tympanitic type and the fleshy type are not at all approached with iron.
For that is deadly, if you have treated this ascitic type of bubo with medication and your treatment has not been effective, then look: if the patient has become extremely weak, or if he has another illness besides the bubo, such as
cough or diarrhea or the like, then beware of treating him with iron, for it is dangerous. But if you see that the patient is strong, with no illness besides the bubo alone, and he is neither a child nor an old man, then the proper procedure is to have the patient stand before you. and an attendant behind him squeezing his belly with his hands and pushing the water downwards towards the pubic region, then you take a pointed scalpel in this manner:
pointed at both ends, long, with a defined tip like a scalpel, except that it has a slight curve so that it does not penetrate the intestine during the procedure and injure it. Then observe if the bubo has originated from the side of the intestines.
Then it is necessary to make the incision three fingers' breadth below the navel, parallel to it and above the pubic region. If the bubo has originated from liver disease, then let your incision be three fingers' breadth to the left of the navel; and if its origin is from spleen disease, then let the incision be on the right side by three fingers' breadth.
Nor should the incision be on the side on which the patient wishes to lie down, lest the discharge flow to that weak area. Then pierce the entire skin with the instrument, and insert the instrument into that incision, and raise your hand.
with the scalpel between the skin and the peritoneum, as if you are stripping it off, and the amount you strip off should be about the size of a fingernail or so. Then pierce the peritoneum until the scalpel reaches an empty space, which is where the water is. Then remove it and insert into the hole the instrument which has this shape.
It is like a tube made of a thin cane, made of silver or copper or smooth polished lead, having a small hole at its lower end and three holes in its sides, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
And one may put its sharpened end in the form of a pen nib, in this shape:
At its upper end is a ring.  If the instrument reaches the water, it will immediately descend onto the instrument and drain a moderate amount of water. For if you drain more than necessary at once, the patient may die from the dissolution of his vital spirit.
Or he may suffer a faintness close to death. But drain it according to his strength and what the patient's condition indicates to you of the strength of his pulse and the good color, then remove the instrument and the water will be retained, for it will be retained immediately.
Because of the skin which covers the hole in the peritoneum, which I instructed you to prepare in that manner. Then return the instrument another day if you see the patient able to bear it.
And remove also a little of the water; do this for several days, being watchful and cautious against error, until only a little water remains. If you fear for the patient and leave a large quantity of water, treat him by burial in hot sand.
And profuse sweating in the bath and sun, and accustom him to thirst, and treat him with drying medicines until he recovers. And cauterize the stomach, liver, and spleen after removing the water, as I described above.
Chapter Fifty-five.
In the treatment of children who are born with their urinary passages imperforate, or with a narrow or misplaced opening.
Some boys may be born with their rectum imperforate; it is necessary to perforate it as soon as they are born.
With a very fine scalpel, in this manner:
Then place a thin lead nail in the opening, bind it and keep it there for three or four days. When he wants to urinate, remove it, and when he has finished, replace it. If you do not use the lead, the urine that flows to the place may not allow it to close.
As for one whose opening is narrow, it is treated with lead, as we have said, for many days until it widens.  Those whose opening is misplacedsome are born with the opening at the end of the rectum and cannot urinate forward.
until he raises the urethra upwards with his hand, and it is not born before because the semen cannot reach the uterus in a straight line; this is a very bad condition. The method of treatment is that the patient lies on his back.
Then stretch his rectum with your left hand with a strong pull and shave the head of the urethra with a sharp blade or scalpel, the size of a pen, or as if you were carving something so that its middle protrudes, resembling a penis, and the opening is in the middle as it should be.
And be careful during your work not to cause bleeding, for this often occurs; therefore, stop the bleeding and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-Six.
Concerning the ulcer that appears in the foreskin and penis, and the blackness, corruption, and adhesion of the foreskin to the penis.
This ulcer often appears in the urethra; it is a fleshy, rough growth, and there is benign and malignant.  The benign should be snagged with a fine hook.
And cut it until you have completely removed it, then apply a cotton swab soaked in Egyptian ointment, then treat it afterwards with palm ointment until it heals. But if the ulcer is malignant and dark in color, then you should cauterize it after cutting and removing it.
If the ulcer is in the foreskin of an uncircumcised man, and part of the ulcer is inside the foreskin and part outside, then you should remove the inside part first, until it heals; then treat the outside part.
Because if you treat both at the same time, you risk perforation of the foreskin.  Blackness and corruption may also appear in the labia and foreskin; you should excise everything that has blackened and is beginning to corrupt or has already corrupted.
Then apply honey mixed with crushed and sifted pomegranate peel and alum, then treat it with other remedies until it heals. If bleeding occurs, use cauterization with a crescent-shaped cautery iron in this manner:
For cauterization is beneficial in both cases, I mean bleeding and a corrupted wound. If the glans is eroded and completely destroyed at some point, then you should insert a lead tube into the urethra so the patient can urinate through it. As for the adhesion of the foreskin to the glans
This adhesion occurs in those whose foreskin is intact and who have not been circumcised.  It may also occur from a wound or swelling.  You should separate it with a blunt scalpel until the bond is broken and the glans is freed on all sides.
If it is difficult to distinguish them completely, you should remove some of the glans and some of the foreskin.  Because the foreskin is thin, it may easily perforate due to its thinness. Then separate the foreskin from the glans with a thin linen cloth soaked in cold water so that they do not adhere again.
Then treat with an astringent drink until it heals.
Chapter fifty-seven.
On the cleansing of boys and the treatment of what befalls them of harm.
Circumcision is nothing other than the separation of a connection like other wounds, except that since it is done by our will and is especially used in boys, it is necessary that we describe the best practice in it.
And the easiest way leading to safety, so I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books because it was not used in their laws, but rather it is something we have acquired.
by experience, and that is that I found the majority of surgeons and barbers using purification with the scalpel and scissors, and they use the curette and ligature with thread and cutting with the nail.  And I have tried all of these methods.
So I found nothing better than purification with scissors and ligature with thread, because purification with the scalpel often causes the skin to retract, because the skin of the foreskin is two layers, so perhaps the upper skin is cut and the lower layer remains.
He is forced to make another cut and renewed pain, and purification with the curette does not guarantee against cutting the end of the urethra because it might enter its opening. As for purification with the nail, the skin might slip and your work is spoiled or the boy's skin is naturally short...
so often they are born like that and do not need purification, and I have seen that.  As for purification with scissors and ligature with thread, experience revealed its advantage to me, because the cut of the scissors is even, because the blade above is like the blade below.
So when you squeeze your hand with the evenness of the two blades, you cut to a uniform measure and in one time, so the ligature of the thread becomes like a wall for the skin of the urethra from all sides, with no mistake at all. And the method of work first is to reassure the boy.
Especially if he is one who understands a little that you are only tying the thread in his urethra and leaving it until another day, then cheer him up and please him in every way you can and that he accepts with his mind, then make him stand before you upright.
And let him not be sitting, and hide the scissors in your sleeve or under your foot, so that the boy's eye does not fall upon it at all, nor on any of the instruments. Then you insert your hand into his urethra and inflate the skin and lift it upwards until the glans penis emerges.
Then you clean it from whatever dirt gathers there, then tie the marked place with a doubled thread, then tie a second ligature a little below it, then hold with your thumb and forefinger the lower ligature firmly, and cut between the two ligatures. Then quickly raise the skin upwards and bring out the glans penis, then let the blood flow a little, for it is better and reduces swelling of the urethra. Then wipe it with a damp cloth, then sprinkle on it some ash of burnt dry gourd, for it is the best I have tried, or finely ground alkanet, for that too is excellent.
Then quickly raise the skin upwards and bring out the glans penis, then let the blood flow a little, for it is better and reduces swelling of the urethra. Then wipe it with a damp cloth, then sprinkle on it some ash of burnt dry gourd, for it is the best I have tried, or finely ground alkanet, for that too is excellent. Then apply to the wound from above a compress of a cooked white egg, beaten with fresh sweet rose oil, and leave it on until the next day, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
And this is an image of the scissors suitable for circumcision:
It should be sharp, cutting, not crooked, with a nail-like point, and the length of the two blades should be equal to the length of the handles. As for the mistake that occurs in circumcision, sometimes the entire inner skin or part of it is turned over during the cutting, so you should extend it immediately with your fingernail before the area swells.
And cut it evenly; if you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then pull it with a hook and cut it. If three days have passed and what is under the glans penis remains swollen and inflamed, leave it until the acute inflammation subsides, and then remove it gently.
And cut it as seems best to you, and be careful of the glans penis; for if something of the glans penis is cut, it does no harm, so treat it with that which heals the wound from the medicaments which we described in the chapter on medicaments.
And if more skin than necessary is cut and it retracts upwards, that also does not do much harm; treat it with what we have mentioned until it heals.
Chapter fifty-eight.
On the treatment of urine retained in the bladder.
Urine retained in the bladder is due to its obstruction by a stone, or clotted blood, or pus, or a growth, or something similar.  If you treat this with what we mentioned, the types of treatment described in the classification.
And if the urine does not flow and you see that its retention is due to a stone that has lodged in the neck of the bladder, then it is necessary that the patient sit on his knees, then a man should mount his back and press his belly towards his back, and the patient should hold himself as much as he can.
Then the neck of the bladder will be forced to push the stone backwards, and the urine will flow. If it does not flow with what we mentioned, and the matter becomes severe for the patient, then it is necessary to use its extraction with the instrument called a catheter, whose image is this:
It is made of silver and is thin, smooth, hollow like a bird's quill in thinness, about a span and a half long, with a gentle funnel at its head.  The way to draw off the urine with it is to take a doubled thread and tie to its end wool or cotton firmly.
And insert the end of the thread into the bottom of the catheter and cut with scissors if any of the wool is in excess so that it enters the tube and closes it like a button. Then lubricate the catheter with oil or butter or egg white and seat the patient on a chair.
And cleanse his bladder and urethra with moist ointments or oil and lukewarm water. Then gently insert the catheter into the urethra until it reaches the root of the urethra, then bend the urethra upwards towards the navel, then push the catheter inside.
Until the urethra is completely bent, then push the catheter inside until, when it reaches near the rectum, incline the penis downwards with the catheter inside, then push it until it reaches the bladder and the patient feels that it has reached something empty.
This is done in this manner because the passage through which the urine passes is tortuous. Then gently pull the thread with the wool; the urine will follow the wool. Then remove it and the urine will flow out, and replace the catheter.
Continue doing this until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.
Chapter fifty-nine.
How to inject the bladder with a syringe.
And the instruments suitable for this are made of silver or hollow ivory, having a long tube as fine as pus.  And if you wish to instill waters and medicaments, this is done with an instrument called a syringe, and this is its image:.
It is made of silver or hollow ivory, having a long tube as fine as a hair, entirely hollow except for the tip, which is solid and has three holes, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
And the hollow part in which the propellant is, should be only as large as to fit it without excess, so that when you draw up something of the fluids, it is drawn up, and when you push it, it is pushed out to a distance, like the syringe used to throw oil in naval battles.
Then if you wish to inject fluids into the bladder, you insert the tip of the syringe into the fluid and draw it upwards with the piston, then the fluid will be drawn into the body of the syringe; then you insert its tip into the urethra as we described in the catheters, and then you push the fluid with the piston.
Then that fluid will reach the bladder immediately, so that the patient feels it. And this is the image of a delicate clyster with which the bladder is injected:
It is made of silver or lead; its upper end resembles a small funnel, and below it is a groove in which the ligature is placed; then take a camel's bladder and put in it the fluid which you wish to inject into the bladder, and then tie it tightly between the two grooves with a doubled thread.
And warm that fluid slightly over the fire, then insert the tip of the clyster into the urethra, then press your hand firmly on the bladder with the fluid until the patient feels that the fluid has reached the bladder.
If you do not have a bladder, take a piece of parchment and make a circle from it and pierce it all around; then insert a strong thread into those holes as it goes round, then gather the circle as you gather a napkin, after having placed in it whatever you want of fluids, ointments and water.
Then tie it to the instrument and do with it as you did with the bladder, pressing with your hand until the fluid reaches the bladder.
Chapter Sixty.
On the extraction of a stone.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of stones and their treatment with medicines, and mentioned the difference between stones generated in the kidneys and stones generated in the bladder, and the signs of all of that, and mentioned the stone in which one operates with the hand and incision.
This is the stone generated specifically in the bladder, and that which lodges in the urethra, and I am describing that with clear explanation and brevity.
I say that the stone generated in the bladder most often affects boys, and among its signs is that urine exits the bladder resembling water in its thinness.
And sand appears in it, and he scratches his penis and plays with it, and often it hangs down, then spreads, and the anus protrudes with it in many of them.  It is easy to remove from boys until they reach fourteen years of age, and difficult in the elderly. As for young men, it is intermediate between those.
And the one whose stone is larger, his treatment is easier, and the small one is the opposite. So if we proceed to treatment, it is necessary first that the patient be given an enema to remove all the feces in his intestines, for it may prevent the presence of the stone upon examination.
Then the patient is taken by his feet, and shaken and jolted downwards so that the stone descends to the neck of the bladder, or he jumps from a high place repeatedly. Then you seat him before you, erect, and his hands under his thighs, so that the whole bladder inclines downwards, then you examine him.
And palpate him from the outside. If you feel the stone in the space, then immediately begin to incise upon it.  If it does not fall under your touch at all, then it is necessary to lubricate the index finger with oil from the left hand if the patient is a boy, or the middle finger.
If he is a fully developed boy, then insert it into his anus and search for the stone until, when it falls under your finger, you move it little by little to the neck of the bladder. Then press upon it with your finger and push it outwards towards the place you wish to incise.
And you order a servant to press the bladder with his hand, and you order another servant to stretch with his right hand the labia upwards, and with his other hand to stretch the skin beneath the labia away from the place where the incision will be. Then take the lancet whose shape is this:
And incise between the anus and the labia, not in the middle, but towards the left buttock. The incision should be upon the stone itself, your finger in the anus, and you press it outwards obliquely, so that the incision is wide on the outside and narrow on the inside, to the extent that allows the stone to exit.
Not larger, for perhaps the finger in the anus will press the stone during the incision, and it will exit without difficulty. And know that there may be stones that have angles and edges, making their exit difficult for that reason.
And some are smooth, resembling an acorn and round, so their exit is easy.  But if it has angles and edges, then increase the incision slightly. And if it does not exit in this way, then you should contrive a way, either to grasp it with a strong forceps whose end is like a file.
To firmly grasp the stone so it does not escape, or else to insert beneath it a delicate instrument with a hooked end.  And if you are not able to remove it, widen the incision slightly. And if you are overcome by blood, staunch it with alum.
If there are more than one, push the largest first towards the opening of the bladder, then incise upon it, then push the smaller one afterwards.  And do likewise if there are more than two.  If it is very large, then it is folly to make a large incision upon it, for it exposes the patient to one of two things.
Either death, or a constant dribbling of urine because the wound will not heal at all. But try to push it out, or contrive to break it with forceps until you remove it in pieces.
When you have finished your work, cauterize the wound with costus, aloes, and myrrh, and bandage it. And place over it a cloth moistened with oil and vinegar, or with rose oil and cold water, to soothe the hot inflammation. Then let the patient lie on his back, and do not untie the bandage until the third day.
When it is loosened, wash the area with plenty of water and oil, then treat it with palm ointment and basil ointment until it heals. If excessive hot inflammation, ulceration, and the like occur in the wound, such as blood clotting in the bladder and preventing the flow of urine  which is known by the passage of blood with urine 
insert your finger into the wound and remove that blood, for if it remains it will lead to the corruption and putrefaction of the bladder. Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and treat each type of [problem] with its appropriate treatment until it heals.
In all these treatments, it is necessary to bind and draw together the thighs to secure the medicines that are applied to the area. If the stone is small and has lodged in the passage of the penis and prevented the flow of urine, treat it as I have described before it necessitates incision.
Often I have dispensed with incision by this treatment, which I have tested, and it is that you take a probe of steel, which is of this form:
The end is triangular, sharp, and fixed in a shaft; then take a thread and tie it around the penis below the stone, lest it return to the bladder; then gently insert the steel probe into the urethra until the probe reaches the stone itself.
And turn the probe in the stone itself little by little, aiming to pierce it until you pass through it to the other side; then the urine will flow immediately.  Then press your hand on what remains of the stone outside the penis, for it will crumble and be expelled with the urine, and the patient will recover.
If this treatment is not feasible for you due to some impediment, then tie a thread below the stone and another thread above the stone, then incise upon the stone in the penis itself between the two ligatures, and then remove it.
Then untie the ligature and clean the clotted blood which has formed in the wound.  The reason for tying the thread below the stone is to prevent it from returning to the bladder, and the other ligature from above is so that when the thread is untied after removal of the stone, the skin may return to its place and cover the wound.
Therefore, when you tie the upper thread, you should lift the skin upwards so that it will return when you are finished and cover the wound, as we said.
Chapter sixty-one.
On the removal of stones in women.
Stones rarely develop in women. If a woman does develop a stone, its treatment is difficult and impossible for many reasons. One is that the woman may be a virgin, and another is that you will not find a woman who permits herself to a physician.
If she is chaste or one of those with whom marriage is forbidden, and thirdly, you will not find a woman skilled in this craft, especially in working with the hands; and fourthly, the place of incision for the stone in women is far from the location of the stone, thus requiring a deep incision.
And in that there is danger. If necessity demands it, then a skilled female physician should be sought, but they are rarely found. If you cannot find one, seek a chaste and gentle male physician, or bring a skilled midwife experienced in women's matters, or a woman who knows something of this craft.
Then prepare her and instruct her to do everything you order her to do, first examining for the stone.  If the woman is a virgin, the finger should be inserted into her rectum to search for the stone; if found, press it with the finger.
Then order the incision. If she is not a virgin but a married woman, instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and search for the stone after placing her left hand on the bladder and pressing it firmly.
If she finds it, she should move it from the mouth of the bladder downwards as far as possible until she reaches the groin.  Then she should make an incision opposite the middle of the vulva at the root of the thigh, whichever side allows it and where she feels the stone.
Her finger should not leave the stone, pressing it downwards. The initial incision should be small, then the dilator should be inserted into that small incision. If she feels the stone, she should enlarge the incision as much as is needed for the stone to come out. Know that there are many kinds of stones.
Some are small, some large, smooth, rough, long, round, and branched.  Know their types so you can know what to do. If bleeding is excessive, sprinkle powdered verdigris on the area and leave it for an hour until the bleeding stops, then resume your work.
Until the stone comes out.  Prepare the instruments mentioned for removing stones from men, to assist you in your work. If bleeding is excessive and you know from the pulse that it is from a severed artery, apply styptics to the area.
And bind it tightly with bandages, leave it and do not return to it, and leave the stone and do not remove it, for the patient may die. Then treat the wound. If the severity of the bleeding subsides after a few days and the area becomes infected, return to your work until the stone comes out.
Chapter Sixty-Two.
On the incision on the water bladder.
Hydrocele is an accumulation of moisture in the tunica vaginalis, which is under the skin of the scrotum surrounding the testicle.  It may be in a special membrane prepared by nature in one part of the testicle.
So that one might think it is another testicle.  It is between the skin of the scrotum and the tunica vaginalis that we mentioned, but this only rarely occurs. This hydrocele is generated from weakness affecting the testicles, so that this substance collects there. It may also arise from a blow to the testicles.
This moisture is of many colours: it may be yellowish, or bloody red, or dark, or white and watery, which is most common.  The signs by which it is known are:
Where the water collects, if it is in the tunica vaginalis that we mentioned, then the swelling is somewhat elongated and round, like the shape of an egg, and the testicle does not appear because the moisture surrounds it on all sides.
If the moisture is in a special membrane, then the swelling is round on one side of the testicle.
Therefore, one imagines that it is another testicle. If the moisture is between the skin of the scrotum and the tunica vaginalis, then it is palpable. But if you wish to know the colour of the moisture, puncture the swelling with the square trocar whose image we have shown previously; then judge what is inside according to what comes out through the trocar.
If we proceed to treatment with cautery, you should order the patient to be bled if he is able and you see that his body is full. Then he should lie on his back on something slightly raised, with many cloths placed underneath him. Then you should sit on his left.
And you should order a servant to sit on his right, stretching his penis towards one side of the scrotum and towards the groin. Then you take a broad scalpel and incise the scrotum lengthwise in the middle, close to the pubis.
Make the incision straight, parallel to the line that divides the scrotum in half, until you reach the tunica vaginalis.  Then dissect it and be careful not to tear it. Your dissection should be more from the side attached to the testicle, and continue the dissection as much as you can.
Then widely evacuate the watery tunica vaginalis and remove all the water. Then separate the edges of the incision with hooks and stretch the tunica upwards, without touching the containing scrotal skin. Cut the tunica however you can cut it, either all at once or piece by piece.
Especially its thin side, for if you do not completely cut it, you cannot be sure that the water will not return. If the testicle protrudes outside its skin during your work, then, after you finish cutting the tunica, return it, then join the edges of the scrotal skin with sutures.
Then treat it like other wounds until it heals. If the testicle is already corrupted by another disease, you should ligate the vessels in the spermatic cord for fear of bleeding, then cut the testicle from the spermatic cord and remove the testicle, and treat it as we have mentioned.
If the fluid is collected on both sides, know that there are two hydroceles. Incise the other side as you did the first, or if it is easier to do it all at once, do so.  Then wipe away the blood.
And insert into the incisions wool that you have soaked in oil or rose ointment, and on the outside place more wool that you have soaked in wine and oil, and spread it over the testicles and the abdomen, and place folded cloths, which are the pledgets, on top. And tie it on top with a bandage which has six ends, in this manner:
Then two cloths are taken, sewn together, and stuffed with fluffed wool in this way.  Make the ends that bind it from wet cotton or wool, and gather them tightly in the waistband of the trousers; one end is taken to the buttocks towards the back.
The second is taken towards the pubis, and the two ends to the thighs below, and the remaining two ends to the groins, and all the binding is gathered in the waistband of the trousers. This hydrocele can also be treated by cauterization instead of incision with an iron.
This is done by taking a smooth, knife-like cautery and incising the skin of the scrotum with it while it is hot, as we have described, until the white peritoneum containing the water is exposed. Then take another cautery of this shape:
It resembles the Greek eye.  Then sear that peritoneum with it while hot until all the moisture is expelled. Then stretch the peritoneum with hooks and flay the containing layer with the cautery and cut it as much as you can until you excise it all, taking care that the testicle is not touched by the fire.
Know that this procedure is safer and prevents more bleeding, and is better than the procedure with incision. If during the incision or cauterization you encounter a hot swelling, bleeding, or something else, then you should treat whatever arises according to the appropriate treatment as described before.
If the patient is cowardly and cannot endure this procedure because of its ugliness, then use cauterization as I describe, if it is confirmed by the evidence presented earlier.
If the hydrocele is watery, then the patient should sit on a high chair, then sear the lower part of the swelling with a wide scalpel, making the cauterization along the length of the body. The opening should be wide so that all the water flows out.  Then place cotton on the area and bind it.
Leave it to drain the remaining water, then treat it until the wound heals. If the water does not flow out during cauterization, it is because the tunica albuginea obstructs the opening of the wound and prevents the water from flowing out.
Then you should insert a goose feather or the instrument for extracting the water of hydroceles, the image of which has been shown previously, or slightly enlarge the opening of the wound.
Know that the water may return and accumulate after six months or so. If it accumulates, cauterize it in the same manner, and the patient should protect himself during those days.
Chapter Sixty-Three.
On the incision on the fleshy membrane and its treatment.
Know that an incision on this membrane is one of the dangerous things leading to death in most instances; therefore, we advise leaving it alone and being safe from it. I will mention the treatment and the types of swelling.
I say that many swellings may occur in the bodies which are composed of the testicles, and this is due to many causes, either from a sharp humour which descends to the testicles, or from a blow. The color of the swelling is the same as the color of the body and it does not contain water.
And the swelling may be hard, and perhaps the swelling may be hardened, its color being like lead and without feeling.  It may be from the complication and swelling of the arteries, as has been mentioned before, or from swelling of the veins. That which is from the swelling of the arteries is known by the spreading of the swelling.
If you press it with your fingers, it does not spread.  It should not be incised at all. As for that which is from the swelling of the veins, nothing of it spreads when pressed with the fingers. This type can be incised, as well as the other flesh.
And that is to incise the skin of the testicle, then extend the testicle upwards and bring it out from the white peritoneum, and free the spermatic cord from the vessels, and tie the vessels, and cut the spermatic cord after freeing it from every side of the testicle; if the testicle has adhered to that growing flesh.
Then it is necessary to remove the testicle and cut it. And if the adhesion is between some of the peritonea or between the vessels, then it is necessary to free all that adhesion and cut it with a circular cut.
If the growth of flesh is in the place of adhesion which is from behind, then it is necessary to cut all of it and remove the testicle as we said. When your work is completed, dress the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and syrup, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
Chapter sixty-four.
In the treatment of hydrocele with a hernia.
A hernia is a twisted tumor, somewhat twisted, resembling a bunch of grapes, with relaxation of the spermatic cords and difficulty for the patient in movement, exercise, and walking.  The treatment of this condition is similar to what has been mentioned previously.
However, it is necessary to mention the procedure as the ancients did. I say that it is necessary that the patient sit on a high chair, then push the spermatic cord downwards, then grasp the skin of the testicle with your fingers along with the vessels which are near the penis.
And let your assistant hold it and stretch it tightly. Then incise with a wide, sharp scalpel, making an oblique incision along the vessels until the vessels are exposed. Then dissect from each side as I described to you in the dissection of the arteries in the armpits, then insert a needle with a doubled thread.
And tie it at the first point where the hernia appeared and tie it also at its end, then incise it in the middle with a vertical incision along the body and remove what has collected in it of the foul, putrid fluids.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds that you want to heal, to allow the vessels that nourish one of the spermatic cords to fall away, there is no harm in that. If the hernia affects all the vessels, then it is necessary to remove one of the spermatic cords with the vessels, lest the testicle be deprived of nourishment. before cutting the vessels, so that they wither and are of no use.
Chapter sixty-five.
On the treatment of intestinal hernia.
This hernia occurs from a fissure that appears in the peritoneum extending over the abdomen towards the groins from the abdominal cavity, so the intestine descends from that rupture into one of the spermatic cords.
And this rupture is either from a fissure in the peritoneum or from its extension, and these two types occur from many causes, either a blow, a jump, a shout, or lifting something heavy, and so on.  Its sign, if it is from an extension of the peritoneum, is that it occurs little by little over a long time.
It does not occur suddenly, and the swelling is even towards the depth because the peritoneum constricts the intestine. Its sign, if it is from a fissure in the peritoneum, is that from its beginning it causes great pain suddenly, and the swelling is visibly different under the skin nearby.
That is by the exit of the intestine and its passage outside of the peritoneum.  The omentum may also exit with the intestine; this hernia is then called intestinal and omental. It may be with wind, and feces may flow into the intestine and be retained there, resulting in the death of the patient.
Because it causes severe pain and gurgling, especially if constricted. Treatment of this type of ailment with cautery is dangerous, so one should be wary of resorting to it. The method of treatment is to instruct the patient to return the intestine into his abdomen by hand if it is possible for it to return.
Then he lies on his back in your hands and raises his legs. Then you stretch the skin next to the groin upwards and incise all the skin of the scrotum lengthwise. Then you insert hooks into the edges of the incision to the extent needed to open the incision with them.
The incision should be large enough to allow the testicle to emerge. Then you dissect the membranes under the skin of the scrotum until the white, hard peritoneum is exposed on all sides. Then you insert your index finger next to the testicle.
Between the white peritoneum under the skin of the testicle and the second peritoneum, and you separate the adhesions behind the testicle. Then you draw it inward with your right hand into the scrotum's skin, while simultaneously stretching the white peritoneum upward with your left hand and lifting the testicle with the peritoneum toward the incision.
You instruct the assistant to pull the testicle upward, and you completely separate the adhesions from behind. You explore with your finger lest there be any twisted intestine in the hard white peritoneum; if you find any, push it down into the abdomen.
Then you take a needle with thick thread twisted from ten threads and insert it at the end of the peritoneum under the scrotum's skin next to the incision. Then you cut the ends of the thread's loop so that there are four threads. Then you place some of them on top of each other in a firm manner and tie the peritoneumwhich we said is under the scrotum's skintightly on two sides.  Then you also twist the ends of the threads and tie them tightly so that nothing from the vessels that nourish it can reach it. Lest a hot swelling result from that, and make a second ligature outside the first ligature, less than two fingers away from it. After these two ligatures, leave the amount of a finger's bone width of the peritoneum under the scrotum's skin.
Lest a hot swelling result from that, and make a second ligature outside the first ligature, less than two fingers away from it. After these two ligatures, leave the amount of a finger's bone width of the peritoneum under the scrotum's skin. And cut all the rest in a circular manner and remove the testicle with it. Then incise the lower part of the scrotum's skin, making an incision from which blood and pus flow, as we described previously. Then use wool soaked in oil and place it in the wound, and use the ligature which we described.
And the white peritoneum, which we mentioned after cutting it, may be cauterized to guard against bleeding, for that often occurs.  And leave the ligature until it falls off by itself. If it is slow to fall off, then loosen it with warm water and apply something that causes it to sweat until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with all the treatments for wounds until it heals.
Chapter sixty-six.
Concerning the windy hernia.  This windy herniaI have not seen anyone dare to treat it with iron. The ancients mentioned that it should be done as we described in the hernia with a sac, that is, that the vessels should be gently ligated below after the incision.
Then incise in the middle and treat the tumor with what opens it until the vessels fall away, and treat the wound as we mentioned until it heals.
Chapter sixty-seven.
Concerning the hernia which is in the groin.
A hernia may appear in the groin, as we said, so that the place swells but nothing of the intestine descends into the scrotum; and if it descends, it is slight and returns at all times.
But if time passes, the fissure in the peritoneum increases until the intestine and epiploon descend into the scrotum; and this happens from the extension of the peritoneum which is in the groin, as we said. That is because the peritoneum extends, then relaxes and the place swells and protrudes, and its treatment is by cauterization, as I have previously described.
It may also be treated with an iron instrument in this manner: the patient lies on his back between your hands, then you incise the swollen, protruding hernia transversely to the extent of three fingers, then you loosen the peritoneal membranes which are under the skin.
Until, when the white peritoneum which follows is uncovered, you take a suture and place it on the protruding part of the peritoneum and press it into the depth of the abdomen, then you sew the two protruding parts together at the end of the suture of the peritoneum, and you join one to the other with the suture.
Then you pull the end of the suture and do not cut the peritoneum at all, nor touch the testicle or anything else, as I have taught you in the treatment of intestinal hernia, then you treat it with the treatment of wounds.
If the sutures break, I clean them and dress the wound until it heals, for the peritoneum contracts and the protrusion of the hernia does not increase.
Cauterization is better in this location because it is closer to safety.
Chapter sixty-eight.
On the relaxation of the skin of the scrotum.
The skin of the scrotum often relaxes in some people without the flesh inside relaxing, and its appearance is unsightly.  Whoever wishes to treat it should lay the patient on his back.
And he cuts all the relaxed skin over the thick skin, then brings the lips together. And if you wish, first sew the relaxed excess skin with three or four stitches, ensuring the stitching is secure, then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it like any other wound until it heals and the stitches fall out.
Chapter sixty-nine.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
Therefore, I should not mention it in this book. However, I have mentioned it for two reasons:  one, so that the physician may know about it if asked and know how to treat someone who has undergone it; and the other, because we often need to castrate some animals for our benefit.
Such as lambs, goats, cats, and similar animals.  I say that castration is of two types: either by crushing or by incision and cutting.  That which is by crushing, its method is that we place the animal in hot water until its testicles relax, soften, and hang down.
Then crush them with your hand until they dissolve and are not perceptible to the touch. As for castration by incision and cutting, you should hold the animal and squeeze the skin of its testicles with your left hand, then tie the suspensory ligaments and make a single incision on each testicle.
So that when the testicles emerge, cut them after you have peeled them and do not leave on them any of the membranes except the thin membrane that is on the vessels. This type of castration is better than that which is by crushing.
Because crushing may leave something of the testicles, so the animal may desire mating. Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
In the treatment of the hermaphrodite.
The hermaphrodite in men is of two types: one is that which appears in the area behind the scrotum or in the skin of the scrotum between the testicles, a shape like a woman's vulva, with hair, and urine may flow from that which is in the skin of the scrotum.
As for women, there is one type, and it is above the vulva on the pubis like small male testicles, protruding outwards; one is like a man's penis, and the two are like testicles. And the treatment of the three types.
The two types in men and the one type in women: those superfluous tissues should be cut away until their trace is gone, then treated with the treatment of other wounds until it heals. As for the second type in men, from which urine flows, which is in the skin of the scrotum, there is no action to be taken, nor is there any benefit in seeing the testicles.
Chapter seventy-one.
On the excision of the clitoris and the protruding flesh from the vulvas of women.
The clitoris may exceed the natural amount, so that it becomes large and ugly in appearance, and in some women it may grow large until it resembles that of men and becomes suitable for intercourse.
You should grasp the excess of the clitoris with your hand or with forceps and cut it off, and do not be excessive in cutting, especially in the depth of the root, lest bleeding occur.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds until it heals. As for the growing flesh, it is flesh that grows in the mouth of the womb until it fills it.
And sometimes it protrudes outwards like a tail. Therefore, some of the ancients call it the "tail" disease.  You should cut it off in the same way as you cut off the clitoris and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Two.
In the treatment of prolapse.
Prolapse is when a woman's vagina is not perforated, or the perforation is small. This can be either congenital or acquired.  The acquired type results from a previously existing condition, and it may be due to excessive growth of flesh.
or a thin or thick membrane. This may be located in the depth of the uterus, or in its sides, or above or below it. It prevents intercourse, conception and childbirth, and may also prevent menstruation.  This is diagnosed by a midwife's examination.
If the obstruction is visible and near, then if it is not visible, she should examine it with her fingers or a probe. If the obstruction is a thin membrane and is close to the labia, then proceed to pierce it.  This is done by placing something like pledgets on the labia, then placing both thumbs together.
The woman lies on her back with her legs spread. Then the labia are strongly stretched until the thin membrane is pierced and the obstruction is opened. Then take wool, soak it in oil, and apply it to the area. The woman should have intercourse every day so that the area does not close again.
If the membrane is thick and dense, it should be incised with a wide scalpel resembling an Asian leaf. If the obstruction is due to excessive flesh growth, then hang it up with hooks and cut it.  You should have styptic medicines at hand which do not sting, such as kaolin, acacia, and frankincense, mixed with egg white.
Then use a wide lead tube so that the wound does not close quickly. Keep it in place for several days, and use a dry linen wick, then treat it with other healing methods until it heals.  Other flesh growths may occur in the uterus; these should be cut in the same way.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor. A cancerous tumor in the uterus should never be treated with iron instruments.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red sores that appear in the vulva of women.
As for hemorrhoids, they are a swelling of the mouths of the veins, so that much blood constantly flows from them. If hemorrhoids persist, they become warts.  Their types and symptoms were mentioned in the classification, and we will mention them here.
What accepts treatment and what does not accept treatment. I say that hemorrhoids and warts, if they are deep within the uterus and do not appear to the touch, have no treatment with iron instruments.  Those that are at the mouth of the uterus and can be felt are the ones that are treated.
Then the woman should enter a cool place, then stretch the warts with a scraper or a rough cloth, and cut them at their base. Then apply to them, when the blood flows, one of the styptics that stop blood without causing ulceration.
Such as acacia, alchemilla, frankincense, and the like. Then raise her legs to the wall for an hour, then lower them into cold water. If the bleeding continues, seat her in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate peels, oak galls and the like. Then take barley flour, knead it with honey and vinegar.
And apply it as a compress to her back. If the bleeding stops, otherwise, apply cupping glasses to the loins and breasts without restriction. Then take wool, dip it in the juice of the plantain, or the juice of verbascum, or the juice of willow branches with oak gall decoction, and apply it to the area.
If the swelling subsides, treat the area with ointment until it heals. As for the red pimples, they resemble the heads of millet, rough in appearance.  It is necessary to cut off what appears of them, as I have described for warts, and treat them until they heal.
Chapter Seventy-Four.
On the suppuration which occurs in the uterus.
Many types of tumors may occur in the uterus, such as cancer, hard tumors, ulcers, fistulas, phagedena,  fistulas, hemorrhoids, fissures, warts, and hot tumors. I have mentioned all these diseases, their types and signs in the classification.
It is necessary to mention in this chapter the hot tumor which occurs in the uterus, if it is one of the tumors which collect for a time, how its suppuration is done with iron.  You should look, for if the pain of the tumor is initially sharp, with throbbing, inflammation and fever,
and you see it red when touched, do not hasten to its suppuration, and treat it with what aids in maturation, until these symptoms subside. Then you should make the woman sit on a chair with two seats and lean on its back.
and bring her legs together towards the lower abdomen, her thighs being apart, and her arms under her knees, and bind them with a suitable bandage.
Then the midwife sits on the right side and uses the instrument with which the mouth of the uterus is opened, the image of which comes later. If you wish to use this instrument, you should estimate with a probe the depth of the woman's uterus, lest that which enters from the instrument be more than the depth of the uterus.
This harms the patient. If the instrument is longer than the depth, then pledgets should be placed on the lip of the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the depth of the uterus, and the spiral which runs in the instrument should be on the upper side.
And you hold the instrument, and the attendant turns the spiral until the cervix is opened.  When the abscess appears and is touched by hand and is soft and thin, then you should incise it at its head with a wide scalpel. When all the matter has been evacuated,
a soft tent soaked in rose oil or green oil containing some astringent should be placed in the wound. The tent should come out from the incision in the cervix, and clean wool soaked in water in which alum has been boiled should be placed externally on the uterus and on the pubis.
Then treat it after two days with the ointment which is suitable for that until it heals.
And the uterus and wound may be washed by injecting with honey and water, then with water in which licorice root or long zedoary has been boiled, then return to the ointment, if the abscess is hidden inside the uterus.
Then you should refrain from treating it with iron, but it should be treated with what we mentioned in the classification.
Chapter seventy-five.
In teaching midwives how to treat living fetuses if they emerge in an unnatural manner.
The midwife should first know the form of natural childbirth.  Among its signs is if you see the woman straining downwards and yearning to breathe the air, and what she suffers from in childbirth becomes easier for her.
And the expulsion of the fetus is quickened. Know that this childbirth is in the natural manner, and its expulsion is head-first, with the placenta attached to it or hanging from its navel. If you see these signs, then you should press her abdomen so that the fetus comes out quickly.
For if it descends head-first, the placenta descends with it and is cleansed of those impurities in a good way. Whatever is other than this childbirth is unnatural and blameworthy, for the fetus may emerge feet-first, or its hands and feet may emerge before its head.
Or one hand or one foot, or its head and hand may emerge, or it may emerge folded, and perhaps it may be turned on its back, and similar blameworthy forms.  The midwife should therefore be skillful and gentle with all these things, and beware of slips and mistakes.
And I am explaining each form and how to deal with it, so that you may understand and deal with it. If the fetus emerges head-first  the natural emergence  and this is difficult for the woman and the labor pains are severe for her, and you see that her strength has weakened, then seat her on a chair and instruct women to support her.
And pack her womb with cooked fenugreek and moist ointments, then the midwife inserts a small scalpel between her fingers and cuts the placenta with it, or cuts it with her fingernail, until the moisture within flows out, and she presses the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then she should inject the woman with fenugreek saliva mixed with shirj ointment, then after the injection she should instruct her to cough and sneeze her with a kanda, and hold her mouth and nose, for the fetus will emerge immediately. If the hands of the fetus emerge first, then they should be gently returned little by little.
If they do not readily enter, place the woman on a raised platform and raise her legs upwards, then shake the platform on the ground while the woman holds on so that she does not fall when it is shaken. If the hands do not enter and the fetus is dead, cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them moderately, then he will emerge.  The emergence of the fetus with its feet: If the fetus emerges with its feet, then they should be pushed upwards, then the fetus should be turned little by little until it is straightened, then take one of its feet and extend it little by little.
If they emerge to the thighs, instruct her to cough and sneeze her with the kanda, then he will emerge. If he does not emerge as we described, otherwise return the fetus little by little until you make it into its natural form, then it will emerge smoothly. If it is prevented by all that we described,
Take some saliva of the Khatmi plant and saliva of fenugreek and shirj ointment and dissolved gum arabic, and grind everything well in a mortar, then apply it to the woman's vulva and lower abdomen, then seat her in lukewarm water until it reaches the sharasif.
If you see that her lower part has softened, make a pessary for her from myrrh and she will tolerate it. If the pessary stays for an hour, seat her on a chair, then make her sneeze, close her mouth and nose, and gently squeeze her lower abdomen, then the fetus will emerge immediately. The emergence of the fetus on its knees and hands:
If it emerges in this manner, gradually insert its hands, then the woman lies on her back and hangs her hips downwards while lying on a bed and clasps her hands, then gently squeeze above her abdomen. Then remove the pillows from under her legs so that her lower part is raised. If the fetus does not emerge, take both her legs and move them vigorously, then gently squeeze above the sharasif until the fetus ascends upwards, then the midwife inserts her hand and straightens the fetus little by little.
And order the woman to strain until the fetus emerges. The emergence of the fetus sideways, hanging by one of its hands.  Gently try to return its hand; if you are unable to return it, raise the woman and make her walk; if she is unable to walk, let her lie down.
