on her bed and shake her legs vigorously; then use the poultice which I described from the saliva. If labor pains seize her, seat her on a chair, then work at returning the hand and straightening the fetus into its natural form, and make the woman sneeze and order her to strain until it emerges.
The emergence of the fetus on its back, extending its hands, and its face towards its mother's back. The midwife should grasp the hand of the fetus, then turn it little by little and gently straighten it. Once straightened, apply to its anterior what we described of the ointment and poultices.
Then tell her to move little by little. If she does that, seat her on the chair and tell her to lean to the right side, then tell her to strain and make her sneeze with the kands [sneezing powder]; then it will emerge. The emergence of the fetus erect on its side.
If it descends to the mouth of the womb in this manner with the placenta, split the placenta with your fingernail until it is emptied of moisture. If it adheres to the face and neck of the fetus, cut it from the navel lest it suffocate and die; then push the fetus inwards until it is as it should be.
And tell the woman to strain repeatedly; then it will emerge easily. The emergence of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and three or four may be born and live, although that is rare; as for five, it is something outside of nature and they do not live at all.
And they are surrounded, even if numerous, by one placenta, and they are separated by a membranous partition between them, attached to the navel of each one.  They may emerge like a single fetus, naturally and easily, or they may emerge in a difficult manner as we described.
If some of them are difficult at birth and become as we have described in the unnatural forms, treat them as we described. And let the midwife be gentle and do what she does with gentleness and slowness, and devise for each form what means are available to her that lead to safety.
Mention of what is conceived in the womb of fetuses that are aborted. One, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten may be conceived in the womb. It has been confirmed to me that a woman aborted seven, and another fifteen, all of them conceived.
By the estimation of the All-Mighty, the All-Knowing. All of this should be in the knowledge of the midwife, for perhaps such a thing may occur to her someday, so she may confront it appropriately.
Chapter seventy-six.
In the extraction of the dead fetus.
If you treat the dead fetus with what I mentioned in the section on treatment and it does not come out, and you are forced to use your hand, then you should look. If the woman is strong and healthy and has none of the symptoms that one fears may cause her harm, then the woman should lie on her back on a bed.
And her head should be inclined downward and her legs raised, and a servant should support her from both sides, or she should be tied to the bed, lest her body be pulled when the fetus is extracted.
Then moisten her vulva with moist ointments along with the saliva of the mallow and fenugreek and flaxseed. Then the midwife should anoint her hand with these ointments and saliva, then gently insert it into the womb, then seek with it a place to insert the hooks into the fetus, and the suitable places for that.
See, if the fetus is positioned to descend headfirst, then insert the hooks into its eyes or the nape of its neck or its mouth or its palate or under its chin or collarbone or in the areas near the ribs or under the clavicles.
But if it descends feet first, then [insert the hooks] in the groin and the middle ribs.  It is necessary to hold the hook with the right hand and place its bend between the fingers of the left hand, and gently insert the hand and insert a hook into some of those places we mentioned.
Until you reach something empty, then insert another hook opposite it, or a third if needed, so that the traction is even and does not lean to one side. Then pull with a straight pull, and the pull should not be straight only, but should be moved to every side.
So that its exit is facilitated, as is done with a tooth when it is extracted.  It is necessary during this to relax the pull; if it is obstructed on one side, then the midwife should anoint some fingers with ointment and insert them from that side to rotate what is obstructed. If some of the fetus emerges, then move the hooks to other places.
It is slightly elevated. Do this until the entire fetus emerges. If a hand emerges before anything else and cannot be returned because of its compression, then it is necessary to wrap a cloth around it and then pull it until, when it is completely out, you cut it.
Either at the joint of the wrist or at the elbow.  Thus it is necessary to do in the other hand and in the feet. If the head of the fetus is large and experiences pressure in its exit, or if there is collected water in its head, then it is necessary to insert a hooked scalpel between the fingers.
And split the head with it so that the water emerges, or extract it with the instrument called the *mishdaakh*, whose image comes with the images of the other instruments in the chapter which follows this.
Similarly, do this if the fetus is naturally large-headed. Then it is necessary to split the skull or crush it with the *mishdaakh* as we said, then extract the bones with hooks. If the head emerges and is compressed at the clavicle, then let it be split so that the moisture in the chest is poured out.
For then the chest will be constricted. If the chest is not constricted and its removal is not possible, cut it as much as you can. If the lower abdomen is swollen or if there is a swelling in it, then it is necessary to incise it so that the moisture flows from it.
If the fetus is on its feet, then pulling it is easy and straightening it to the mouth of the womb is simple.  If it is compressed at the abdomen or chest, then it is necessary to pull it with a cloth which you have wrapped around your hand, and split the abdomen or chest until what is in them flows out.
If all the limbs are extracted and the head is retracted and retained, let the midwife insert her left hand. If the mouth of the womb is open, let her insert her hand into the neck of the womb and seek the head with it and pull it with her fingers to the mouth of the womb, then insert a hook or two hooks into it and pull it with them.
If the mouth of the womb has constricted due to a hot swelling affecting it, then it should not be forced.  Instead, it is necessary then to use pouring of moist, fatty things, and to use compresses, and to sit in waters which relax and moisten.
And if the fetus is on its side, if it is possible to straighten it, then let what we mentioned for the living fetus be used. If this is not possible, then let it be cut and extracted.  It is necessary that nothing of the placenta be left inside at all.  When you have finished your treatment, use what we mentioned of the treatment for the hot swellings which appear in the wombs.
If bleeding occurs, let her descend into astringent waters and use all other treatments. I have witnessed a woman who had conceived, and the fetus died in her womb, then she conceived again upon it.
Then the other fetus also died.  After a long time, a swelling appeared in her womb, and it swelled until it opened and began to discharge pus. I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time, but the wound did not close, so I placed on it some strong, drawing ointments.
Then a bone came out of the place. Then days passed, and another bone came out. I was amazed by this, since the abdomen is a place where there is no bone. I considered that it was from the bones of the dead fetus, so I examined the wound and extracted from it many bones from the bones of the head of the fetus.
I continued doing this until I extracted many bones from her, and the woman was in the best of health.  She lived like that for a time, discharging a little pus from the place. And I have only related this unusual case here.
because in it there is knowledge and aid for what the skillful physician attempts with his hand in treatment.
Chapter seventy-seven.
In it are the images of the instruments which are needed in the extraction of the fetus.
Image of a screw to open the mouth of the womb.
This is the binder with which books are bound; it has two screws at the ends of the two pieces of wood, except that this screw should be finer than the binder and should be either of ebony or of boxwood, and the width of each piece of wood should be the thickness of two fingers.
And their width is about a finger and their length is a span and a half, and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two guides of the same kind of wood which have been fastened in them; their length is half a span or a little more, and their width is about two fingers or a little more, and these two guides
are those which enter into the mouth of the womb to open it by turning the screws; an image of another instrument for the same purpose, finer and lighter:
It is made of ebony or boxwood in the shape of hooks, except that it has two guides at its end, as you see; the length of each guide is about a span, and their width is two fingers.  If you wish to open the womb with it,
then seat the woman on a bed with her legs hanging down and spread apart between her thighs, then insert these two guides, close together, into the mouth of the womb, while you hold the end of the instrument below between her thighs, then open your hand with the instrument as you do with hooks, as much as you want to open the womb. until the midwife does what she wants. An image of another screw mentioned by the ancients:
An image of the instrument which pushes the fetus:
Image of the forceps with which the head of the fetus is grasped.
It resembles scissors, with teeth at the end as you see.  It may also be made rectangular like hooks, in this image as you see, it has teeth like saw teeth to cut and tear with.
Image of an instrument also.
Image of a hook.
This hook should be somewhat thick so that it does not break when pulling the fetus with it. Image of a hook with two points.
Image of two wide scalpels for cutting the fetus.
All these instruments, the more numerous their types and the better prepared they are by the craftsman, the quicker his work and the higher his standing among people. Therefore, do not despise any instrument that you have prepared, for there will certainly be a need for it.
Chapter seventy-eight.
On removing the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, you should instruct the patient to hold herself, then sneeze using a  (sneezing) powder and press your hand on her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out this way, well and good; otherwise, use a [forceps].
And pierce a hole in the lid and place in it herbs that open the mouth of the womb, such as rue, henbane, dill, chamomile, wormwood,  [a plant] and centaury; all these medicaments or some of them. Submerge them in water and place them over the fire, then place a silver tube on the hole in the lid of the pot and the other end in the mouth of the womb.
And hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the womb, then cause her to sneeze as we said; then the placenta will come out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to dip her left hand in  [a type of oil] or mallow sap.
Then insert it into the vagina and search for the placenta.  If she finds it, she should grasp it and gently pull it little by little until she removes it. If it is attached deep in the womb, insert the hand as we described, until, when she finds the placenta, she pulls it little by little, being careful.
Lest the womb fall out with strong pulling, but it should be moved gently to the sides, right and left, then the amount of pulling should be increased; then it will yield and free itself from the adhesion. If the mouth of the womb is constricted, we have described the treatment with sneezing and the treatment with the pot and herbs.
If it does not come out with all that we have described, avoid violence in pulling it again, but it should be tiedwhat has come out of it to the mouth of the wombto the woman's thigh, then inject it with the four-component ointment, for it will cause it to rot after a few days.
And it will dissolve and come out, except that if it becomes putrid, a foul odor will ascend from it to the stomach and head, and this will harm the patient.  Therefore, suitable fumigations should be used.
Therefore, and some of the ancients have tried the fumigation of rue and dried figs.  The image of the instrument with which the woman is fumigated when menstruation and the placenta are retained, and the like:
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or it is made of copper; the thin end is placed in the vagina, and the wide end over the fire, and the incense is placed on the embers; it is held until that incense is gone, and another is added.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
In the treatment of the non-perforated fistula.
Many boys may be born with their rectums imperforate; a thin membrane may have closed it.  It is necessary for the midwife to perforate that membrane with her finger; otherwise, let her incise it with a sharp scalpel, and let her beware of the muscle, not to touch it.
Then she places on it wool soaked in syrup and oil, then treats it with ointments until it heals. If she fears that it will close up, let her place a lead tube in the opening for many days, removing it whenever the child wishes to defecate.
The occlusion of the rectum may also occur from the healing of a wound or a tumor; it is necessary to incise that closure, then treat it with the treatment and the lead tube we mentioned.
Chapter Eighty.
In the treatment of fistulas which occur in the lower parts.
Fistulas which occur in the lower parts are a complication and thickening which occurs near the rectum, externally or internally in the space from one of the sides, and the fistula may be one or more.
If this complication becomes chronic, it opens and a watery white moisture or thin pus flows from it.  Some of these fistulas may penetrate to the bladder and to the intestine, and some may not penetrate.  Those which penetrate may be recognized by what comes out of them of feces and wind when the patient uses the privy.
And perhaps worms come out of them.  And there may be fistulas among them, if they are in the space, penetrating to the bladder and to the urethra. And there may be some penetrating to the hip joint and to the sacrum.  And what distinguishes the fistula which penetrates to the rectum from that which does not penetrate is also known.
That you insert your index finger into the rectum and insert a thin probe into the fistula, of copper or iron, if the fistula is not tortuous. But if it is tortuous, then insert into it a thin lead probe or a horsehair.
Until you feel the probe or the hair with your finger. If you do not feel it at all, and nothing of feces, nor wind, nor worms, as we said, emerges from the opening, then know that it is not penetrating, so proceed to treatment.
And if the fistula penetrates to the bladder or to the urethra, its sign is the emergence of urine from it and the inability of the place to heal with medications.  And as for if it penetrates to the hip joint or to the sacrum, its sign is the arrival of the probe there.
If it is not tortuous to the bone, and there is pain in that direction, and pus constantly comes out of it, and no treatment works, and it does not heal with ointmentthese penetrating fistulas, all of themthere is absolutely no cure for them.
And their treatment is futile effort for those who attempt it among ignorant physicians. As for those that are not penetrating and not chronic, recovery is hoped for with cautery, as I describe and as I have tried.  It is that you lay the patient on his back between your hands.
And raise his legs upwards, and tilt his thighs towards his abdomen, then insert the copper or lead probe if there is tortuosity in the fistula until you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it towards the rectum, then you should insert your index finger into the rectum.
If you feel with your finger that the probe has penetrated by itself, uncovered, without feeling peritoneum or flesh between your finger and it, then know for certain that it is penetrating, so do not exert yourself on it, for there is no cure for it, as we said.
And they have said that it heals in some people rarely. And of the treatments from which benefit is hoped for is that you heat a thin cautery iron according to the width of the fistula, as has preceded, and insert it hot into the fistula until it reaches towards the rectum, then repeat it two or three times.
Until you know that all that excess, matted flesh resembling the tube of a bird's feather has been burned. Then treat it with lint soaked in fat until the burned flesh comes out, then treat it with healing ointments, and if it heals.
Otherwise, it will never heal with any other treatment. But if you insert the probe and it does not penetrate to your finger which is in the rectum, and there was between it and the probe a thick partition of flesh or peritoneum, and you saw the fistula near the surface of the skin, then incise the skin at the beginning of the fistula. And you pass with the incision with the probe, while it is in the fistula, until you reach with the incision where the end of the probe has reached, and the probe is freed and falls out. Then you cleanse that matted flesh resembling the tube of a bird's feather and leave nothing of it.
Then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If the bleeding overcomes you and prevents you from cutting that flesh, the best and quickest remedy is cauterization with fire or a caustic medicine, because cauterization with fire combines two good things: burning that excess flesh.
And cutting the blood and drying the moisture. Then treat it with lint soaked in fat or sulfur powdered with oil until the place suppurates and that burned flesh comes out in the pus. Then treat the wound with healing ointments that grow solid flesh.
These are medicines which constrict and dry, for if it is closed, it has healed and there is no fear of recurrence. If the fistula has reached the depth of the rectum and is distant from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the rectum and explore with it.
If you feel the probe and between you and it a partition of peritoneum or flesh, and it was near to penetration, then the action in it is only by way of hope, that is, that you use in it one of three methods: either cauterize it as we said.
Or incise it until you reach its bottom so that you can easily insert the lint and treat it from nearby. Do not extend the incision lest you cut the muscle surrounding the rectum and cause the patient involuntary bowel movements.  Then treat it as we mentioned, and perhaps it will heal as we said.
Or penetrate that partition and pierce it after you incise it near the rectum as we said, either with a probe or with another sharp-pointed instrument. Then cleanse all the adherent flesh in the incision as far as you can, completely.
Then close the whole wound with the incision and the upper opening until it is sealed, and the fistula remains open inside the rectum, which is easier for the patient.  The fistula may be perforated in this manner.
And if you insert the probe into the fistula and it is in a side of the rectum towards the surface of the body with the skin and the ring of the rectum, then take a probe with a perforated end like a shoemaker's needle in this manner:
And insert into it a twisted thread of five or so threads, then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its bottom. If it is penetrating into the margin of the rectum from inside nearby.
Then bring the thread out through that hole by inserting your finger into the rectum and bringing out the end of the thread, and bring both ends together and tighten them and leave it for a day or two.  Whenever the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well until that flesh between the ends of the threads is severed and falls out, then treat the wound until it heals and recovers.
If the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, penetrate it however you can, except that it should not be very deep, for you should not do that on account of the muscle lest you cut it. Then do with it as we have mentioned until it heals.
The image of the hooked bistoury with which fistulas are incised: its hook is very sharp and the other side is not sharp, lest you cut what is not needed, as you see.
Chapter eighty-one.
Concerning the opening of hemorrhoids from which blood flows, and their excision, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two kinds: either they are inside the rectum, resembling red bladders or like grapes, and there are small and large ones among them, and blood constantly flows from them.
And they may be one or many, and they may be outside the rectum and at its edges, except that those which are outside the rectum are, in most cases, less moist, with a constant flow of yellow water or a little blood, and they are the color of the flesh.
The treatment for those which are inside the rectum is that you should order the patient to strain and bear down until the rectum is protruded and the growths appear to you.  Then quickly grasp them with hooks or seize them with your fingernail, then cut them at their base. If the hooks do not hold because of their moisture and laxity,
then rub them with a rough cloth or a piece of a tanned hide, or pull them with your fingers, then cut them. Then, after cutting, sprinkle on them some sharp powders to act as a cautery, or cauterize them as described in the chapter on cautery.
Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals. If the rectum does not protrude, inject the patient with an enema containing a little irritant to wash what is in the rectum and cause it to quickly protrude when the patient strains. As for the growths outside the rectum, their treatment is easy and simple.
This is to seize them with your fingernail or hook them with a hook and cut them, then treat them as we have mentioned until they heal.  And whoever dislikes cutting with iron should use a ligature in this manner: take a twisted thread and insert it into a needle.
Then pull the growth upwards and pass the needle through its base from the other side, and twist the ends of the thread below the needle while it is crosswise and pull the growth tightly. Then tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this to all the growths, leaving one unligated.
so that the excess blood flows from it. Then place a cloth soaked in rose oil or kyroton on the rectum, and tell the patient to remain still. Then leave it until it falls off.  If the growths fall off, treat them with ointments and other treatments until they heal.
As for the treatment of fissures, they often result from dryness of the feces and retention of the temperament. If they become chronic and medicine does not work, then it should be scraped with a scalpel blade or your fingernail until it becomes moist and the upper crust that prevents it from healing is removed.
Then treat it until it heals properly. If it does not heal, repeat the scraping more severely than before until it bleeds well and peels, then treat it, for it will heal.
Chapter Eighty-Two.
On the treatment of ingrown and non-ingrown nails, dry warts, and nits.
A corn is a round nodule, the color of the body, resembling the head of a nail. It occurs on the whole body, but especially on the soles of the feet and the toes, and causes pain when walking.
It should be incised around the corn, grasped with a hook or forceps, and pulled out by the root, then treated.  If you wish, cauterize it as explained previously in its chapter. As for dry warts, each one is a small, rough, matted, round protrusion on the surface of the body.
They often appear on the hands of children.  Its treatment is to stretch the wart and cut it, or pierce it with a silk thread or hair until it falls off. If you wish, cauterize it with fire or a caustic medicine, as previously explained.
Ingrown and non-ingrown warts may be removed with the instrument I have mentioned in the treatment of nits. As for nits, they are also a small, matted, thick protrusion on the surface of the body, extending deeply.  If cold affects them, a sensation similar to the sting of ants is felt.
They occur all over the body, but most often on the hands.  Its treatment is to take a tube made of a strong goose or eagle feather, and place this tube over the nit so that the circumference of the tube surrounds it on all sides. Then rotate your hand with the tube until you incise around the nit.
Then insert it into the depth of the flesh, and the nit will be pulled out by its root. If you wish, you may do this with a copper or iron tube in this manner:
The top of the tube should be solid and twisted to facilitate gripping and turning it with the fingers. If you wish, cauterize the nit as previously explained in the cauterization of warts in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
On the shapes of instruments used for injections in cases of rectal disorders, diarrhea, and colic.
The syringe may be made of silver, china, or cast or beaten copper.  Small and large versions of this instrument may be made, according to the users. Those used in the treatment of small children should be small, and those for patients with narrow or painful rectums should be very gentle.  Image of a large syringe:
Its upper funnel, to which the bladder is tied, is wide in this manner; it has a stopper where the bladder is tied, as you see.  Its lower end, which is inserted into the rectum, is solid and very smooth, except that on one side there are two holes and on the other side one hole.
The size of the hole should be the thickness of a probe, or slightly larger. The bladder, in which the medication is placed, is made from an animal's bladder or sheepskin, shaped like a small flask, its diameter being one cubit.
Many holes are pierced in a circular pattern, the distance between each hole being the thickness of a finger. Then, a strong thread of twisted yarn, composed of ten or so strands, is inserted into these holes, and the bladder is gathered together like a pouch. Then, once the medication is placed inside, it is tightly bound at the top of the syringe between two stones, and then injected. This is an image of the circle of the bladder and the hole.
The method of injection is to fill the bladder or skin with warmed medication, to an extent the patient can tolerate. Beware of it being too hot or too cold. Then the patient lies on his back with his legs raised.
A sheet or blanket is placed under him. Then the end of the syringe is lubricated with oil, egg white, fenugreek saliva, or something similar. Then it is gently inserted into the rectum. Then an assistant takes the bladder in both hands and squeezes it forcefully until everything inside the syringe is emptied into the intestine.
Then the syringe is removed, and the patient raises his legs to the wall. If the medication stimulates evacuation, he should hold it as long as possible. If he sleeps with it all night, it is more beneficial.  If it is injected for diarrhea and its exit is hastened, the injection should be repeated until he is cured.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the rectum, the syringe should be very gentle and smooth, smoother than those used to inject children. This is an image of a gentle syringe with its skin stretched over it.
Chapter eighty-four.
In the treatment of wounds.
I have mentioned in the classification the treatment of what resembles cauterizations, and I am now mentioning here its treatment by hand and by medication completely.  I say that wounds differ according to the thing by which the wound is made and according to the location where the wound occurs.
The things that cause wounds are many, such as a blow from a stone, or a cut from a sword or knife, or a stab from a spear or stick or arrow, and so on from many things.
As for wounds according to the locations on the body, there are wounds that occur on the head, or neck, or chest, or abdomen, or liver, and similar organs.
And I am describing the treatment of some wounds to make them a measure and rule for all wounds, and I begin with simple head wounds especially because the complex ones will be mentioned at the beginning of the third chapter of this book.
I say that when a simple wound occurs in the head and there is no fracture of the bone, I look; and if it is from a blow from a stone or the like, and it has only torn the skin, and the wound is large and I fear for the patient the occurrence of hot inflammation, then I hasten and incise the cephalic vein in that place. And do not delay that, and let blood be drawn from him according to his strength, if nothing prevents bloodletting, especially if the blood that comes out of the wound is little, and let the bloodletting be from the opposite side of the injured area.
And warn the patient against fullness, drinks, and thick foods; and if something prevents him from bloodletting, let him reduce food, and not approach meats or drinks. And apply to the wound, if hot inflammation occurs in it, cotton soaked in rose oil alone or with a drink that has astringency.
And if you are safe from hot inflammation, apply to the wound, if it is fresh and the air has not changed it, the powder that I will describe to you shortly; but if the air has changed it, apply some ointments to it.
Until pus forms, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large and is from a sword cut or the like, and its lips do not come together by themselves, then join it by suturing, as I describe in suturing abdominal wounds.
If the skin exposes the bone and hangs, and its attachment is only by a small connection, cut it off, then treat the wound with medicines that produce firm flesh in place of the skin, and instruct the patient to eat foods that have firmness, such as heads and brains and the like.
If decay occurs in the skin and does not adhere to the bone, cut away all that is decayed and throw it away, then treat it. If in these wounds there is an artery or vein bleeding blood, and the bleeding is not stopped by medicines, then search for the artery.
If you find it and it is not severed, sever it with the scalpel and tie it. If necessity demands, if what we mentioned does not work, cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and superficial, their treatment is easy; it suffices to treat them by sprinkling this powder on them while they are still bleeding.
before the air changes them.  The recipe for the powder is: take one part frankincense, two parts of acacia, and three parts of slaked or unslaked lime.  Grind all together, sieve it, and fill the wound with it, binding it tightly so that it adheres well.
It will coagulate with the blood; then bind it from above with bandages and leave it; do not unbind it as long as it does not swell, for the medicine will not be removed from it until it heals.  The lime alone may do this if you do not have frankincense and acacia.
This action may also be done with alum in many large wounds if its application and binding are skillfully done, and the wounds are fresh with their blood, as long as the air has not changed them; but if the air has changed them somewhat, especially in summer,
then applying this powder is meaningless. Treat it as mentioned, by applying some ripening ointments or a poultice of barley flour made with water and honey until the pus is drawn out, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
But if a fracture of the bone occurs with the wound, and it is slight, pull it with the forceps. I have described the treatment of head fractures in what follows, in the surgery of the neck. If the wound occurs in the neck, there is no difference in treatment from a head wound if it is slight.
But if a nerve or artery of the neck has been severed, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy except to avoid treating it with things that cause strong constriction, such as verdigris and vitriol and the like, because they harm the nerve and cause it to spasm.
And nothing cold should be placed on the wound at all, because the essence of the nerve is cold, and its connection is with the brain, which is the noblest of the organs. What you treat it with should be from soft medicines, such as ceruse washed many times with fresh water, moistened with oil or rose ointment and tutty.
If it is also washed with fresh water and other mineral stones in this manner, and moist ointments, but if the wound is large, use stitching or bind its edges with bandages, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has depth and a cavity forms at its bottom where pus collects, lance it in its lowest place. If an artery has been severed in the wound and you see its blood flowing, ligate it or cauterize it, or if necessary, amputate it.
If the wound has severed some or all of the cartilages of the larynx, and the vessels are unharmed, join the edges of the skin by stitching over the windpipe, do not touch the windpipe itself, but straighten it and restore it to its natural shape, then bind it tightly and leave it for some days.
Then treat it with ointments that have astringent and drying properties until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, examine it; if there are bone splinters, extract them all, or as many as possible; what you cannot reach, leave until the wound suppurates and heals, and it will be easier to remove them.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle on it the powder we have described. If a bone becomes suppurative in the wound and forms a fistula, treat it with what will be mentioned in its chapter, in the surgery of the chest and between the shoulders.
If it is a wound from a spear or knife and you see depth to it, observe: if air escapes from it when the patient breathes, know that it is a mortal wound. If it does not have depth and is fresh with blood, do not at first apply powder to it nor bind it tightly, lest the blood be retained in its depth.
It will then recede to the heart and kill the patient. But put on it an attractive ointment and dress it twice a day.  If you do not have ointment, put in the mouth of the wound old cotton to absorb the moisture that comes from it, and have the patient sleep on the wound.
so that what gathers there may flow out. And if three days or more have passed since the injury and the patient has not experienced convulsions, a bad palpitation, or shortness of breath, and you see all his other conditions to be good, know that the wound is safe; then treat it with cauterization and other treatments until it heals.
If it is difficult to see it and the pus flows constantly, know that it has become a fistula, and treat it in its chapter. The patient should lie on the wound so that what gathers there may flow out.  If it is a wound of the chest or back, treat it with the stitching described above.
If it is large, cauterize it; if it is small, use powder. If it has affected the bone and broken off fragments, explore the wound and proceed to remove those fragments if they are clean. If they are not clean, leave them until
the wound becomes foul, for then it is easy to remove them. As for the other wounds occurring in the other members, their treatment is governed by what we have mentioned.
Chapter eighty-five.
On wounds of the abdomen, the protrusion of the intestines, and their suturing.
The wound which occurs in the abdomen may be large, or it may be small, or medium.  The large one may have one or several intestines protruding from it, so the replacement and suturing of them is more difficult and arduous.
The small wound also may be difficult in another way; it is necessary that its replacement is more difficult. And the intestine, if it is not immediately returned to its place at once, swells and thickens, so its replacement becomes difficult. For this reason, the medium wound has become preferable, because with it, the replacement of the intestine is not difficult as in these two types.
And know that the suturing of the abdomen is in four ways. The two sutures are general, suitable for suturing the abdomen and for suturing all other wounds of the body. The other two sutures are specific, used for all wounds, but they are more specific to suturing the abdomen.
And I will describe them to you one by one with explanation and clarification. I say that if the wound is small and something of the intestine has protruded from it and its replacement is difficult, that is for one of two reasons: either because of the smallness of the wound, as we said, or because the intestine has been affected by swelling due to the cold air.
If that is the case, then it should be warmed by dipping a sponge or a wet cloth in lukewarm water alone, or in which anise, rue, and spikenard have been boiled, and wipe the intestine with it until the swelling resolves.
A drink containing astringents may also do this; it is better than water alone in resolving the swelling.  If you see that the swelling has resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which comfrey or mallow has been boiled, for it will make its entry easier.
If its return is still impossible after this treatment, then make a small incision in the wound with the instrument with which you incise fistulas. The first treatment, if successful, is better than the incision.  One only resorts to incision after necessity and failure of other methods. This is an image of the instrument:
One side of it is curved and limited, the other side is unlimited. The thin end is not as fine as a lancet, but is slightly blunt. It is an instrument resembling a small forceps, as you see. If the wound is widened and the intestine has entered, then it should be returned to its natural form.
And its proper place, if you can manage it, is best. But if the wound is wide and is in the lower abdomen, then the patient should lie on his back with his legs higher than his head; and if it is in the upper abdomen, then his head and chest should be higher than his lower body.
Similarly, if the wound is on one side of the abdomen, always aim to make the side with the injury higher than the other side.  This should be used in major and moderate surgeries.
As for minor surgeries, position it as best you can. Then have a skilled assistant ready to hold the entire wound with his hands and bring its edges together, then reveal it to the person performing the suturing, little by little.
This is the description of a single general suture: Take one needle, or several needles, according to the size of the wound. Then leave a space from the edge of the wound equal to the thickness of the little finger, and insert a single needle without thread into the edges of the skin. along with the edges of the peritoneum under the skin from the inside, until you pass through it from that side, having brought together the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, making four layers. Then you tighten a doubled thread around the needle several times from both sides until the edges of the wound are tightly joined.
Then leave a space equal to the thickness of a finger, and insert another needle, then connect it with the thread as you did with the first needle. Continue doing this with as many needles as you need until you complete the entire circumference of the wound. Let the needles be of moderate thickness.
Because very thin needles quickly cut the flesh, and thick ones are difficult to insert into the skin. Therefore, they should be moderate in thinness and thickness.  And the needle should not be inserted into the edge of the skin too deeply.
lest the flesh quickly tear and the wound open before it heals, nor should you space the sutures too far apart, lest the wound fail to unite. Then cut off the ends of the needles lest they injure the patient during sleep, and make for him pads of linen cloth on each side to hold the ends of the needles.
And leave them until you know that the wound has healed. This type of stitching with needles is most suitable for small wounds, because one or two needles, or thereabouts, may suffice for stitching them.
As for the method of stitching for the second type of wound, it is to bring together the four edgesthat is, the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneumat once with a needle threaded with a moderately thick and thin twisted thread. Then, when you have passed the needle through these four edges, return the needle through the same point from which you began.
So that the thread lies interlaced from the top of the wound, so that the stitching is like the stitching of a purse with which goods are tied. And make the distance between each stitch the width of the little finger. This stitching is known to everyone.
With this stitch, I sewed a wound that appeared on a man's abdomen; he had been wounded with a knife, and the wound was more than a span long; and about two spans of the midgut had protruded, and the wound was in the middle of the abdomen.
I returned it after his intestines had remained outside the wound for twenty-four hours. The wound healed in about fifteen days, and I treated him until he recovered. He lived many years thereafter, managing all his affairs, while the physicians had judged that he would never recover at all.
And it is remarkable that I did not treat him with an ointment, because I was in a place where there were no medicines.  I would apply worn cotton to the wound twice a day and dress it by washing with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the two types of special stitching, I mention it according to Galen's words: you begin the stitching from the skin, and insert the needle from the outside to the inside; then, when you have passed the needle through the skin and the muscle which runs straight along the whole length of the belly.
leave the edge of the peritoneum on the side into which you inserted the needle; and pass the needle through its other edge from the inside to the outside, into the other edge of the peritoneum; then, when you have passed it through, pass it again through this same edge of the peritoneum, from outside to inside.
And leave the edge of the peritoneum on that side; and pass the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, and as you pass it through, pass it also into the edge of the peritoneum on its side, until you have passed it completely through.
Then begin also from this same side and sew it together with the edge of the peritoneum on the other side, and bring the needle out through the skin nearby; then return it into that skin and sew the edge of the peritoneum on the other side together with this edge of the peritoneum.
And bring it out through the skin on its side, and do that again, and do it repeatedly until you have sewn up the whole wound in that way. The general description of this stitch is that it is sewn like the stitching of furs together.
That you sew the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin, and leave it once from the other side until you are finished.  As for the second type of special stitching which Galen also mentioned, this is his exact wording.
And that is to sew it in the manner that some practitioners sew it, by bringing each part to its natural counterpart, so they join the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge, and the edge of the intestine to its other edge; and that is as I describe to you.
You should insert the needle into the edge of the intestine nearest to you from the outside and pass it to the inside in it alone, and leave the edges of the peritoneum; then return the needle and pass it from the outside to the inside in both edges of the peritoneum, then return it again.
And pass it from the inside to the outside in the other edge of the intestine which is on the opposite side. This type of stitching is better than the easy general stitching, which is the stitching previously mentioned. This is Galen's exact wording. Some have said that the stitching of the abdomen may be straightened with two needles. That is, you insert a single thread into them, and begin by inserting the needle from your side and pass it to the other side, and insert the other needle from the opposite side, and pass it from your side, according to the stitching of the tentmakers.
Know that if the wound is in the middle of the abdomen, then its stitching is more difficult than in other places of the abdomen. As for the treatment of the wound, its rule in its treatment is the rule of all other wounds; that is, if you find the wound fresh with its blood before the air alters it, and you return the intestine and sew it and tighten it, then apply to it the medicated lint.
If the wound has been altered by the air, then apply to it some of the ointments that you prepare until it suppurates and the stitches fall out and the peritoneum and intestine unite, then treat it as you treat other wounds until it heals.
If you do not have medicines, then apply to it, from the time it begins to suppurate, used cotton and change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it heals.  For you do not need other treatment in most cases if the wounds are simple. If you fear that the wound will involve the principal organs in pain,
then it is necessary that you dip soft wool in moderately warm oil or in rose oil and place it around the areas between the groin and the armpit. If he feels pain or putrefaction in his intestinesand that often occursthen inject him with a lukewarm astringent black drink.
Especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and become a penetrating wound into its cavity. And know that what is thick of the intestines is easier to heal, and what is thin of them is more difficult to heal.
And as for the intestine known as the jejunum, it does not at all accept healing from a wound that occurs in it. This is because of the multitude of its veins and their size, and the thinness of its substance, and its proximity to the nature of the nerve. But if what protrudes from the wound is omentum and you catch it fresh, then replace it according to your replacing of the intestine, similarly.
If a period of time passes and it has turned green or black, then it is necessary that you tie it with a thread above the place that has blackened, lest bleeding occur, for in the omentum are veins and arteries. Then cut below that ligature.
And leave the ends of the thread hanging from below the wound, outside of it, so that it is easy for you to pull it and remove it upon the falling away of the omentum and the suppuration of the wound.  Mention of the wound that occurs in the intestine. But if a rupture occurs in the intestine and it is small
it may be possible that it heals in some people, for I have seen a man who had been wounded in his abdomen by a spear thrust, and the wound was to the right of the stomach.  The wound became chronic and became a fistula from which feces and wind emerged.  So I treated it although I did not hope for its healing. And I continued to treat him gently until it healed and the place rejoined. When I saw the place had rejoined, I feared for the patient that an ill event might occur in his abdomen, but no ill event whatsoever occurred to him, and he remained in the best of conditions. He rightly eats, drinks, copulates, enters the bath, and exerts himself in his work. And some experienced people have mentioned that whenever a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it should be sutured in this manner.
And it is that large-headed ants are taken, then the edges of the wound are brought together, then one of them, with its mouth open, is placed on the edges of the wound.  When it closes its mouth and tightens, its head is cut off, for it will adhere and will not loosen. Then another ant is placed near the first.
And you continue to do that with several ants according to the size of the wound, then you cover it and suture the wound. For those heads remain adhering in the intestine until the intestine heals and recovers, and no harm whatsoever occurs to the patient.  And it is also possible that the intestine may be sutured with fine thread.
That which is drawn from the intestine of the animal adhering to it after it is entered into a needle is that the end of this thread is taken from the intestine, then it is drawn gently, then a fine twisted linen thread is tied to its end, then that thread is entered into the needle. And in it is the intestine thread, and with it the intestine is sutured, then it is returned to the abdomen. And this type of suturing with ants and intestine is only by way of hope and expectation.
But if the perforation is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, then there is no remedy and no healing whatsoever.
Chapter eighty-six.
In the treatment of catarrh and fistula.
Know that every wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic and prolonged and becomes an ulcer and does not heal, and continuously discharges pus that does not stop, is generally called, in whatever organ it may be, a fistula, and we call it catarrh, and fistula.
It is in reality a hardened, matted, white lump, painless, with a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some have called it a feather; and sometimes it is moist, constantly discharging pus, and sometimes the moisture may cease; and this moisture may be abundant or scanty, thick or thin.
And I have already stated in the classification, when mentioning wounds, that every ulcer which does not heal and in which flesh does not grow is due to one of nine causes: one of them is either due to a deficiency of blood in the body, or to its poor quality, or because there is hard flesh inside it and on its edges which prevents the growth of good flesh. Or because it is full of pus and dirt; or because the ulcer itself is foul and the substance it discharges is of poor quality; or because the treatment is unsuitable; or because of a pestilence affecting the locality; or because of a specific property of the locality.
Or because it is full of pus and dirt; or because the ulcer itself is foul and the substance it discharges is of poor quality; or because the treatment is unsuitable; or because of a pestilence affecting the locality; or because of a specific property of the locality. As was observed in the city of Saragossa, where the maturation of diseases is difficult and the healing of tumors is naturally slow; or because there is one or several bones in it.  I have explained all these causes, their signs, and their treatments with medicines in the classification.
It is appropriate here that I inform you of the treatment of fistulas and abscesses which is done by hand. Know that fistulas can occur in all parts of the body; some of them end in large veins and arteries or in a nerve.
Or to the peritoneum, or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to a rib, or to a vertebra of the spine, or to a joint of the compound joints like the hand and foot; and some lead to the vicinity of a principal organ and so on.
Now, those fistulas of this nature are among the difficult-to-cure diseases, which do not accept treatment and working on them is troublesome and ignorant.  If there are fistulas which have not reached these places which I have mentioned, and I have hoped and trusted in their treatment,
And you wish to arrive at knowledge of that, then take a probe of copper or iron. If the fistula runs straight, then examine it with it.  If there is a bend in the fistula, then examine it with a probe of thin lead, because lead is soft in its body.
It will become smooth upon entering and will bend towards the bend. If the fistula has many openings, you cannot ascertain them with a probe; then inject one opening of its openings, for the moisture which you inject with it will pass towards the other openings and will flow.
from them, then thoroughly investigate in any way possible, to know if there is bone or nerve, or if the bottom of the fistula is far or near, or if it is one fistula with many openings.  Ascertain all that to the extent of your ability by questioning the patient and by the presence of his pain when you touch the place.
And in a similar way from the signs and causes incident to the swelling.  If you have ascertained all this truly, then turn to treatment confidently. It is that you look, if it is
the fistula is apparent, near, or in a safe place far from a joint or nerve or artery or vein, or one of the places I mentioned to you, then incise the fistula according to what I described, and remove what is in it of the clot and the corrupt flesh and the excess flesh.
And whatever grows in its opening of flesh or wart and the like, and treat it until it heals. If the fistula is deep-seated and is straight, then it is necessary that you incise it in depth as much as you can, then cleanse it of all its corrupt flesh.
Then use a twisted thread coated in strong medicines and insert it to the bottom of the fistula which you reach with an instrument; do this repeatedly until that strong medicine eats away all that remains in the bottom of the fistula of corruption.
Then bind it with ointments which produce healthy flesh until it heals. If it does not heal by this, cauterize it according to what was previously described. If the cause of the fistula is bone, and this is confirmed for you, then probe it and incise it according to what I told you, if nothing prevents you from
a vein or nerve or principal member as we said, then if the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then remove it until its corruption goes away, then treat it with what will heal it until it heals. If it does not heal and the pus continues as it was doing
know that you have not reached the complete removal of the corruption; uncover everything on it again and thoroughly investigate its removal and cleansing with the utmost effort, then bind it. If it heals, otherwise know that that corruption is in a deep recess of corruption; uncover everything on it again.
and thoroughly investigate its removal and cleansing with the utmost effort. If the corrupted bone is small and you can pull it out, then pull it out with the delicate forceps which are suitable for that.
If there are many bones, thoroughly extract them all and do not leave anything of them. If something of the ligaments obstructs you and there is no danger in cutting them, then cut those ligaments and the flesh if it is there, and free the bones.
If you are not able to extract the bones at the time, then apply to the wound what will cause it to rot, and leave it for some days until it rots what is around those bones of flesh and ligaments, and keep the wound from healing, the incision that you made during your work.
Rather, if you fear that, place on it cotton soaked in powdered sulfur with oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments; for when those ligaments have rotted and the bones have become loosened, it will be easy to extract and pull them out.
If it is one large bone, like a bone of the leg or a bone of the thigh or similar, and only its surface is corrupted, then remove it thoroughly until that blackness and corruption disappears, then set the wound.  If a large part of it is corrupted and the corruption has reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to spread it [the corruption].
And cut it all away to where the corruption ends. Then treat it until it heals. And I will tell you of a case of gangrene that occurred in a man's leg to make it an example and aid in your treatment. This man was young.
About thirty years old, he experienced pain in his leg due to a cause moving within his body, until the materials reached the leg and it swelled with a great swelling, and there was no external cause. Then time went on with the mistakes of the physicians until the swelling opened.
And much material flowed from it, and it was treated badly until the leg became gangrenous and many openings appeared in it, all discharging pus and the body's humors. A number of physicians treated him for about two years, and among them was no one skilled in surgery.
Until they came to me. I saw his leg and the material flowing from those openings in a great flow, and the man's body was emaciated and his color yellow.  Then I inserted a probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone. Then I examined all the openings.
And I found that some led to others from all sides of the leg. So I began by cutting open one of those openings until I uncovered some of the bone. And I found it corrupt, eroded, blackened, putrefied, and perforated until the perforation reached the marrow. Then I spread open what was revealed to me.
And I proceeded to remove the corrupt bone, and I thought that there was no corruption in the bone other than what I had cut and spread open, and that I had excised it. Then I set about closing the wound with vulnerary medicines for a long time, but it did not heal. Then I returned and uncovered the bone again, beyond the first uncovering.
And I found the corruption connected to the bone. So I spread open what was revealed to me also of that corruption. Then I attempted to close it, but it did not close nor heal. Then I uncovered it again, and I continued to cut away the bone piece by piece and attempted to close it, but it would not close, until I had cut away about a span of bone.
And I removed it with its marrow. Then I closed it with medicines, and it healed quickly and completely.  This repetition in the procedure and its opening was necessary because of the patient's weakness and his inability to withstand much, and my fear for his death, because he frequently suffered from severe fainting spells due to excessive vomiting.  Then he recovered completely.
And in the place of the bone, firm flesh grew, and his condition in his body improved, and his strength returned, and he managed his affairs, and in his walking he suffered no harm whatsoever.  If a projecting bone in any part of the body is broken
then it should be spread open in this way: you take a ligature and tie it to the end of the projecting bone, and you instruct someone to pull it upwards, and you make another ligature of thicker wool than the first ligature.
Then you tie it to the flesh under the bone and pull its end so that the flesh is drawn downwards, and you uncover the flesh from the place you want to spread open lest the saw injure the flesh, and you place a piece of wood or a board firmly under the bone from below.
Because if you do that, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the decayed bone.  The sawing should be slightly above the location of the decay, lest there be decay in the cavity of the bone that does not appear on its surface to the senses, requiring you to saw it again.
If the bone is decayed and not projecting, but its parts are connected to each other and the decay is in its middle or in some part of it, then uncover the flesh from all its sides. Then place the wood underneath, and then saw it from the first side where the decay is.
When you have finished sawing it, saw it from the other side, and let the sawing be a little distance from the decay, as we said. If the decay is in a joint, cut the decayed joint itself and saw the bone where it connects from the other side.
If the decay is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy but excision. If the decay is in the metacarpus of the hand or foot, its treatment is very difficult, but you should peel away the decay however it appears to you, excise it, and cleanse it in whatever way you can.
And by whatever method it is straightened for you, provided that a vein or nerve does not obstruct you. And know that the saws and instruments for cutting these bones are numerous, according to the position, setting, thickness, thinness, size, smallness, hardness, and porosity of the bones. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare for each type of work an instrument similar to that work.
And know that the operations themselves may indicate to you the type of instrument you need, if you have long practice and knowledge of the arts of this profession, because that is from the skill of the profession and seeing many types of diseases. He may therefore devise for himself instruments similar to those for each disease, and I have illustrated for you at the end of this chapter several instruments.
Making them examples to be imitated and a measure by which you measure others, God willing. Image of a saw:
