Chapter eighty-seven.
On the amputation of limbs and the sawing of bones.
Limbs may become putrid, either from an external cause or from an internal cause. If you treat this corruption with medicinal treatment and the treatment is ineffective, and you see the corruption spreading in the limb, nothing preventing it,
then it is necessary to amputate that member to where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may thereby escape death or a great calamity which is greater than the loss of the limb.  A sign of this occurring in someone is that the limb will blacken until you think that fire has burned it.
Or it may become putrid after the blackening, until that putridity spreads to what is next to that limb and affects the whole body; therefore, hasten to cut it off.  Likewise, if the cause of the putrefaction is the sting of some venomous creature, like a sea scorpion, or a snake, or a tarantula, or the like.
If the corruption or sting is in the tip of a finger, cut off the finger and do not allow the corruption to spread to the hand. Likewise, if it has affected the hand, cut it off at the base of the forearm, and do not allow the corruption to spread until it affects the bones of the forearm.
Likewise, if it affects the arm, cut off the arm at the elbow in the joint itself. If the corruption spreads and you see it reaching toward the shoulder, do not amputate the shoulder at all, for in that is the death of the patient; rather, use other treatments to the best of your ability.
And you do likewise with the leg: if the corruption affects the toe, cut off the toe at one of the joints; and if it affects the metatarsus, cut off the whole foot.  If it ascends to the knee, cut off the leg at the knee joint.
And if the corruption has reached above the knee, there is no recourse but to leave it and abandon the patient to death. The method of cutting off or excising a limb is to tie a ligature below the place you wish to cut and another ligature above the place.
And one assistant pulls the lower ligature downwards, and another assistant pulls the upper ligature upwards, and you yourself dissect the flesh between the ligatures with a broad scalpel until all the flesh is exposed, then cut or excise it. And it is necessary that you place linen cloths on all sides.
Lest the saw touch the healthy part, causing the patient additional pain and hot swelling. If bleeding occurs during your work, quickly cauterize the area or apply some styptic powder, then return to your treatment until you finish.
Then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. And I will tell you about a case which occurred in a mans leg, the same condition that I describe to you. That is, a blackness occurred in his leg with burning like fire.
And that corruption first occurred in his toe until it took the whole leg. So the man, of his own accord, when he saw the corruption spreading in the limb with the intensity of the pain and burning, cut it off at the joint and healed.
When a long time passed, that same corruption appeared in the index finger of his hand. So he came to me. I intended to repel that corruption with the medications I applied to the hand after cleansing his body. But the corruption did not subside and continued to spread to the other finger.
Until the corruption took hold of the hand.  He asked me to amputate his hand, but I refused, hoping to repel that corruption, and also fearing death for him if his hand were amputated, because the man's strength was failing.  When he despaired of me, he returned to his town.
Then I heard that he cut off his entire hand and healed.  And I have told this story to help with what occurs of this type of illness and to be an example to be relied upon and acted upon.
Chapter eighty-eight.
In the treatment of fistulas and the method of injecting them with medication.
If a swelling occurs in one of the fleshy members, and the duration of the swelling is prolonged until it gathers matter, then bursts or is lanced, and all the matter within it comes out, and the place remains empty as if it were a vessel.
And the skin over it is like a rag, thinned, and its corruption has not reached the point of affecting bone, or nerve, or ligament. From this it deserves to be called a fistula, and not a sinus, except that its duration is prolonged until the corruption affects something of these members.
Then it is called a sinus or fistula, and its treatment is to cut away all that skin as it goes around, especially if it has thinned and become like a rag, and you are certain that it will not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
But if you hope that the skin will adhere, and its corruption has not reached that point, and there is thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with injection.  This is that you look at the fistula; if it is large and the pus that flows from it is foul-smelling, then inject it with the Egyptian medicine.
which is vinegar, oil, honey, and verdigris, equal parts, gathered in a vessel and cooked over the fire until the medicine thickens and comes to the consistency of honey. Then take what you need of it and thin it with water and honey, and inject it into the fistula, and bind its mouth, and leave the medicine in it for about two hours.
Then remove it by pressing.  Do this for several days until the fistula is cleansed and the foul smell is gone.  You may inject it with ash water if this medicine is not available to you.  This is that you take the ash of grapevine wood or the ash of oak wood and pour water over it.
Then filter it and inject it until you are certain that the fistula has been washed. If the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, then inject it with water and honey in which some powdered verdigris has been mixed.
Or inject it with honey and syrup mixed, for honey cleanses and washes, and the syrup adheres to the fistula, especially if the syrup has astringent and drying properties. When you have done this several times and are certain that the fistula's corruption has gone, then inject it with something that will grow flesh in it.
Such as taking some palm ointment and dissolving it in rose oil and an astringent syrup, or inject it with some other medicines and ointments whose descriptions we have given in the chapter on ointments. If the mouth of the fistula is narrow and does not allow the injection tube to enter,
widen it slightly with an iron instrument, or place in it a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment or theriac ointment until it widens.  Similarly, if the opening is also wide, bring its edges together with sutures, leaving only enough space for the injection tube to enter, no more.
Similarly, if the opening from which the pus flows is high up, incise it in the lowest part so that the pus flows downwards, for if the pus is retained in the depth of the fistula it prevents the growth of flesh.
If you cannot incise the fistula downwards as you wish, then position the limb so that the pus flows out easily as best you can, so that no pus at all is retained in it.
As for the cauterizing medicines which are applied externally to the fistula and how to apply them: take some of the cauterizing ointments suitable for what you want and spread it on a linen cloth; let the cloth be large enough to cover the whole fistula. Then, with scissors, cut a hole opposite the mouth of the fistula, slightly larger than the mouth of the fistula. Then spread some soft ointment on another cloth, to the size of the hole, and place it there, keeping it away from the mouth of the fistula to facilitate the discharge of pus; and do not remove the larger cloth until afterwards.
several days; but the one on the mouth of the fistula, you remove at all times so that what has collected there of pus may come out, and you may know whether what flows from it of pus is much or little, mature or immature.
And also with this, examine the fistula itself: does its owner feel pain in it or not, and is there swelling in it or not? If your treatment continues like this and you see that the matter which comes out is slight at the mouth of the fistula,
know that the fistula has adhered or is close to adhering; then, resolve it on the morrow or on the third day. And when you open the wound, replace the cloths with others and renew the ointment. If, after a long time, thin pus comes out of the fistula, do not despair of its closing.
Its healing is near, for some people are slow in the growth of flesh in their wounds, and others the opposite. If, after many days, immature pus comes out of the fistula, know that the fistula has not closed. If the closing of the fistula is slow,
increase the drying medications, and let them be medications that are moist in their consistency and dry in their strength, like palm ointment if an excess of extract of henbane has been added to it. And among the most effective things to treat it with is that palm ointment is taken.
and it is resolved with rose oil, and sprinkle on it old wine of moderate consistency, then mix it with it and use it. He may do something similar if you do not have palm ointment: honey, if you cook it until it thickens and use it; or take myrrh, aloe and olibanum, crush everything and sprinkle it on the honey while it is on the fire. Then spread it on a cloth and bind it to the fistula; or it is spread with the cooked honey until it thickens, and sprinkle on it the drugs and place the cloth on it and bind it.
And long rhubarb, the root of the asphodel, and the flour of costus and centaury may be used in such a case, singly or together, crushed, sifted, and sprinkled on the honey as described, and used. If the fistula persists and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption in its depth.
And if it affects the bones, or other hard nerves, or ligaments, treat it as a fistula, as previously described.
Chapter eighty-nine.
On the treatment of the felon, the injured nail, the amputation of the superfluous finger, and the separation of adherent fingers.
A felon is an excessive growth of flesh under the nail of the thumb of the hand or foot, and it may grow in other fingers. If it persists and its treatment is neglected, it swells into a hot tumor.
Or it becomes putrid and foul until it eats the root of the nail, and perhaps destroys it entirely, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone, until it has a foul odor and the tip of the finger becomes wide and its color becomes livid. If you treat it with what we mentioned in the division and your treatment is not effective,
then it is necessary to cut away with iron all the superfluous growth which grows from the nail, and then cauterize the wound afterwards, for cauterization is very beneficial in this. And if the bone is sound and the nail is also sound,
and the corner containing the nail has pinched the flesh inwards and caused it to be ulcerated and injured, then it is necessary to place a thin pledget under the corner of the nail which pinches the flesh and raise it upwards and cut away that flesh gently.
And you place on what remains of the flesh of the cauterizing corrosives until it is all gone, then you treat it with ointments until it heals. And if the corruption has affected the bone, then it is necessary to cut away that bone and remove it, for it will not heal as long as there is corrupt bone at all.
If you see the erosion and corruption spreading in the finger, then cut it off at one of the joints as mentioned before, then treat it until it heals. If the nail receives a blow or injury and severe pain occurs in it, then it is necessary to bleed the patient first.
Then you incise the nail with a sharp scalpel, an oblique incision from above downwards, and be careful not to reach with the incision to the flesh under the nail, for by doing so you cause the patient severe pain, and it will be a cause for the growth of excessive flesh in the place, then treat the place with what relieves pains.
As for the supernumerary finger which develops in some people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy, and it may have bones in some of it, and it may have a nail, and the growth of some of them is at the root of the joint of some of the fingers.
Or its growth may be in some of the finger joints. Those which grow in some of the finger joints do not move, and those which grow at the finger joint may move.  As for that which is fleshy only, its cutting is easy, and that is to cut it at its base with a wide scalpel.
As for that whose growth is at the root of the joint, its treatment is difficult, so avoid cutting it. As for that which grows in the finger at one of the joints, it is necessary first to cut its flesh in a circular cut to the bone, then to saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for that.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the adhesion which occurs to the fingers, some to some, this often occurs, and this is from what a person is born with, and it occurs when a wound heals or a burn from fire and the like.  Then it is necessary that you incise that adhesion.
Until the fingers return to their natural form. Then place between them a pledget or a piece of linen soaked in rose oil, lest it adhere quickly, and separate them, or place between them a thin lead plate until the place heals as it should.
Similarly, if adhesion occurs to some of the fingers with the palm, incise that adhesion according to what is suitable and improves the shape of the member.
Chapter Ninety.
On the cutting of polyps.
Polyps are thick, twisted veins filled with black, viscous matter which occur in most parts of the body, most frequently in the legs, especially in the legs of vine-dressers, reapers and porters. Firstly, strong rubbing of the body with black hellebore should be employed.
Then bleed the patient in the basilica vein.  As for treating it with iron, there are two methods: one is to incise and evacuate the black blood; the other is to extract the vein and remove it entirely. As for incision, the leg should first be bathed in hot water.
until the thick, turbid blood becomes liquefied. Then the patient's leg is bound from above the thigh to below the knee with a bandage, and then the vein is incised in one, two or three places with a wide incision.
Then you evacuate the black blood with your hand from the lower leg upwards and from above downwards until you remove the amount of blood you consider sufficient or what the patient's strength can bear.  Then you bandage it and instruct him to avoid foods that generate black bile.  The purging and bloodletting are repeated.
When the veins are full and this harms the patient. As for its extraction, this is how it is done: shave the patient's leg if it has much hair, then put him in the bath and bathe his leg in hot water until it reddens and sweats, or he performs vigorous exercise.
If a bath is not available, heat the limb. Then incise the skin over the vein lengthwise, either at its end near the knee or at its lower end near the heel.  Then open the skin with hooks and dissect the vein from every side until it is palpable.
When it appears, you will see it is dark red. When it is freed from the skin, it also looks like a tendon. Then insert a probe under it until it is raised and emerges from the skin; suspend it with a smooth, blunt hook. Then make another incision near that incision, about three fingers' breadth away.
Then dissect the skin from over the vein until it appears. Then raise it with the probe as you did before and suspend it with another hook as you did initially.  Then make another incision, or several incisions if needed.
Then extract it and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel. Then pull and extract it until it comes out of the second incision. Then pull it to the incision above it, and do this until you pull it from the third incision, the highest of all the incisions, until when it is all extracted, cut it.
If it does not yield to traction and dissection, insert a needle with strong doubled thread, tie it, and pull it. Insert the probe underneath and twist it with your hand in every direction until it emerges. Be careful that it does not break; if it breaks, it will be very difficult for you to extract it.
And harm will come to the patient from it. When you have extracted it completely, apply wool soaked in a medicament and rose oil or olive oil to the site of the incisions, and treat it until it heals. If the varicose vein is tense, tortuous, and twisted in places,
and not straight as we said, then it is necessary to incise it at each bend and twist. Then suspend it with hooks until you extract it entirely. Be careful when incising it not to cut or injure the vein, for it will make its extraction difficult for you; therefore, be careful.
Image of the instrument with which the varicose vein is incised.
Image of the blind hook.
It should not be hooked like other hooks, nor should it have a sharp point, lest it injure the vein. It should be thick, smooth, and curved. For if it is thin, it will cut the vein with its thinness. Rather, it should have a considerable thickness, as we said.
Chapter ninety-one.
On the removal of the varicose vein.
This vein develops in the legs in hot countries like the Hijaz and the lands of the Arabs, and in hot, barren, and sparsely fertile lands.  It may also develop in other parts of the body besides the legs, and its development is due to an infection that occurs under the skin.
As happens with living creatures and worms inside the bodies, and the gourd worm, and the worm that develops between the skin and the flesh.  A sign of the beginning of this vein is that intense inflammation occurs in the leg, then the area swells, and then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that swelling.
As if it were the root of a plant or animal; when its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing one to two drachms, and tied, leaving the lead hanging from the leg.
Whenever something emerges from it to the outside, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it becomes very long, cut some of it and wrap the remainder.  Do not cut it from its root before it has all emerged, for if you cut it, it will retract and enter the flesh, causing swelling and infection in the area and a foul ulcer.
Therefore, it should be carefully and gradually drawn out until all of it emerges and nothing remains in the body.  From this vein, in some people, something may emerge that is five or ten cubits in length.
It has been reported to me that it emerged from a man to a length of twenty cubits. If it breaks while you are treating it, insert a probe into its hole and gently probe it along the body until all its material is emptied.  Then attempt to cleanse the area with medicines for some days.
Then treat it as you would treat a tumor. This vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in a joint of the leg or in the leg itself, causing many openings. From each opening, a branch emerges. Treat it as we have mentioned in the classification and as previously described.
Chapter Ninety-Two.
Concerning the incision for the worm that is generated under the nail, which is called the cow's disease.
This disease is called in some of our countries "the cow's disease," because it frequently affects cows. It is a small single worm that is generated between the skin and flesh and moves throughout the body, ascending and descending.
Its movement from one member to another can be felt until it breaks through the skin at some point and emerges.  Its formation is from the corruption of some humors, as happens with worms, snakes, and gourd-like growths in the abdomen.
The danger from it is that if it moves in the body and ascends to the head and reaches the eye, it may open it and emerge, destroying the eye. This happens frequently. If you want to treat it and remove it, this should be done when it is moving and its presence is felt.
It is necessary to tightly bind above and below it with a strong ligature, then incise over it and remove it. If it has sunk into the flesh and you cannot find it, apply cautery with fire to the area until you burn it. The greatest danger, as we said, is its damaging the eye.
If you see it has reached the head near the eye, then bind tightly below it on the forehead, then incise over it and remove it. The patient should also be treated by purifying his body with purgative medicines for bad, corrupt humors, and should abstain from foods that produce corruption.
Chapter Ninety-Three.
Concerning the incision for the disease known as the *nafir*.
This disease, which in our country is called *nafir*, is a pain that appears in some organs and then moves from one organ to another; I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was called to a sick woman in the countryside. She uncovered her arm and showed me a slight swelling in a vein of the forearm. After a short time, I saw that swelling moving with the ulna, like a worm, ascending to her shoulder as quickly as possible, like mercury.
When it moved from one place to another, the pain disappeared from that place and settled in the shoulder. Then, after a while, it ran throughout the body until it reached the other arm. Then she told me that it went around her whole body, as I had witnessed.
I was amazed by the speed of its movement from one organ to another. I had never before seen this disease with my own eyes in this way, except that I had seen several people whose pain moved from one organ to another, but I had not seen it with my own eyes as I saw it in this woman.
I judged this to be because the woman was from the countryside, dry-bodied, with visible veins.  Therefore, that moving wind was perceptible, and it would not appear similarly in people of comfort and those with moist bodies and hidden veins.
If you wish to treat it and the patient feels pain, and if it appears to you visibly as we have mentioned, then bind above and below it tightly with a bandage and make an incision so that the collected wind escapes and cauterize the place.
If you do not see it with your eyes, then treat it by shaking the body and with things that dispel and disperse the wind, such as the seeds of  *Asafetida* and *Scabiosa* and similar medicines.
Chapter ninety-four.
On extracting arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and according to the locations in the body where they are found. As for their differences according to their types, there are large and small ones, hollow glass ones and solid glass ones, and some have three points and some four.
Some have barbs and some have splinters. As for those according to the organs in which they are found, they are of two kinds: either the organs are hollow principal organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lung, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like; If an arrow has entered one of these organs and you see the signs of death which I will describe to you later, then you should avoid extracting that arrow from it, for death will overtake its owner in most instances. But if these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow has not disappeared into the depth of the organ, then extract it and treat the wound.  And of the signs [of injury to] the brain, if an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone and wounds the membrane upon the brain, then there will result from that a severe headache, stupor, and dizziness.
But if these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow has not disappeared into the depth of the organ, then extract it and treat the wound.  And of the signs [of injury to] the brain, if an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone and wounds the membrane upon the brain, then there will result from that a severe headache, stupor, and dizziness. And redness in the eyes and redness of the tongue, and convulsions, and confusion of the mind, and vomiting, and perhaps blood will come from the nostrils or ears, and perhaps speech will be lost and the voice will be gone, and a white moisture resembling gruel will come from the place of the wound, and from it will flow something like the water of flesh.
If these signs appear to you, then refrain from treating the patient and from extracting the arrow if it has not come out. As for the signs [of an arrow striking] the heart, if it is near the left breast and you feel it as if it has been driven into something solid and not into something empty,
and perhaps the arrow will have a movement resembling the pulse, and black blood will flow from the wound, and this will be followed by coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting; then know that death is inevitably approaching. And a sign [of an arrow wounding] the lung is the outflow of frothy blood from the wound.
And the vessels which are next to the neck will swell, and the color of the patient will change, and he will breathe heavily and will seek to inhale cold air. If the arrow strikes the diaphragm which is in the chest, then it will be near the small ribs, and the breathing will be labored with severe pain.
And he will sigh and all the members of the shoulders will move. And if the arrow strikes the liver, severe pain will follow, and blood resembling the liver in its redness will come from the wound. And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then perhaps something undigested from the food will come from the wound.
And its condition is obvious. And if the arrow strikes the abdomen and becomes embedded in it, and something of the excrement or fat or intestine which has been perforated comes out from the wound, then there is no hope for its treatment or for extracting the arrow. If the arrow strikes the bladder and urine comes out,
and something protrudes from it to the outside and the pain becomes severe for the patient, then know that he is doomed. As for the other members, like the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, vertebrae of the back, clavicle, thigh, leg, and the like of the members, they are usually spared.
When the arrow does not encounter an artery or nerve and the arrow is not poisoned. And I inform you of some of what I have witnessed concerning these arrows so that you may thereby be guided in your treatment. And that is that an arrow once struck a man in the corner of his eye at the root of the nose.
Then I extracted it for him from the other side under the lobe of the ear and he recovered, and nothing untoward happened to his eye. And I extracted another arrow for a Jew which had struck him in the lobe of his eye under the lower eyelid, and the arrow had become hidden, and I seized only its small tip which is attached to the wood.
And it was a large arrow of the composite bows, square-headed, smooth, without barbs.  Then the Jew recovered, and no ill befell his eye. And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian, and the arrow was Arabian.
And it is the one which has barbs; so I made an incision for him between the jugular veins, and it had sunk into his throat. So I gently manipulated it until I extracted it, and the Christian was saved and recovered. And I extracted another arrow for a man which had struck him in the abdomen, and we judged that he would die from it.
Then when thirty days or so had passed, and nothing in his condition had changed, I made an incision upon the arrow and maneuvered it, and extracted it, and he recovered, and no ill befell him. And I saw a man struck by an arrow in his back, and the wound had closed over it.
Then, after seven years, the arrow emerged at the root of his thigh. And I saw a woman who had been struck in her abdomen by an arrow, and the wound had closed, and the arrow remained.  Nothing in her condition changed, nor did she experience any harm from it in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man struck by an arrow in his face, and the wound closed, and he felt little pain from it. And there are many similar cases. And I extracted an arrow for a man from the Sultan's bodyguard, which had struck him in the middle of his nose, leaning slightly to the right side.
And the entire arrow was hidden. I was called to treat him three days after the arrow struck. I found the arrow wound very narrow, so I explored it with a thin probe, but I did not feel it. He felt a stinging pain under his ear on the right side of the wound.
I hoped that this stinging pain was from the arrowhead, so I dressed the wound with a dressing possessing drawing and ripening power, hoping that the area would swell and the arrows location would be revealed, so I could incise it.  But nothing happened in the area to indicate that the arrow had reached that place.
I continued the dressing for many days, but nothing happened. Then, during that time, the wound closed, and the patient despaired of the arrow's removal for a time, until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and told me.  I then applied a strong, corrosive medication to the wound for many days.
Until it opened, and I probed it. I felt the thin end of the arrow that adheres to the wood. Then I increased the opening of the wound with that strong medication until the arrowhead appeared before us.  I spent about four months dealing with this.
Then, when the wound widened and I could insert forceps into it, I pulled and moved it, but it did not come out.  I continued to gently manipulate it and devise ways using various instruments until one day I grasped it with strong forceps, as its image is shown at the end of the chapter.
The physicians judged that the cartilage of his nose would not heal, but I healed it, and the wound closed, and the patient recovered completely, without any further harm. I tell you how to extract some arrows to make this a measure and a guide for what I have not mentioned.
Because the parts and details of this craft cannot be grasped by description nor contained in a book.  Rather, the skillful artisan judges the much from the little, the absent from the present, and devises a new work and a new instrument for strange emergencies.
If we descend from this craft, then I say that arrows are extracted from the members in which they are lodged in two ways: either by pulling from the point of entry, or from the opposite direction, and those that are extracted from where they entered.
Either the arrow is protruding in a fleshy place, in which case it is pulled out.  If it does not readily come out at the time, then you should leave it for a few days until the flesh around it putrefies, making it easier to pull out. This also applies if it is lodged in bone.
And if it does not readily come out, leave it also for days and return to pulling and moving it every day, for then it will come out.  If it does not come out after days, then you should pierce around the arrow in the bone itself from every side with a delicate borer, until you widen the space for the arrow, then pull it out.
If the arrow lodged in the skull has penetrated deeply into one of the ventricles of the brain and some of those symptoms I mentioned appear in the patient, then refrain from pulling out the arrow and leave it until its condition improves after a few days.
then proceed to pull out the arrow. If it is very firmly lodged and does not come out easily, use the borers around the arrow as I described to you, then treat the area until it heals.  As for if the arrow has disappeared into a part of the body, and is hidden from sensation, then probe for it with a probe. If you feel it, pull it out with some instruments suitable for pulling it out. If you cannot reach it because of the narrowness of the wound and the depth of the arrow, and there is neither bone, nor nerve, nor vein,
then incise it until the wound is widened and you can reach the arrow so that you can remove it. If it has barbs, grasp them, and free the flesh attached to them from every side by every means you can.  If you cannot free the flesh, break the barbs and twist them until you succeed.
And when you attempt to remove the arrow, wherever it may be, use the forceps to twist your hand in all directions until you free it. Be extremely gentle, lest the arrow break and it becomes difficult for you to pull it out. If you cannot remove it at once,
leave it for a few days until the flesh around it becomes putrid, then try again; it will then be easier. If bleeding occurs, use the treatments mentioned in their respective chapters.  Guard against cutting a vein, nerve or tendon, and use skill.
By any means you can, free the arrow; let this be done gently, slowly and firmly as I have described to you. It is necessary that when you pull out the arrow, you place the patient in the position he was in when the arrow struck him; this is most suitable.
If you cannot do that, use whatever positions you can. As for an arrow that exits on the opposite side, either a part of it will have emerged, or you will feel the tip of the arrow just under the skin, and see it protruding.
Incise it, and let the incision be large enough for the forceps. Then pull it out; it will be easy to remove. If it is stuck in a bone, twist your hand circularly so that the arrow affects the bone and makes room for itself, then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for a few days, then return to it until it comes out. If the arrow shaft is still there, push it with it. If the shaft has fallen off and you wish to use pushing, insert the hollow instrument so that its cavity enters the end of the arrow, then push it with that.
If the arrow is hollow, push it with an instrument that enters that cavity; the arrow will then easily come out. If the arrow is poisoned, it is necessary that you cut away all the flesh in which the poison is, if you can, and then treat it with what is suitable for that.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, abdomen, bladder, or flank, and is near enough to be felt with a probe, and you can incise it, then incise and be careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then suture the wound if it needs suturing, and treat it until it heals.  A picture of the forceps with which arrows are extracted:
Their ends are like a bird's beak; they are made like a file, so that when they grasp the arrow or anything, they do not let go.  Large, small, and medium-sized ones can be made, all according to the size of the arrow and the width and narrowness of the wound.

A picture of the solid pusher:
This has a solid end like an awl to facilitate its entry into the hollow arrow and its pushing.
Chapter ninety-five.
In the incision of veins.
The veins which it is customary to bleed in the body number thirty; of these, sixteen are in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the sensitive ones, and the two arteries in the temples which are apparent, and the two veins in the corners of the eyes known as the visual ones.
And the vein rising in the middle of the forehead, and the vein at the tip of the nose, and the two jugular veins in the neck, and the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth, and the two veins in the lower lipthese veins are known as the jugarksand the two veins under the tongue.
As for the veins which are bled in the arm and hand, they are five veins; one of them is the cephalic, which is on the ulnar side, and the common people call it the vein of the head; and the median, which is the middle vein, and it is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, and the common people call it the vein of the body.
And the basilic, which is situated on the medial side, and it is also called the axillary, and the common people call it the vein of the abdomen; and the vein of the arm, which is situated on the ulna, and to which it adheres, and which appears clearly above the thumb; and the azygos.
And this is the vein which is between the little finger and the ring finger; it has two branches. And in the leg and foot there are three veins: one of them is that which is below the hollow of the knee on the ulnar side; and the second is the saphenous, and its place is at the heel on the medial side; and the vein of the sciatic nerve, and its place is at the heel on the ulnar side.
And in the other leg there are three veins similar to them. As for the two veins which are behind the ears, the benefit of bleeding them is for chronic colds, migraine, ringworm, and chronic, bad sores of the head; and their method of bleeding is as I describe.
And that is to shave the head of the patient and rub his scalp well in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth, then the patient's neck is constricted with a turban until the two veins and their location behind the ears appear in the two lowest places of the head; then you search for them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, there you mark with ink.
Then you take a lancet, which is known as the *nashal*, and you insert it under the vein in the skin until the lancet reaches the bone; then you lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut the vein along with the skin, a cut that is severed, and the length of the cut should be the amount of two closed fingers.
Or something similar, and you let out the amount of blood that you want, then you bind them with bandages and leave them until they heal.  Cutting and cauterizing them has been discussed earlier in the book. As for the two arteries which are in the temples, the benefit of bleeding them is for chronic migraine, severe headache, persistent ophthalmia, and the flow of sharp humors directed towards the eyes.
And their method of bleeding is as I describe to you: the patient's neck is constricted with a turban until the veins appear clearly to the touch, and their pulse becomes apparent under your finger; then you mark with ink. Then you lift the skin from above the vein upwards with your index finger.
And you insert the lancet (*nashal*) from below and lift the vein upwards and sever it as you did with the other two veins, and you let out blood according to your need; then you loosen the patient's neck constriction and place your finger on the vein for a while.
Then you place cotton and a bandage on it and bind it tightly from above and leave it until it heals.  Their mention, cutting, and cauterization have been discussed earlier in the book. As for bleeding the vein of the forehead, its benefit is, after bleeding the cephalic vein, for chronic facial ailments such as ringworm, ulcers and persistent redness.
And the method of bleeding it, as I describe to you: the patient constricts his neck with a turban until the vein appears; then you take the instrument called the axe, and this is its image:
You place the projecting point which is in the head of the axe on the vein itself and strike from above with a comb or something similar, and you let the blood flow to the extent you desire; then you loosen the patient's neck constriction and bind it until it heals.
It may be opened with a wide lancet, but it should not be sharp-pointed like other lancets, rather it should be slightly broad-ended, and you should bleed it obliquely, because the bone is near, and the lancet might break in it if it is thin.
As for the two veins in the temples, their benefit is for eye ailments such as ringworm, redness, and blepharoptosis, and facial diseases. As for the method of bleeding them, it is that the patient constricts his neck with a turban, then you bleed them while you are standing over his head, and let the bleeding be slightly obliquely lengthwise with a small, slightly broad lancet.
For the place has no flesh in it; if the lancet is thin-pointed, it might break. Then you draw off the amount of blood you need and put cotton on them and bind them for one night, then unbind them. As for bleeding the vein of the nose, it is beneficial for acute fevers.
And severe headache and facial diseases such as erysipelas which appears in the nose, especially if it is chronic.  The method of bleeding it is that the patient constricts his neck, then you grasp his nose with your left hand and take a thin, long lancet.
And you insert it in the middle of the nasal septum itself, between the nasal partition, straightly, because the vein does not appear to the touch there. Then the blood will immediately appear. You should push your hand a little with the lancet and draw off the amount of blood you need, then bind it for a night, for it clots quickly.
As for the jugular veins, the benefit of bleeding them is for shortness of breath, the onset of leprosy, and melancholic diseases which appear on the surface of the skin, such as black vitiligo, scabies, bad ulcers and sores.  The method of bleeding them is that the patient constricts beneath them in his neck with a bandage, and the surgeon stands at the head of the patient.
And the patient is seated on a chair, then you bleed the vein lengthwise, a somewhat wide bleeding, then you draw off a moderate amount of blood, or according to what you see is necessary. Then you do likewise with the other vein, then you untie the bandage.
And you constrict the two veins moderately, lest the patient suffocate, and you leave him until the next day, for the wound will heal. As for the veins of the temples, the benefit of bleeding them, after bleeding the cephalic vein, is that it benefits from canker sores in the mouth, corruption of the gums, bad ulcers, cracking of the lips, and bad ulcers.
which are in and around the nose.  The method of bleeding them is that you seat the patient before you and constrict his neck with a turban, then you turn back his lips and look at the two veins, one of which you see to the right of the lip and the other to the left, and you identify them by their blackness.
And that is also because around them are small, black veins; so cut them with a cutting incision. If it is difficult for you and you do not know which they are, then aim to cut the largest and most evident of them.  Likewise, do with the two veins in the lower lip.
As for the two veins under the tongue, the benefit of bleeding them after bleeding the cephalic vein is for the inflammations that occur in the throat from disease of the uvula and diseases of the mouth. The method of bleeding them is that you seat the patient before you facing the sun, and you raise his tongue and look under the tongue, on one side one vein and on the other side another vein, and their color is dark.
Then you bleed them, and be careful not to go too far in cutting them, for beneath them are arteries, and bleeding from those arteries may occur. As for the three veins that are bled in the elbow, they are those which it is customary to bleed in all people, and their bleeding is in two ways.
Either by piercing with a wide, wind-shaped or olive-shaped lancet to the depth of the flesh, or by cutting with a knife-shaped lancet, which is the scalpel [and this is its image], the image of the wide wind-shaped lancet:
It is wide, as you see, so that it is suitable for opening hollow, full, prominent, apparent, thick veins which contain thick, turbid blood. And this is the image of the olive-shaped lancet:
And this lancet is less wide and thinner at the tip; it is suitable for bleeding thin veins which contain thin, yellowish blood. And this is the image of the scalpel lancet:
This is the scalpel which is suitable for cutting, and there are wide and narrow types of it, according to the width and narrowness of the veins also. And one may infer from this [description] others [types of scalpels], and it is well-known to the artisans.
And as for the basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of bleeding it is that it draws blood from the illnesses which are under the throat and neck, from what is next to the chest and abdomen.  It is necessary for the phlebotomist, when bleeding it, to be cautious and be on the alert, for beneath it is an artery. If he errs and goes too deep with the lancet, he will cut that artery.
Then bleeding will occur, therefore it is necessary that its bleeding should not be done with a lancet for puncturing, but its bleeding should be by incision with the scalpel.  If the basilic vein does not appear clearly, then it is necessary to avoid it and turn to another [vein] or seek one of its branches.
Or bleed in its place the vein of the arm, for it is clear, and incise it with the scalpel lancet as we said. If you wish to bleed it itself, then before tightening the arm it is necessary to feel the place until you recognize the place of the pulse, then mark it with ink.
Then you tie the arm and incise the vein with a sharp incision with the scalpel lancet as we said, and take care that the incision is far from the place of the artery. And when you see, upon tightening the bandage, a swelling in the place which you marked with ink, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery, so avoid it.
If you see the blood, upon bleeding, spurt like the urine of a boy, and the blood is thin and red, then know that it is from the artery.  Then immediately place your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger. If the bleeding stopsand it often stopsthen tighten the bandage.
And leave it, and warn the patient against neglecting it, and let him be on his back and not move for several days until it heals. If the bleeding does not stop and it overcomes you and you do not have medicine at hand, then amputate the artery if it appears to you, for its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop.
Or take a pistachio shell and split it, and take one half and bind it tightly to the place of the vein with a bandage and pledgets until the next day. If the bleeding stops, well and good; otherwise, treat it with what was previously mentioned of applying styptics, and stopping its bleeding is not difficult in most cases.
Because of the smallness of the wound and the tightness of the bandage on the arm, know this. As for the basilic vein, the benefit of bleeding it is that it draws blood from the head and the lower body because it is composed of a branch from the basilic and a branch from the cephalic, as we said.
It is necessary for the one who bleeds him to be on his back when bleeding him, for beneath it is a nerve. If he goes too deep with the lancet and injures the nerve, numbness will occur in it which is difficult to cure, and perhaps it will not heal at all. This nerve is often perceptible to the touch.
If it is hidden in some people and is thin and cannot be distinguished, then you should make your incision with a lancet and avoid the nerve as much as possible. If the vein is between two nerves, then split the vein lengthwise. As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of bleeding it is that it draws blood from the head.
It benefits from eye diseases, and it is necessary in this vein especially, if you wish to bleed it, to make an incision with an olive-shaped lancet or with a broad, basil-shaped lancet because it is the safest of all veins, because there is no artery or nerve beneath it.
Except that it is necessary for you, when bleeding, to avoid the head of the muscle with the lancet and seek the soft place.  It does not harm him if he is not hit by the first incision to be bled again several times, except that perhaps swelling will occur in some people if it is not bled on the first incision.
But that swelling does not harm him at all. As for the method of bleeding, its complications, and what should precede in its treatment, the first of that is that you should know that bleeding is either used only to maintain health.
And it is either used in diseases.  That which is used to maintain health and longevity and to guard against the occurrence of diseases is that bleeding should be in one of the three veins in the elbow, I mean the cephalic, the median basilic, and the basilic, and that the bleeding should be at the beginning of spring if signs of plethora appear.
And the bleeding should be on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours of the day have passed. As for bleeding that is used in diseases, it does not have a limited time, but whenever need and necessity demand it, day or night.
And in every hour and at every time.  Children should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age, nor should the elderly who have passed sixty years of age be bled. If someone intends to bleed for whatever reason,
he should cleanse his intestines before bleeding with a gentle enema if there is much retained feces, lest there be drawn into the veins from the intestines foul excesses that harm the principal organs. And one who is feverish, or drunk, or intoxicated should not be bled until that passes from them.
Bleeding should also be avoided after dysentery, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive coitus, exertion, exercise, wakefulness, fasting, and everything that weakens the body, whether physical or mental. Then look to the thinning of the humors beforehand if the blood is thick.
with foods, drinks, and medicines if he can, then he should enter the bath if nothing prevents him, or engage in some exercise so that the blood is thinned and make his bleeding in the middle of the day as we said, and he should intend to clear his chest that day of all bad psychological symptoms.
like worries, anger, and fear, and from all physical symptoms like excessive fatigue and exertion, coitus, and the like.  And he should have present in his session the things that people are accustomed to using, such as various perfumes, fragrant herbs, entertainment, and the like, each person as much as he can.
Then the phlebotomist sits on a cushion higher than the cushion on which the patient sits, then he extends his arm and the phlebotomist rubs it with his hand two or three times, then he tightens the ligature with the shackle and twists it two or three times, and let the tightening be moderate.
For if the tightening is not moderate, either by excessive tightening, it prevents the flow of blood, or if it is relaxed, it also prevents the flow of blood. Then, after tightening, the patient should rub both his hands together.
until the veins swell and become perceptible to the touch. Then the phlebotomist wipes the lancet with a little old oil, especially, then he places the index finger of his left hand on the same vein a little below the place he wants to bleed, lest the vein slip away and avoid the incision. Because some veins you find are like a tendon, they slip away during phlebotomy, and some are full of wind, so when you place the lancet on them, they sink under the lancet and deceive the phlebotomist, and the lancet does not open the vein, or if it opens it, it is only a narrow opening.
Therefore, the phlebotomist should be certain and deliberate in all these matters, then he lowers the lancet; if the vein opens from that incision, otherwise he repeats it again a little below or above that place quickly if the place has not swollen.
If the patient swells or is distressed, leave him for a day or two and do not tighten the ligature, for it may cause a hot swelling, and do not enter the bath, then he may repeat the phlebotomy if he wishes. If the lancet is inserted and the opening is small and the flow of blood is thin,
and you fear that the desired amount of blood will not come out, then return the lancet into the same hole gently and straight, and increase the opening slightly, and do this quickly before the place swells, for in many people the place may swell with a small opening.
But if you see it has swollen, do not repeat it at all, for it will not help you. Place something of oil dregs on it, for it facilitates the flow of blood and is better in this place than the oil itself and other ointments as well.
So use oil dregs in all your phlebotomies when the flow of blood is difficult.  And the theriac Pharun and the shekhzenaya may do this if one of them is placed on the place, for the blood will thin and dissolve if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of phlebotomyand this often happens, especially to someone who has been phlebotomized only that one time or the opening of the vein was smallthen act quickly and place a sponge soaked in slightly warmed salt water on the swelling and bind it for an hour, for it will resolve.
And it is necessary that you do this after the complete exit of blood from the vein itself or from another vein. If something of blackness or greenness remains in the place after days, it does no harm. If you wish, apply something of aloes and myrrh dissolved.
Or something of the juice of the fudung and the like. Often a swelling and a protrusion occur when phlebotomizing the basilic vein. Place your hand on it; if you find it soft when you poke it, then that is a bad protrusion.  Beware of putting anything on it from what we have mentioned.
For it may bleed arterial blood. But bandage it with something astringent to constrict the place, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals. It is necessary that you remove from each person a quantity of blood proportionate to his strength and what appears of the color predominating in the blood.
For if the blood is black, let it flow until it reddens. And likewise, if you see it thick, let it flow until it thins. And likewise if it is sharp, until its sharpness goes away.  It is necessary for someone who is full-bodied and strong and needs to have blood removed all at once...
...to widen the phlebotomy and the lancet should be wide. And for someone who is weak, the opposite. It is necessary that you remove it in several times and that the hole be narrow.  The best thing to use in phlebotomy is something sharp, well-honed, incised, not pierced.
And this type of phlebotomy is safe from bleeding and from cutting a nerve. It is better and safer than phlebotomy across and lengthwise. And if someone is accustomed to fainting during phlebotomy, then it is necessary to give him before phlebotomy something of bread soaked in pomegranate water or oxymel.
If he is feverish and blood is removed from him in three or four times, then if he is cold-natured, let him take before phlebotomy bread soaked in hydromel or in sweetened honey-drink with herbs or in a fragrant rose-water drink.
If fainting occurs during phlebotomy and its cause is the removal of much blood, then it is necessary that he be given meat broth and a light rose-water drink. And he should use fragrant galbanum, and rub his chest with it, and use everything else we mentioned in the section on fainting.
which results from vomiting. And as for someone who wants to relieve his arm and thin his blood again, it is necessary for someone whose phlebotomy was for copious evacuation and whose strength is weak that the blood be thinned little by little according to his strength in successive days.
And as for someone who wants to relieve his arm and thin his blood again and his body is strong, let him do that after seven or nine hours from his first phlebotomy. And as for someone who wants to draw blood from his body to the opposite side from which it inclined, then it is necessary to relieve him on the second or third day.
And as for someone in whose body there is much blood, which has become hot and sharp and caused fever, then it is necessary to remove blood from him in a single instance and remove a large amount, and widen the opening until fainting occurs, after having observed all the conditions of phlebotomy. And that you place your hand on his pulse during the flow of blood, lest death occur instead of fainting, for that often happens if the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligence occurs. And it is not necessary, if you want to open the arm and thin the blood again,
if the mouth of the vein has closed and the exit of blood is difficult, that you prick it forcefully or twist it strongly, for that is very bad. Rather, either you leave it until you phlebotomize it again, or you remove with the blade of the lancet what has frozen of the blood in the mouth of the vein.
Or you apply something of salt dissolved in water, or you apply something of theriac or *shakhzanaya*, and prick it delicately until the blood comes out.  If the vein has swollen, then leave it.
And do not touch it until the swelling subsides. If necessity dictates a second bloodletting, then either you phlebotomize above that place, or you phlebotomize in the other arm or in another vein.
As for phlebotomy of the brachial vein, it is phlebotomized instead of the basilic and cephalic veins if they are not found or are hidden, because it is composed of them.  Its method of phlebotomy is that the patient puts his hand in hot water until the forearm reddens and the vein appears clearly, then it is tied above it with a ligature slightly, with a moderate tightening.
Then you phlebotomize the vein at a slight angle, neither transversely nor longitudinally, and let the opening be wide, and your phlebotomy should be slightly above the wrist joint. If the exit of blood is difficult, return the hand to the vessel of hot water and let the blood flow into the water.
Until it reaches your need. If you are in the summer months, you may dispense with returning the hand to the hot water.  The use of hot water to facilitate blood flow is mostly done in winter. Phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all other veins. Because there is neither a major vein nor a nerve beneath it. As for phlebotomy of the cephalic vein from the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments. Its method of phlebotomy is that you tie the wrist with a ligature or with your hand after putting it in hot water until the vein swells.
Because there is neither a major vein nor a nerve beneath it. As for phlebotomy of the cephalic vein from the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments. Its method of phlebotomy is that you tie the wrist with a ligature or with your hand after putting it in hot water until the vein swells. It becomes very apparent to the touch, then you phlebotomize it at a slight angle; and even if you sever it completely, it will not harm him at all. Be careful not to push your hand deeply with the lancet, for beneath it are the nerves of the fingers, and the location is devoid of flesh. Then return the hand to the hot water.
And leave it to flow.  If you do not return it to hot water, the blood will clot in the opening of the vein and will cease flowing. When you have drawn the required amount of blood, apply fat and salt to the vein, lest it close quickly.
And similarly you should act with every narrow branch. As for the benefit of phlebotomy from the left hand, it is beneficial for ailments of the spleen; and you act in its phlebotomy just as you did in the second, the same. As for phlebotomy of the saphenous vein, its benefit is for diseases in the lower part of the body, such as uterine ailments.
And retention of menses, and kidney diseases, and chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar diseases.  Its method of phlebotomy is that he puts his leg in hot water and you massage it until the veins swell, then you tie above the ankle joint with a ligature, and the vein is located at the heel, apparent towards the great toe.
And many branches extend from it on the instep of the foot.  Therefore, phlebotomize it in the widest branch, or at the heel, at its confluence; this is best and safest. If you phlebotomize it on the instep, then beware of the nerves that lie beneath it on the instep.
And make your phlebotomy of it by twisting, as if you want to cut it off, and let the lancet be sharp. If the blood's exit is difficult, let him return his foot to the hot water and let the blood flow until it is emptied. If the phlebotomist misses the vein at the first attempt...
let him return to phlebotomizing a little higher, for the place is safe and there is no fear of harm from it if you avoid the nerve, as we said. And similarly you act with the saphenous vein of the other leg, the same. As for the sciatic vein, its location, as we said, is at the heel from the lateral side.
And its benefit is for hip pain, if that is from hot blood. And its method of phlebotomy is that you place the patient in the bath and hasten, and you bind his leg from the hip to above the heel with four fingers' breadth of a thin, long bandage, for it does not appear except in this way.
Then, when it appears, phlebotomize it in any way that suits you; either by twisting, which is best, or by cutting it off completely, or by incising it, for its location is safe.  And in most people it is very hidden; if you do not find it...
and it does not appear at all to the touch, then phlebotomize some of its branches, which are those that appear on the dorsum of the foot toward the little finger and ring finger, and be careful of the nerves, and let out the amount of blood you want.
Then loosen the binding and place cotton on the site of phlebotomy and bind the place, for it quickly heals.
Chapter ninety-six.
On cupping and its method of use.
Cupping vessels may be made of horn, wood, copper, or glass, and cupping is of two types: one is cupping with incision and evacuation of blood, and the other is cupping without incision.
And this cupping without incision is of two types.
And cupping of the two sides of the neck, on both sides completely; and cupping of the chin, which is under the lower jaw; and cupping of the shoulders; and cupping of the coccyx, on the sacrum; and cupping of the forearms, which are the middles of the arms; and cupping of the legs; and cupping of the heels.
Cupping draws blood from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and for this reason it does not weaken as much as phlebotomy.  And cupping, whether with fire or without, should not be used in any of the illnesses that result from fullness until the whole body is purged.
If cupping is needed because of illness or custom, we use it at any time, at the beginning of the month, at the end of the month, in the middle of it, and at any time.
That is because some people, when their blood increases to the point that it needs to be removed by cupping, find heaviness and headache in their heads. And some find fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. And some find itching in their face and sides, darkness and pain in their eyes. And some itch at the site of their cupping.
And some laugh a lot. And some find the taste of blood in their mouths, their gums swell, and they spit blood. And some sleep a lot. And some see blood, redness, pus, and wounds in their sleep, and things like that. So whenever we see any of that...
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we order cupping then after two or three hours of the day have passed. As for the benefit of cupping the temples, it benefits from heaviness in the head and water flowing into the eyes.
But it should be after emptying the whole body. And this cupping can be a substitute for phlebotomy of the cephalic vein. And it is warned against using it for someone who has a cold brain or has a cold, for it harms him greatly.
Therefore, the elderly and those with cold diseases in their heads should not use it.  And whoever frequently uses it will develop forgetfulness.
Therefore, you should instruct the cupper to lower his hand with the cupping glass a little lower, for fear of causing forgetfulness. As for cupping the nape of the neck, it is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilic and cephalic veins.
Therefore, it benefits from asthma, shortness of breath, rupture of the respiratory apparatus, cough, and fullness.  The cupping of the nape of the neck should be raised a little, for if it is lowered, it causes weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the temples, it benefits from headaches, ophthalmia, migraine, angina, and toothache. It is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilic vein. You should instruct the cupper not to deepen his hand in the incision, lest he cut a vein and cause bleeding.
As for cupping under the chin, it benefits from thrush in the mouth, corruption of the gums, and similar diseases in the mouth. It may replace phlebotomy of the veins in the lips. As for cupping the shoulders, it benefits from palpitations that result from fullness and heat.
As for cupping the forearms, it benefits from what benefits phlebotomy of the three veinsthe basilic, cephalic, and medianbecause it draws blood from all those small veins in the flesh, and it draws those small veins from other thicker veins until the drawing reaches the three large veins.
You should instruct the cupper not to deepen the incision, because the area is devoid of flesh and beneath it are nerves and arteries. As for the single cupping applied to the coccyx, it benefits from hemorrhoids and lower sores.
You should instruct the cupper that the cupping glass be large and made of copper, because the area requires strong suction, and a glass cupping glass might break and require much incision. As for cupping the legs, it visibly reduces fullness.
Because it draws blood from the whole body, it benefits from chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder; it promotes menstruation; it benefits from boils and abscesses; and it replaces phlebotomy of the saphenous veins and the veins of the ankles, except that it greatly weakens the body and causes fainting in most people.  The benefit of cupping the ankles is similar to the benefit of cupping the legs.
The method of applying cupping glasses is that the cupping glass is first placed empty and moderate suction is applied. Do not prolong the application of cupping glasses, but rather apply them quickly and remove them quickly so that the humors flow to the area evenly. Continue to repeat this until you see the area has reddened and opened.
When the redness of blood appears, then incise and resume suction slowly. Then look at the condition of the bodies; if a person has thin flesh with porous skin, then you should incise only once, lest the area ulcerate.
And instruct the cupper to widen the incision and deepen it slightly, and to adjust the suction gently and with a light movement. If the blood is thick, then it should be incised twice; in the first incision, to open a path for the thin watery blood, and in the second, to completely extract the thick blood.
If the blood is very turbid, repeat the incision a third time to achieve the goal. In short, if we want to extract a little blood, we are content with one incision; if we want to extract a lot of blood, we make more incisions, especially if we judge the blood to be thick.
It is necessary that we make a deep incision; the moderate limit in the incision is only the depth of the skin. What ointments should be used when applying cupping glasses, and what waters, and what the cupped person should avoid. As for someone whose skin is thick, hard, dry, and whose pores are narrow,
then the places of the cupping glasses should be anointed with opening, softening, resolving ointments; and if it is in the summer time, then with such as the oil of hypericum, or oil of violet, or oil of sweet almond, or oil of pumpkin seed.
And if it is in the winter, then such as oil of narcissus, or oil of iris, or oil of chamomile, or lily, and the like. If the waste is thick and cold, let the ointment be oil of germander, or oil of savory, or oil of pennyroyal, or oil of rue, and the like.
If the cupped person has wide pores and soft flesh, then the ointment should be avoided. These should wash their cupping places after cupping with rose water, or with cold water, or with water of fox grapes, or with gourd water, or with water of chickweed, and the like.  As for someone whose blood is very moist,
he should wash his cupping places with vinegar, or with water of asafoetida and sumac, and the like. As for someone whose waste is thick, he should wash his cupping places with old wine, or with water of germander, or a decoction of rue, or chamomile, and the like.  Cupping should be avoided in the bath and after the bath.
Rather, it should be used after leaving the bath by an hour or two. No one should sleep after cupping.  What the cupped and bled person should manage before and after cupping.
First, it must be considered: if the cupped or bled person is choleric, and sharpness and inflammation predominate in his blood, then he should take refrigerants such as pomegranate and dandelion with vinegar, lettuce, oxymel, and sherbet, and the like. And he should make his foods of fowl and lamb stews, and sour things, and the like.
And if his temperament is cold, then he should be given honeyed drinks and drinks of myrobalan or seed oxymel, and he should consume moderately perfumed wine, that which is between old and new. He should be ordered to eat little, and he should make his food of fowl, partridges, sparrows and squabs, stews.  On the day of cupping and bleeding, drink should be more than food.
And it may be necessary to give some people, at certain times, some of the theriac of Avicenna, or musk medicine, or silphium, before cupping and before or after bleeding, to strengthen the principal organs and thin the blood.  It should not be given to those who are feverish.
As for cuppings which are without a condition, they are those which are placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, the region of the kidneys, and the hip joint, for these organs do not tolerate being conditioned, but they are desired either to draw blood from one organ to another.
Such as placing the cupping glass on the breasts in a case of epistaxis, or we use it to remove a cold humor which has settled in an organ, such as placing the cupping glass on the abdomen and navel, for it loosens the organ, warms it, and removes the pain by resolving that humor.
It may be placed on the kidneys if there is an obstruction or stone present there, for by the strength of its drawing it may open the obstruction or dislodge the stone from its place.  Likewise it acts if it is placed on the liver and spleen when there is a humor that disturbs them.  These cuppings may be used empty, by suction only.
It may be used with fire, or it may be used filled with lukewarm water in cases of inflammation. That is, fill the cupping glass, and let it be large, with hot water alone, or with water in which some herbs suitable for this purpose have been boiled.
Then it is placed full on the place, held, and removed, and repeated several times according to need. And this is the image of the cupping glass which is used with fire:
Its mouth should be the width of two open fingers, as we have pictured, and its depth half a span. And there should be in its side, about halfway up, a small hole about as large as an awl can enter. It is made of Chinese copper or yellow copper, with a thick rim.
Smooth, even, and polished, lest it injure the member when placed upon it. And in its middle there should be a transverse groove of copper or iron, where the burning candle is placed. This cupping glass may be made larger than we have described or smaller, according to the diseases.
And the manner of its use is that the cupping glasses for boys and thin people are not the same as those for men and stout-bodied people. As for the manner of placing this cupping glass with fire on the member, it is to prepare a burning wick of tightly woven linen or a small candle of pitch and to place it on the middle of the solidified rod which is in the middle of the cupping glass.
So that the ascent of the fire will be upwards towards the bottom of the cupping glass, lest it burn the body of the patient. Then it is placed on the member, and the finger on the hole which we mentioned, until, when we have cupped as much as we need, the finger is removed.
And the vapor will exit through that hole, and the cupping glass will adhere to the place. Then prepare the wick in the manner described and replace it if you need to.  As for the cupping glass which is used in the treatment of phlegm with water, it does not have a solidified rod nor a hole.
It is only used by being filled with water and placed on the member.  And this is its form:
And this cupping glass, the larger it is so that it holds much water, the better it is.
Chapter ninety-seven.
On the suspension of the leech.
Leeches are used mostly in cases where cupping glasses cannot be applied, either because of the small size of the member, like the lip and gum and the like, or because the member is devoid of flesh, like the finger and nose and the like.
And the method of using them is that you should choose leeches which are in fresh, pure water, free from impurities, then leave them for a day and a night in fresh water until they are hungry and nothing remains in their bellies. Then the body is first purged by bloodletting or cupping, then the affected member is wiped.
Until it reddens, then it is applied; and when it is full, it falls off. And if it is possible to cup the place, it is more effective; otherwise, wash the place with vinegar, then with plenty of water, and rub and squeeze it.  If the bleeding continues after the leech falls off, and it is a trickle, then let a linen cloth be dipped in cold water.
And place it on top until the trickle stops. If the bleeding is profuse, sprinkle on it powdered vitriol or gallnuts or the like of astringents until the bleeding stops, or apply halves of peeled lupins to the place.
And leave it until the lupin adheres to the place, for then the blood will stop.  And it is preferable, if it is necessary to reapply leeches, not to use those leeches if others are available.  If the leeches refuse to attach, let the place be wiped with fresh blood.
Or stick a needle into the place until some blood comes out, then apply them; for when they sense some blood, they will attach themselves. And if you want to make them fall off, sprinkle on them some aloes or salt or ashes, for then they will fall off.
Chapter Three.
On Setting (bones).
This chapter also is from and about what is needed in the art of medicine, which is the setting of fractures and dislocations occurring in bones.
Know, O my sons, that this chapter is claimed by the ignorant among physicians and the common people, and by those who have never looked at any book of the ancients on it, nor read a single word thereof.
For this reason, this art of knowledge has become nonexistent in our country, and I have never encountered anyone at all who was skilled in it. And I only derived what I have derived from my long study of the books of the ancients and my eagerness to understand them, until I extracted knowledge of it from them; then I devoted myself to experimentation and practice throughout my life.
And I have described for you in this chapter everything that my knowledge encompasses of it. And my experience has passed over it after I have brought it closer to you and freed it from the branches of prolixity, and shortened it to the utmost, and clarified it to the utmost, and depicted for you therein many images of the instruments used in it.
For it is from an increase in explanation, as I did in the preceding two chapters, and there is no strength except in God, the Most High, the Most Great.
The first chapter.
In it are summaries and collections concerning the matter of fractured bones, and it is necessary to present them. And before we begin to mention the fractured and dislocated members one by one, it is necessary that we mention at the beginning of this chapter summaries of speech and sections. It compels you first to understand them and stand upon their truth, you and whoever else is eager to learn this noble craft.
Then I say that when a fracture or dislocation or wound or fall occurs to someone, it is necessary to hasten first to bleed him or purge him or both. Unless something prevents it, such as weakness of strength, or the one to whom this has happened is a child or a very old man, or the time is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then he should restrict his diet to cold vegetables and the meat of birds and kids, and he should avoid drinks and strong meats and overfilling with food, and every food that fills the veins with blood, until the swelling is safe and the flow of material to the place is not expected.
Then let the patient return to his first regimen to which he is accustomed.  When the fractured bone begins to knit, the patient should be nourished with foods that provide abundant, thick, strong nourishment, which possesses viscosity, such as porridges, rice, heads, necks, and cow tripe. And eggs and soft fish and thick drinks and the like, for by this regimen the consolidation of the fracture will be quicker and better. And know that fractured bones, if they are in strong men and old men, cannot ever unite and join together in their first nature.
Because of the dryness of their bones and their hardness. And it may unite and join what was of bones in the utmost softness, like the bones of small children. But nature produces on the fractured bone from all its sides something resembling glue, thick, which adheres to it and tightens it until some of it adheres to the other.
And it binds some of it to the other until it comes to the utmost strength and firmness as it was at first, until nothing hinders him from his actions.
For this reason it is necessary to give the patient foods which possess solidity, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said. And know that fractures differ in their types according to the differences of the members, for a fracture of the tibia is different from a fracture of the skull, and a fracture of the chest is different from a fracture of the back.
And so all the other members are different from one another. And we shall come to the mention of each type of fracture, explained in its chapter, detailed from the others. And the type of fracture of the bone may also differ in itself.
For it may be its fracture a splintering without fragments occurring in it, and it may be its fracture along the length of the bone, and its fracture may have fragments and excrescences, separated and not separated. And the fracture may be with a wound and a perforation in the skin, and the fracture may be a slight crack.
And for each type there is a special method in its setting, as will be mentioned in detail in its place. And of what makes known the fracture of the bone is its crookedness, swelling, and its appearance to the touch, and its creaking when you probe it with your hand.
Then when there is not in the place an apparent crookedness, nor creaking, nor do you feel upon touching the bone a disturbance, nor does the patient feel much pain, then there is not a fracture, but it is possible that it is a sprain, or a slight fracture, or a slight crack.  Therefore, it is not necessary to move it by stretching and probing at all.
Rather, apply to it of the medicines whose mention will come after a while, what is suitable for the place, then bind it gently. And know that if the bone is fractured and broken into two without fragments occurring in it, then each part has only separated from its fellow.
Then it is necessary for you to hasten at once to straighten and align it before a hot swelling occurs to it.  If a hot swelling occurs to it, then leave it for days until the hot swelling subsides, then align it in whatever way you are able to do so gently and skillfully.
And know that its setting and alignment is easier than a bone in which fragments have occurred. Then it is bound as will be mentioned. If the bone has fragments in it, then it is necessary to stretch the broken limb from both sides, whether hand or foot.
Either with your hands if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with a rope and the hand. And let your placing of the limb be on a level surface in its natural shape, until when the parts of the broken bone are extended, then return those fragments to their places in whatever way you are able to do so skillfully and gently.
And strive with all your effort that you do not cause the patient pain or suffering by your actions, and strive with all your effort to unite one of the bones with its fellow in the best manner.
