And it is necessary at that time to touch and feel them with your hand; and if you see there something contrary, correct and align it to the best of your ability.
And beware of strong stretching and forceful probing, as many ignorant people do; and they often cause by their actions a hot swelling or inflammation in the limb, as I have witnessed repeatedly from their actions.
Then, after the alignment, perfection, and binding, keep that limb still and quiet, and warn the patient not to move it while awake or asleep, and during his turning and restlessness, and during his defecation and all his movements, to the best of his ability, and that he should strive to keep the limb in a position where pain is avoided.
And that is because, whenever he feels pain or suffering in the position of the limb, he should move it to another position where he does not feel pain, and he should strive to make that position even and straight.
Lest crookedness occur in the limb if it is set, and as for the manner of binding the broken limb, it is as I describe to you. Know that broken limbs differ in their smallness and largeness and their shapes; and those that are small, such as the arm, finger, forearm, and the like.
Then it is necessary that the bandages of the cloth be soft and gentle, and those that are thick, such as the thigh, back, and chest, then it is necessary that the bandages be wide and firm, for the wide bandage is necessary for the large limb, and it tightens it from every side with equal tightness, so that no defect intervenes.
Then, after you have finished the alignment, you begin to apply the appropriate plaster. In a soft manner on the place of the fracture, then you begin to wrap the bandage on the place of the fracture itself, three or four turns, according to what the limb requires, and you tighten your hand a little with the bandage, then you take it to the upper side of the place of the fracture.
With less tightening than your tightening of the broken place, then you spread out the wrapping from the place of the fracture a little, and loosen the tightening little by little until you take from the correct place something suitable, then you take another bandage and wrap it also on the broken place.
turns, then you take the bandage to the lower side of the fracture, and let your action in tightening and loosening the bandage be as we mentioned in the first upper bandage, then you place between the bandages from the soft lint or cloths what evens out the crookedness of the fracture.
If there is crookedness, otherwise do not put anything in it, then you wrap another bandage around it, then you align on these bandages the firm splints at once if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or hot inflammation, then apply what calms that inflammation and removes the swelling, and leave it for days, then tighten the splints on it at that time, and let the splints be from wide, carved, skillfully prepared halves of reeds, or let the splints be from sieve wood.
which are made from pine or palm leaves or  reed or  boxwood and similar things which are available, and let the width of each splint be in this form and this shape exactly.
Except that the splint which is placed on the fracture itself should be a little thicker and wider than the other splints, and as for the length of the splint, it is made according to what suits the broken limb in its size and smallness.  Image of the splint:
Then you tighten the splints with another bandage according to your first tightening exactly, then you tie from above with strong threads according to what we mentioned of the tightening, which is that your tightening on the place of the fracture should be more. And the farther it is from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And the threads should be moderate in thickness and thinness, and let them be of wet linen especially, because if the threads are thick, as I have seen done by the ignorant, who make their threads from twisted linen strips, this is a great mistake.
Because the tightening with them becomes excessive, and thin threads are also unsuitable because you do not achieve with them the tightening you want, and there should not be less than a finger between the splint and the splint.
If the patient is harmed by the edges of the splints after tightening in the healthy places, then place under them soft tow or fluffy wool so that nothing harms him from that. As for if the fracture is with a wound and a rupture in the skin occurs, we will mention it separately.
Know that not every broken limb should be tightened with splints from the first day, and that if the limb is large, then the splints should not be placed on it except after five or seven days or more, according to your assurance of the absence of hot swelling.
And this is a description of the bandages with which the ancients used to bind fractures, dislocations, and sprains.  A description of a common, concise bandage that binds fractures and is suitable for most temperaments, especially children and women, because it is something that is not overcome by heat or cold.
And it is that you take from the dust of the mill, which is the core of the flour that adheres to the walls of the mill during the movement of the millstone, then knead it as it is without sifting it, with egg white, and make its kneading neither thick nor thin, then apply it.
A description of another bandage that binds fractures, dislocations, and sprains: Take of mastic, leadwort, acacia, orris root, gum arabic, and sugar, ten drachms of each; of myrrh and aloes, five drachms of each; of acacia, twenty drachms; and of Armenian or Roman clay, twenty drachms. Pound all together and mix with acacia water or with egg white if the patient's temperament is hot, then use this bandage; for it is of good composition, quickly binds broken bones, and is suitable for most people because of its moderation.
A description of another bandage that is also useful for fractures and sprains: Take gum arabic, mastic, and white leadwort, ten drachms of each; myrrh and aloes, five drachms of each; acacia, six drachms; Armenian clay, twenty drachms. Pound all together finely, sift, and knead with water or egg white and use.
A description of a bandage for joints and bones that have shifted from their positions, and which alleviates the pain arising from them, and from bone fractures and breaks: Take carded wool, dip it in vinegar and cooked oil, and place it on the affected area. This bandage has no binding strength.
But it is excellent in relieving hot swelling and especially in relieving pain. A description of another bandage that binds broken bones: Take the leaves of the black mulberry and the leaves of wild poppy, pound them together, and apply them moist. A description of another brief bandage used when a bone fracture is being set.
And you want to resolve the remainder of the swelling, take of the root of leadwort, chamomile, violet flowers, and crushed lentils, one part of each. Pound everything together and knead it with white of egg if the member is not hot; but if it is hot, knead it with coriander water or with water and apply it.
A description of another bandage stronger in resolution than the first, used when a hard swelling occurs when a bone is being set: Take of the root of leadwort, flaxseed, fenugreek, rosemary, germander, violet flowers, and chamomile, one part of each.
Pound everything together and knead it with vinegar water or with fresh water or with white of egg, all according to the heat of the member and the state of its inflammation. As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that it should be placed on the broken member when it is being set.
Except for the cerate made from wax and oil only, and he described it as being moderate between thickness and thinness. As for Galen, he thought that things which contain dryness along with some heat, such as myrrh, aloes, frankincense, and what we have mentioned, should be placed on the broken member when it is being set.
A description of a bandage which benefits weakness and pain: Take of comfrey, chickpea flour, human hair clippings or bird feathers, leadwort, and salt, equal parts. Pound, sift, knead, and bandage with it. As for the amount which the bandage should remain
Then loosen it; this is to look: if no pain, itching, or movement of the broken bone from its place occurs in the affected part, do not loosen it for many days.  If severe itching or distressing pain or swelling occurs in the place
then hasten to loosen it at once and do not delay that at all, and remove the bandage from it. Then take a soft rag or a wet sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the place with it until the itching and pain subside, then leave the member to rest for an hour.
Then apply wool, moistened and dipped in vinegar and oil or rose oil, and bandage it for a night, until you are safe from hot swelling, and the swelling of the member subsides and its pain goes away. Then return it to gentle tightening and a light bandage, and do not tighten it as in the first instance.
And treat it gently until it heals. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and puffiness, and all the symptoms have completely gone, and you need a bandage and tightening, then prepare it as you did at first, equally. If nothing of what we have mentioned occurs in the member
then do not loosen it except after three, four, five, or seven days, and it may be left for twenty days; all this depends on what appears to you of the state of the member, as we said, until the fracture is set and the flesh approaches consolidation around it.
Then also increase the tightening more than your first tightening, and also increase the thickening of the patient's food to what was previously mentioned. If you see that the place of the fracture has dried and become thinner than it should be, then know that food is prevented from reaching it.
Then bathe it with lukewarm water each time you loosen it, and let that be every three days, and loosen the tightening a little. For by this action, food flows to the member and it heals quickly. As for what ignorant bone-setters do, of breaking the member again
if it is not first set properly, and is set crookedly, that is an error of their action and a great deception. And if it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and acted upon it, and I have not seen any trace of it from any of them at all. and the correct thing is not to do it.
Chapter Two.
Concerning fractures occurring in the head.
The types of fractures occurring in the head are numerous, their forms varied, and their causes diverse.  Some fractures are caused by a sword blow, and this may either completely sever the bone until it reaches the membrane beneath the bone. Just as an axe does to wood.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called an axe-like fracture.  Or else, the sword may cut some of the bone, severing only its surface, and the cut not penetrating to its end; this type of fracture is called a complete avulsion. The wound in these two fractures may be either large or small.
And some fractures are comminuted or contused; their cause being a blow with a stone, or a fall onto a stone, or something similar. This fracture may either penetrate and nearly reach the membrane beneath the bone, or else be located on the surface of the bone.
And the wound in these two fractures is also either large or small.  And some fractures are hidden in the bone, as thin as a hair, being a slight fissure.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called a hair-like fracture. And there is a fracture which results from a fall or a blow from a stone or the like, which drives a section of the bone inwards, and makes a concavity in that place, as happens to a copper pot when struck, a part of it being driven inwards.
This occurs most frequently in the soft bones of the head, such as the heads of children.  All these types of fracture may have loose or impacted fragments. We shall describe the treatment of all of them in their proper place, and we shall recognize all these types of fracture by examining them.
And examining them with probes, and removing the corrupted flesh from them. As for the hair-like type, it is recognized by exposing the bone, and wiping it and smearing it with ink; then the fracture will appear black. As for the treatment of fracture, first look at the symptoms of the patient.
If you see in his symptoms that which clearly indicates danger, such as vomiting bile, convulsions, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, sinking of the eyes and redness, and similar symptoms, then do not approach the patient nor treat him, for death is inevitable in most cases with these symptoms.
But if you see symptoms that do not alarm you and you hope for his recovery, then begin his treatment.  And if the wounded man comes to you at the very beginning of his injury and it is in the winter.
Then you should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case. And if it is in the summer, then you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the membrane under the bone is corrupted, and those symptoms which we described appear.
If the fracture of the bone has reached the membrane covering the brain, and there is shattering and bruising, then you should cut away the shattered and bruised part as I describe to you. That is, shave the head of the wounded patient.
And uncover the bone in whatever way is possible for you, and according to the shape of the wound and what is easiest for the patient.  If, upon uncovering the bone, you find bleeding or a hot swelling, then treat that as is appropriate.
That is, pack the area with cloths soaked in a syrup and rose oil, and dress the wound until the swelling subsides and the bleeding stops. Then begin to scrape away and remove the bone; this can be done in one of two ways.
The first method is to cut the bone with a fine, narrow-bladed saw.  And this is its image:
Then use another saw, slightly wider than the first. And this is its image:
Then also use another saw, wider still than the second.  It is necessary that you have several saws of different sizes, some wider than others, and some shorter than others, and that they be extremely sharp. Let them be made of Indian steel or good quality iron.
And use gentle strokes with the saw, lest you shake the head and injure it. If the bone is strong and hard, it is necessary that you bore holes around it before using the saws, with the drills called non-immersive drills, or rather, they are called non-immersive drills.
Because they do not go beyond the edge of the cranial bone to what is beyond it, because the drill has a round edge below its sharp head, similar to a ring or small circle, preventing it from penetrating and going beyond the thickness of the bone.
And you should have many of these drills, each suitable for the thickness of that bone, so that you have for every skull a drill whose sharp point is appropriate in length and shortness to the thickness of that skull. And this is an image of three types. of the drills: large, medium, and small.
As for the method of drilling around the fractured bone, it is that you place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated, then you move the drill to another place, and you make the distance between each hole about the thickness of an awl or similar.
Then you cut between each two holes with the cutters, and you do this with the utmost gentleness as we said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with something else from some of the instruments that you have prepared for that.
such as forceps and fine hooks. And you should be very careful that the drill or cutter does not touch anything of the peritoneum. If the bone is removed and is clear of the peritoneum, if it was adhering to it, and you have taken it out, then it should be cleaned.
And you smooth the roughness of what remains of the bone with another instrument similar to the cutter, except that it should be thinner and finer than the other cutters. If any small bones or fragments remain, then take them gently with the instruments you have.
Then you treat the wound afterwards with cauterization and ointments which will be mentioned. As for the other method of the procedure, it is a very easy method, far from danger; Galen mentioned it and praised it greatly, and this is what he said. You should first begin by exposing the part of the bone where the fracture is most severe and prominent, until, when you have exposed that part, you place the end of this lenticular cutter under it, and this is its image:
The lenticular part of it is smooth, so that it does not cut anything, and the sharp part of it is on its sides going lengthwise, as you see, so that the lenticular part rests against the peritoneum and the face of the sharp cutter on the bone, then you strike the cutter from one side.
with a small hammer until you cut all the bone gently as it turns, and you are safe from the membrane, and nothing frightening happens to it at all, even if the surgeon were the most ignorant and cowardly of people, yes, even if he were sleepy.
If anything of the membrane remains attached to the bone in some parts of the bone, then scrape it off with the end of the lenticular cutter itself and detach it gently, for it will detach without harm or fear.
But if the fracture of the bone did not penetrate to the membrane, and what was broken of the bone was its surface, and roughness and small fragments remained in it, then it is necessary to remove that roughness and extract those fragments with fine forceps, of which various sizes have been made, so that you can use in each place the most suitable and appropriate of them, according to what the work leads you to.
and the broken bone itself and its shape, and you should use first in your scraping of the bone the widest of those forceps, then use after it a finer one, and continue to do that successively until you come to use the finest and thinnest of them all.
As for the other small, hair-like fissures and slight fractures, it is necessary to use for each one a treatment according to what leads to its improvement, which is something not hidden from anyone who has the least skill in this craft and has understood what we have written and explained in the large fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you extract the bone, then you should take a linen cloth the size of the wound and dip it in rose oil and place it on the mouth of the wound, then take another folded or triangular cloth. and dip it in a syrup and rose oil and place it on the first cloth, and do this as gently as you can, lest you weigh down the peritoneum, then use a wide bandage on top and do not tighten it except to the extent that it only holds the wound.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are safe from the hot swelling, then untie it and use afterwards the medicines which have the virtue of drying, such as the roots of iris, and crushed birthwort, and crushed spikenard and zedoary and the like.  And you should make from these medicines powders.
And sprinkle them on the wound as they are dry. And in general, use in that a medicine which cleanses and does not burn, and strive with all your effort during your treatment of the wound that it be clean, that there be no excess of oil, nor dirt, and do not allow pus to accumulate in it at all.
Because if pus collects on the membrane of the brain it spoils and corrupts it, and from that a great calamity befalls the patient, and it may occur in the peritoneum of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if one neglects its treatment.
Blackness on its surface. If the patient shows the symptoms which we have mentioned, then know that he is inevitably doomed. And if the blackness occurred only from a medicine which was placed on it, and it was in the power of that medicine to cause that blackness.
Then you should take one part of honey and three parts of rose oil, and mix them well, and smear them on a cloth, then place it on the peritoneum, then treat it with the kinds of treatment which are necessary until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On the setting of a broken nose.
Know that there is no fracture of the nose except its upper two parts, both of them, or one of them, because they are bones, for the lower part of it is cartilaginous and does not fracture, but rather there occurs to it contusion, crookedness, and flattening.
If one of its parts is fractured, then it is necessary to insert the little finger into the nostril and to set that fracture from the inside with your index finger and thumb from the outside, until you return the nose to its natural shape, and let that be done by you gently, and be careful that your action does not cause the patient pain.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and the finger does not reach it, then it should be set with the end of a probe of moderate thickness. If the fracture is on both sides, do likewise, and let him attend to its setting on the first day of the fracture.
If possible; otherwise, after the seventh or tenth day, when the hot swelling subsides, then insert into the nostril a tent of linen rag if the fracture is on one side, or insert two tents if the fracture is on both sides.
Let the tents be of a thickness such that they fill the nostril.  Some of the early bone-setters mentioned that the tents should be moistened with fat and changed every day, but I do not think that is so; rather, the tents should be moistened with egg white mixed with mill dust.
Then leave the tents until the bone sets and the cartilage hardens.  Goose quills, after having been wrapped with soft rags, may be inserted into the nose at the place of the tents; this will make the retention of the fractured nose firmer and will prevent the patient from being unable to breathe.
This is not necessary; if you wish, do it; if you wish, make tents. If hot swelling should occur in the nose during your work, apply a compress to the nose with  kypros or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil or some diachylon ointment.
If no hot swelling occurs, then you should dress it externally with fine semolina and powdered myrrh kneaded with egg white, then apply a soft pad, and do not bind the nose at all if the nasal bones are fractured only slightly.
Or if they are shattered, you should incise over them and remove them with the appropriate instrument, then suture the incision and treat it with appropriate ointments which heal and cicatrize. If a wound occurs inside the nose, you should treat it with tents and use lead tubes. until it heals.
Chapter Four.
On the setting of the lower jaw if it is fractured.
If the lower jaw is fractured and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, then if the fracture is only from the outside and is not fractured in two parts and is not depressed inwards, then its identification is easy.
Then it is necessary, if the fracture is in the right side, that you insert the index finger of the left hand into the mouth of the patient, and similarly if the fracture is in the left side, then insert the index finger of the right hand and gently raise the prominence of the fracture from the inside to the outside.
And with your other hand from outside the bone, you control it to straighten it. If the fracture of the jaw has been broken into two, then it is necessary to use traction from both sides in a straight line until you are able to straighten it. If it has happened in the teeth, loosen them.
Or separate them, then tie what you hope of them to remain with a gold, silver, or silk thread. Then place a splint on the fractured jaw, then place a folded cloth over it, and place over the cloth a tight large splint or a piece of leather the length of the jaw. Then tie it from above according to what suits you to tie it and agrees with its binding so that it does not become undone, and order the patient to rest and be still, and make his food soft drinks. If you think that something of the shape has changed in any way, then hasten to untie it on the third day.
Then tie it from above according to what suits you to tie it and agrees with its binding so that it does not become undone, and order the patient to rest and be still, and make his food soft drinks. If you think that something of the shape has changed in any way, then hasten to untie it on the third day. Then you repair what you have bandaged with mill dust mixed with egg white or with semolina flour after removing the splint from it, and place a soft poultice on the bandage. As long as that bandage adheres to it and the condition of the bone does not change, leave it.
Do not untie it until it heals and the fracture solidifies. Often this fracture solidifies in three weeks. If a hot swelling occurs during that time, use what we have repeatedly mentioned to soothe it until that swelling goes away.
And as for if the fracture is with a wound, look; if a splinter or splinters have separated from the bone, be gentle in extracting those splinters with whatever instrument suits you for their extraction. If the mouth of the wound is narrow, widen it with the scalpel to the extent of your need.
Then, when you have extracted those splinters and none remain, stitch the wound if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the ointments suitable for that. And stitch the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On the treatment of the clavicle if it is fractured.
The clavicle is most often fractured in front, at the end of the shoulder, and its fracture is one of three types: either it is fractured and broken into two without splinters, which is easiest to set;
or splinters occur in the fracture, which is more difficult to set; or the fracture is accompanied by a wound.  The procedure, if the fracture is without a wound, is that you prepare two assistants, one to hold the arm next to the fractured clavicle, and the other to extend the neck towards the other side.
Then you set the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be, and there is no protrusion or irregularity. If you need more extension, you should place under the patient's armpit a ball of rags or wool.
And its bone will be to your need, and you extend and raise the clavicle and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is set as it should be. If you cannot pull the end of the clavicle outwards because it has gone inwards, then the patient should lie on his back.
And a moderately sized pillow is placed under his shoulder, and the assistant presses his shoulder downwards until the clavicle bone which is deep rises upwards. Then you set the fracture and align it with your fingers.
If you feel that a splinter of the clavicle has broken and is moving, you should incise over it and remove that splinter gently. If the splinter is lodged in the bone, try to cut it with one of the cutting instruments prepared for that purpose. After you place under the clavicle the instrument which supports the peritoneum, which is an instrument of wood or iron, and this is its image:
It resembles a spoon without a curve; its width is according to the size of the bone, whether large or small; and as for its length, it is also according to what allows you to work. And let it have two ends, as you see; one end wider and the other narrower.
If the wound opening which you incised when removing the bone splinter is wide and you are safe from hot inflammation, then join the edges of the wound by suturing.  If the opening is small or you fear hot inflammation, then cauterize the wound with a cautery and styptics, according to the extent of the incision.
If hot inflammation appears, moisten the cautery in rose oil and vinegar or wine and apply it. As for setting the bone if it is without a wound or incision, it is to apply to the bone a plaster made from mill dust with egg white. And you place the soft splint on it, then place the ball under his armpit if he needs that. Then take a bandage that is very long, and its width is a cubit or so. Then apply a folded compress over the splint and plaster, then take a splint from a thin board. Let its width be three fingers and its length likewise. Then wrap it in a cloth, then wrap that cloth together with the splint in the part of the bandage that lies on the place of the fracture, then tighten the bandage on the fracture as you wrap it. And pass it over his neck and under his sound armpit and under his injured armpit, and wrap it repeatedly on each side, and as you see that the tightening controls the fracture securely, which is not hidden from you.
And the aim of all this is that the splint does not move from the broken bone. Therefore, you should check on the patient every day; whenever the bandage loosens and you see that the splint has moved, correct that and tighten the bandage. Then have the patient sleep on his back, and place a small pillow under his armpit when he sleeps at night.
So that his arm may be raised from his side, and the clavicle fracture will be raised with the raising of the shoulder; or tie his arm to his neck. And do not untie the bandage unless something happens at the site, such as itching or swelling, until twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see a reason for it. And return to the tightening, and leave it until the clavicle fracture heals and consolidates, and it strengthens and becomes more solid in twenty-eight days. And it may be in some people in less than that.
Chapter Six.
Concerning the setting of a fracture of the humerus.  The humerus rarely fractures in its wide part; rather, its ends fracture.  When a part of it is fractured or it is fractured in its middle, this is known by touch.  Then, according to what the shape of the fracture is, treat it accordingly.
Straighten it and return it to its natural shape in every way you can, then apply to the area mill dust with egg white and soft lint. Place on top of that a pad of folded cloth, then apply a wide splint of wood.
Thin enough to fit the whole shoulder or slightly wider. If there is a lump under the splint at any point on the shoulder, smooth out that lump with soft lint until the splint lies flat, then bind it from above with a long bandage.
Bind it tightly and ensure the splint does not move from its place. Check the bandage every day; whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it and straighten the splint if it has moved. Let the patient lie on his healthy side. The shoulder heals in twenty [days].
days or twenty-five days. When this time has been completed, remove the bandage safely, for it is among the bones which are not feared for shattering or displacement. If a fragment of bone protrudes and is pressing against the skin, incise it and remove it. And do what was mentioned in the fracture of the clavicle to soothe the hot swelling if anything of that occurs.
Chapter Seven.
On the setting of a fracture of the chest.
The chest may be fractured in its middle, and this rarely occurs; but its extremities are more frequently fractured and shattered. Among its symptoms, if its middle is fractured, is that it inclines downwards, and there occurs severe pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing.
And perhaps blood is expelled and the depression in the fractured bone is apparent and not hidden from touch.  Its treatment is that the patient lies on his back, and a pillow is placed between his shoulders, then his shoulders are compressed, and the ribs are brought together with the hands from both sides, and gentleness is used in straightening it in every way that is possible.
And continue until the shape of the bone is restored as it should be; then apply a bandage and a splint, and place over it a splint made of a thin board of willow or elm or the like, that is light, after wrapping it in a cloth. Then gently bind it to the fractured bone lest it shift, and pass the bandage around to the back several times, and tighten it firmly; then examine the bandage at all times, and whenever it loosens, tighten it.
And if necessity dictates its removal because of an abscess that appears in the area, or pain, or swelling, then proceed to remove it, and take off the bandage, and treat whatever has occurred by its appropriate treatment, then reapply the bandage if you see fit to do so. And face it and keep it tight until it heals.
Chapter Eight.
Concerning the setting of ribs when they are fractured: Know that fractures of the ribs occur in the thickest parts which are next to the back, and their ends in front are subject to contusion because they are cartilaginous; and the knowledge of this is not hidden from the sense upon examination with the fingers.
And its setting is that you straighten the fracture with the fingers in the accessible way until the shape is made even as it should be, then bandage it, and bind the fractured bone with a splint if it needs that.  If the fracture of the ribs is inclined inwards, then intense pain occurs to the patient.
And it is punctured like the puncture which occurs to one who has a wound of the lung, because the bone punctures the diaphragm, and difficulty of breathing and coughing and the ejection of much blood also occur to him; and this is difficult to treat, and the ancients devised many stratagems for it.
And some of them said that it is necessary to make the patient's food that which produces flatulence and wind, so that the abdomen may swell and expand and the fracture may be driven outwards. And we dislike this, lest it be a confirmation of the occurrence of hot inflammation if it has not occurred; and if it has occurred, then it increases it and confirms it.
And some of them said a cupping glass should be placed on the place, then sucked strongly; and this is more like the correct method, except that it is feared that the cupping glass will draw impurities to the place on account of its weakness. And some of them said that it is necessary to cover the place with wool which has been dipped in hot oil.
And it becomes a pad between the ribs until it is full, so that the bandage is even when you wrap it around circularly. Then you treat the patient with the treatment of a wound, in food and medicine. If the patient is afflicted by a severe matter that he cannot bear, And the bone punctured the diaphragm a harmful puncture, and we feared for the patient; therefore it is necessary that we incise the place and uncover the broken rib, then place under it the instrument which protects the peritoneum, which has been described before, and cut the bone gently and take it out.
Then we bring together the edges of the wound if it is large by stitching, and treat it with ointments until it heals. If a hot inflammation occurs during this, then act quickly: wet pads in rose oil and place on the place, and treat the patient with what cools the inflammation from the inside also.
And he lies on the side on which sleep is easier for him until he recovers.
Chapter Nine.
On the reduction of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if a fracture affects themand this rarely happens, and contusion happens to them more often, and likewise to the vertebrae of the back alsoif this happens to someone and you want to know whether he will recover or not, then look; and if you see that his hands have become relaxed, numb, and dead, and he is not able to move them, nor extend them, nor contract them, and if you pinch them or prick them with a needle, he does not feel it and does not find pain in them, then know that in most instances he will not recover; he is doomed.
But if he moves them and feels the pinching and pricking in them, then know that the marrow of the bone is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment. If the vertebrae of the back are similarly affected and you want to know whether he will recover or not,
look at his legs; and if you see that they have become relaxed and that what happened in the hands has happened in them, then if he lies on his back, wind and feces are passed involuntarily, and if he lies on his stomach, urine is passed involuntarily, and if he lies on his back and wants to urinate, he is unable to do so, then know that he is doomed; do not trouble yourself with his treatment. If none of this happens to him, the matter is less serious. The treatment of what has happened is that you aim to alleviate the hot swelling.
by placing on the injured vertebra rose oil alone, or with roasted egg yolks; apply this three times a day, until the hot swelling subsides. Then apply to the place one of the strengthening, drying poultices, and bind it tightly with a bandage, and order him to remain still and quiet.
And let him sleep only on the side where he feels no pain, until he recovers. If, at the time of the injury, a splinter or something has become embedded in the bone, from which he has not recovered, then it is necessary to incise the skin and remove that bone. Then bring together the edges of the wound, if it is large, with sutures, then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the coccyx is brokenthat is, the tip of the tailbonethen it is necessary to insert the thumb of the left hand into the rectum.
and set the broken bone with the other hand, as much as is possible and the setting allows, then apply a poultice and splint if necessary, then bind it.
If you feel a broken splinter in it, then incise it and remove it, and treat the wound with the previously mentioned treatment until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the setting of a fracture of the hip.
The hip bones rarely fracture; if they do fracture, it is only by their extremities being shattered and split lengthwise, and they incline inwards.  The patient experiences pain in the affected area, numbness, and tingling in the leg on the side of the fracture.
Its treatment is that you pass your hand over it until you ascertain the nature of the fracture. If the fracture is only in its extremities, then set that fracture according to what seems best to you for its reduction, until its natural shape is resembled.
If the fracture is lengthwise, or if it has inclined inwards, lay the patient on his stomach so that the setting of that fracture may be facilitated for you. When you have set it, apply a bandage, then place a splint of wood or leather upon it and bind it firmly.
Do not fear for him the displacement of the fracture or the loosening of the splint.  Fill the hollows of the loins with something to fill them, so that the binding may be even. Instruct the patient to lie on his back or his sound side. If hot inflammation should occur,
desist from setting and binding it until the hot inflammation subsides. Apply what will alleviate it, as previously described; then return to setting and binding it as is proper. If splinters or shattered fragments of bone are present, they should not be removed.
They should not be touched, but smoothed from the outside as we have said, and the binding should be left until healing occurs.
Chapter Eleven.
On the setting of a fracture of the humerus.
The humerus is that which is between the elbow and the shoulder joint. If it is fractured, it is set in one of two ways. One is to take a smooth, curved stick of medium thickness, in this manner:
And you tie two bandages to its ends, then it is hung from a high place, and the patient sits on a chair, then his broken arm is placed on the stick until his armpit is touching the middle of the curve of the stick, then something heavy is hung from above it.
Or a servant stretches it downwards, then the physician sets the fracture with his hands together until he sets the fracture as it should be.  The other method is that the patient lies on his back and his hand is hung from his neck with a bandage.
Then you order two servants, one to hold above the fracture with his hands and the other to hold below it, and each stretches to his side. And if you want the traction to be stronger, then tighten below the fracture with a bandage and above it with a bandage, and each of the servants stretches to his side.
If the fracture is near the shoulder joint, then the middle of the bandage should be under the armpit and the other under the fracture towards the elbow. Similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, then the bandage should be in that place and on the elbow itself.
Then you set the fracture gently without violence, until the fracture is set as it should be and is well united. Then you bind it if no hot swelling appears; if a hot swelling appears, then leave binding it until the seventh day, and apply moistened wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides, then bind it.  The method of binding is to apply the dressing to the fracture, then apply a bandage of new cloth over the dressing, then gather the arm against the upper arm itself and place the hand open on the shoulder.
And apply the cloth and binding to the upper arm and forearm so that the forearm acts as a splint, unless something prevents you from doing so, and nothing changes in the broken bone; if you fear that something will change, then use splints.
This is to place on the fracture itself a splint that is wider and stronger than the other splints, and let there be the width of a finger between each splint, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture, with the addition of three fingers on each side.
Then bind the splints with the binding that I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And that your binding on the site of the fracture should be tighter.
And the further from the fracture, the less the binding should be; if you see that the placement of the splints and the binding, as we said, are suitable at the time of your setting of the limb, then do so.
And if you fear hot swelling, leave off the binding and splints until the seventh day, as we said; then examine the bandage every three days, lest there occur at the site an irritation or swelling, or the nourishment be prevented from reaching the limb because of excessive tightness; and rectify all this as we have described.
But if you are confident that none of that will occur, do not loosen the bandage except after many days; and let the patient lie on his back with his hand on his stomach, and a moderately full cushion of wool be placed under the arm.
And examine at every time, night or day, lest the shape of the broken limb be disarranged or the bandage become loose; then rectify all this with your effort, and make the patient's diet in the order which we have presented, that the food should be light at first, until the bone is about to consolidate.
Then it is necessary to increase his food; for it is the nature of the arm and leg to consolidate in forty days; then it is necessary to loosen [the bandages], and he should use the bath and be treated with ointments which are suitable for this; if the fracture is extensive and comminuted.
Then do not loosen the bandage from him until fifty days or two months.
Chapter Twelve.
On the setting of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, and they are called the ulna; one of them is small, and it is that which is next to the thumb, and the other is large, and it is situated below the small one from below.  Therefore, perhaps the larger ulna is broken alone, or the smaller one alone, and perhaps they are broken together.
When the smaller upper ulna is broken, then its setting is easy, and its healing is quicker. When the lower ulna is broken, then its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.  And the worst is if the two bones are broken together. If the bone which is broken is the smaller upper ulna,
it is necessary for the physician, when setting it, to make its extension easy and gently until he straightens it. If the large ulna is broken, then it is necessary to make its extension stronger. And if both ulnas are broken, then it is necessary to make the extension much stronger.
And it is necessary that the shape of the hand, when it is set and extended, is extended on a cushion, and the thumb of the hand is above, higher than all the fingers, and the little finger is lower than the rest of the fingers, and the patient sits squarely on his own.
And let the cushion be opposite him in height, lest the patient suffer difficulty.
Then a servant extends the arm from below, either with his hand or with a bandage, and another servant extends from above likewise. Then the physician straightens the bone until he returns it to the best shape he can. If there are splinters in the fracture of the bone, then they are removed.
Return each splinter to its place to the best of your ability. If a splinter appears detached and it pierces the skin, and there is no hope of setting it, then incise upon it and extract it in the manner we have previously mentioned. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, then a separate chapter has been devoted to it.
Then take treatment for that from there. If a hot swelling appears at the beginning of your setting, then apply a cloth with  kyrati prepared with rose oil and white wax, and let it be moderate between thickness and thinness, and bind the cloth to it gently until the swelling subsides.
Then remove the kyrati and apply the prepared plaster of mill dust with egg white. Then apply the splints, and let the splint that is placed on the fracture itself be somewhat wider and stronger. And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most cases.
Whether the fracture was in one forearm bone or in both forearm bones together, then make your binding on the site of the fracture stronger and tighter. And the further you go with the binding upwards or downwards, make the binding slightly looser, as previously mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, not very hard. And let the thread with which it is bound be of special flax, moderate between thinness and thickness, as we have described. And examine the limb and the bandage after a few days.
If something happens that needs to be corrected, such as an itching that appears in the limb, then the limb should be washed with warm water until that itching subsides. And the limb should be left unbandaged for a night so that it may rest, then the binding should be resumed.
If the binding has become loose and the bone has shifted or something like that, then correct all of that to the best of your ability. And look also, if the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness, then it should be loosened a little and left for a few days until nourishment flows to it, then tighten it.
If nothing of what we have mentioned happens to the patient, then it should not be undone except after twenty days or so. Then suspend the patient's hand to his neck, and let his arm be straight. And he should guard against strenuous, agitated movements, and let his sleep be on his back.
Know that this fracture of the arm heals in thirty days, or in thirty-two, and perhaps it heals in twenty-eight days; all that according to the conditions of the temperaments and the conditions of strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the setting of a fracture of the hand and fingers.
The metacarpus of the hand and the phalanges of the fingers rarely suffer fracture, but rather they frequently suffer contusion.  So when a fracture or contusion occurs to the hand...
The patient should sit cross-legged, and in front of him a chair evenly placed.
Then he places his hand upon it extended, then the assistant extends the broken bones and the physician sets them, until when they are well-set; then it is necessary to apply a bandage and a splint if there is no hot inflammation. Then a splint is applied above according to the place.
And it is inserted into a soft cloth.  If the fracture is towards the lower part, towards the palm of the hand, then make a kind of sphere from a cloth and tell the patient to grasp it with his broken hand, then it is tightened with a long cloth.
And let the splint be of leather, soft so that it conforms to the skin with the bending of the whole hand, and tighten it as necessary. If the fracture is outward, then it is necessary to place a splint above and another splint below in the hand so that the hand is open and upright.
Then the bandage is applied as the hand rotates and interlaces between the fingers with the ligature. If the fracture affects one of the phalanges of the fingers, if it is the thumb, then let it be set as necessary, then it is bandaged with the hand. And if you wish, make for it a small, upright splint to support the fracture.
And it does not move. If the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle, index, little, or ring finger, then let them be set and bandaged with the healthy finger next to it, or all are bandaged together which is better, or a small, upright splint is placed on them.
As we said in the case of the thumb, and examine during your setting and afterwards for hot swelling, and counter it as necessary whenever anything of that occurs, as has been repeatedly described.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the setting of a fracture of the thigh.
The thigh bone frequently breaks and is apparent to the touch because it moves forward and backward, and its setting is done by tightening a bandage above the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, the patient lying on his stomach.
Then each bandage is extended to its side moderately; this if the fracture is in the middle of the bone. But if it is near the head of the thigh, then it is necessary to tighten a soft bandage, either of wool or similar.
At the head of the thigh towards the groin so that the extension is upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture.  Likewise, if the fracture is near the knee, let the bandage be near the knee so that the extension is downwards. Then the physician straightens the bone with both his hands until he restores it to its natural form.
And the bone unites well. Then it is necessary to maintain the bandage and tightening, if a hot inflammation does not occur in the limb. But if the inflammation occurs, leave it for some days until the hot inflammation subsides, then return to its treatment.
As for its tightening, it is necessary to wrap around the fracture a firm, wide bandage twice or three times, and what remains is left. Then the leg is twisted until the heel is at the origin of the buttock, and a long thread is inserted between the thigh and leg near the knee from below, and the ends of the thread are above on both sides.
Then you wrap around the leg and thigh what remains of the bandage. Then you place splints on the thigh at the place of the fracture itself, and you place one splint on the bone of the leg. Then stuff the space between the thigh and leg with soft cloths so that the tightening is even.
Then begin with strong tightening from the middle at the place of the fracture, three or four turns. And the further the bandage is from the place of the fracture, let your tightening be less, softer, and looser.
Then take the ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and leg, and tie with it what is next to it of the splints from above. Then pass the ends of the thread downwards until you reach the heel of the foot, and tighten with them also the ends of the splints from the other side.
Lest the bandage move from its place. Then leave the tightening on it as long as no pain, swelling, or puffiness, and the like, occurs in the limb. If any of that happens, hasten to untie it and correct whatever occurred of all that as we have repeatedly mentioned.
If there is a splinter in the bone that is sunken, then it is necessary to straighten that if you can. Otherwise, incise upon it and remove it, and treat the wound as previously mentioned until it heals.  And this fracture of the thigh may be bound without adding the leg to it with splints.
As we mentioned in the humerus and forearm, except that its treatment in this way does not result in lameness for the patient.  But if it is set alone without adding the leg to it, then it is necessary that its owner will always limp.
Know that the thigh heals in fifty days, or a little more or less; all that according to the differences in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the fracture of the kneecap.
Fracture of the kneecap rarely occurs, but it is frequently bruised. If a fracture does occur, it is either a crack or a shattering into pieces, and this happens with or without a wound.
You will ascertain all this by touch, and you will set it by aligning the separated fragments with your fingers until they are united and joined, as far as the alignment, gentleness, and skill allow. Then apply a dressing and, if necessary, a circular splint.
Then tighten the appropriate bandage over it, and treat all the conditions that we have described in other fractures, such as hot swelling and the like, counteracting each complication with the appropriate treatment until healing occurs.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the treatment of leg fractures.
The leg has two bones; one is thick and is called the tibia, and the other is thin and is called the fibula.  They are subject to the same types of fractures as the bones of the arm. Therefore, its treatment is the same as that of the arm, and the procedure is identical. If both bones are fractured, the leg is displaced in all directions; if the thinner bone is fractured, the leg is displaced forward; and if the thicker bone is fractured, especially in the lower part, the result is obvious.
Therefore, use traction and reduction, and apply splints accordingly, except that if the leg fracture is severe with many fragments, the traction should be less and gentler, and you should be careful in its treatment.
In treating the leg, there is something in addition to what is done for the arm:  When you have set the splints and completed all your work, take two small pieces of pine wood, the kind used in smoothing the floors laid between the cracks of planks, or use date-palm leaflets or something similar.
Choose pieces of moderate thickness, not thin ones, and let their length equal the length of the leg from the knee downwards. Then wrap each one with two layers of cloth lengthwise, and place one on one side of the leg and the other on the other side.
Let them extend from the knee to the lower part of the foot. Then bind the two small pieces of wood in three places: at both ends and in the middle. By this means, the leg is prevented from tilting to either side and is well supported.  A wooden gutter along the length of the leg may also be used.
It is placed in it to keep it from moving, and this is mostly necessary in fractures accompanied by wounds. Then examine the leg every day and take the greatest care to prevent swelling, inflammation, or anything else. If any of this occurs, treat it appropriately until it heals. A leg fracture heals in about thirty days, or so.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the fracture of the bones of the leg and toes.
As for the heel, it is never fractured; as for the bones of the leg, they may be fractured; and as for the toes, they are rarely fractured, but rather are more often bruised.
If a leg bone is fractured and you see that some of the bones are overlapping, then have the patient stand with their foot on the ground, as if walking.  Then, you place your foot on the elevated bone and press down on it to straighten it.
Until they return to their proper places. Then apply a bandage and splint from above, placing a small board with two flat ends under the sole of the foot.  Then bind the foot tightly after wrapping it with cloths and whatever else is needed.
If three or four days have passed, untie the bandage; you will find the bones straight, whether they were fractured or dislocated. The procedure is as you see. As for a fractured toe, set it and straighten it according to what I have described for you in the setting of the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the fractured toe, the length of the toe and slightly wider. Then place the board I described under the foot and bind it tightly. If two, three, or more bones are fractured,
make a splint for each toe, of appropriate size, layered in soft cloth, and bind the board to the bottom of the foot. Let each end of the board rest outside the sole of the foot to secure it properly.
You should not forget to attend to all that we have mentioned to you in other fractures, the symptoms we have mentioned, and treat each symptom as it should be.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the fracture of the woman's pubis and the man's penis.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, seat her cross-legged, then gently lean her backward.  Let her be held from behind, then let the midwife pack her vulva with cotton until it is full and forms a ball within her vulva.
Then the woman is shaken and her hip is raised little by little; the cotton will come out until it is at the opening of the vulva like a ball, and the fractured bone will return to its place.  Then a dressing is placed on her back. When she wishes to urinate, the cotton is gently removed so that she may urinate.
And it is returned in the manner in which it was initially inserted, then she returns to her first dressing.  This is done for seven days or so, and it will heal. And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder, tie a reed tube to its mouth, and insert the whole bladder into her vulva.
Then blow forcefully into the tube until the bladder inflates inside the vulva; the fracture will be reduced. Then pack it with cotton and keep it as we described until it heals. As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, the same treatment is used to set it.
And its treatment is as we described for the hip bone. The correct procedure for these unusual fractures, which rarely occur, especially for one who has some experience and understands this book well, will not be hidden from you, for the fracture itself will indicate the treatment in most instances.
Understand the correct way to set and bind it. As for a fracture of the penis, take a goose's windpipe, insert the penis into it, then apply a bandage of cloth, bind it, and leave it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
On setting bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
Whoever suffers a fracture with a wound, especially if the bone is large, such as the femur or humerus or similar, should proceed immediately to bloodletting if the conditions for bloodletting, as we have previously explained, are favorable.
If the wound is bleeding, you should immediately stop it by applying powdered vitriol if nothing else is available, then set the fracture that same day and do not delay.
If no hot swelling occurs, then good.  If hot swelling occurs, delay setting it until the ninth day until the hot swelling subsides. Do not touch it at all on the third or fourth day, for it will be exposed to unfavorable symptoms.
If the fractured bone is protruding and exposed through the skin, you should try to reduce and align it gently with your hands with a slight pull. If you are unable to reduce and align it with your hands, reduce it with this instrument. It is an instrument made of iron, seven or eight finger-lengths in length. And its width is according to the wound.
Therefore, the physician should prepare three or four of them, according to what he needs for treatment of each type of fracture. Let them be round, and slightly thick so that they do not bend when pressure is applied during use.
And let the ends be sharp, with a curve at the end. The upper part should be thicker, and from the middle to the lower part much thinner.  This is its image:
It is called in Greek *peron*, meaning a small lever.  The sharp, curved end should be placed on the end of the protruding bone, and it should be pushed gently until the bone returns and is somewhat aligned. Then, align the ends of the fracture with each other.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not grasp it well, then the end of that bone should be cut until the instrument can grasp it. If you are not able to reset the bone in the manner described, then cut it with a similar cut to those we have mentioned.
Or saw it apart however you can. Then, remove any roughness and thin layers remaining on the bone. If, after resetting it, the patient experiences severe, persistent pain, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you are able to return it to its natural position, then do so, for you will greatly benefit the patient. When you have completed the setting of the bone, dip a cloth in a black astringent drink, especially if it is summer, and do not apply any ointment or anything greasy to the wound.
lest putrefaction and corruption occur. Then, apply the splints after you have finished setting the bone, and leave the wound exposed by cutting a hole in the bandages with scissors, the size of the wound.
And be very careful not to bind the wound tightly with the fracture, for many ignorant physicians have done this, causing their patients either death, or gangrene, or tetanus. Let your binding be gentle and loose, unlike the binding of other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or if it is a large wound and you fear some of the bad symptoms we have described, and he feels disturbing pain in the area, then you should not apply splints, but make bandages from firm cloths in the place of the splints and bind it with them.
If after a day or two you see that pus is beginning to form in the wound, remove the cloth that you applied with the astringent drink, then use lint and ointments which we customarily use to treat wounds, such as the tetrapharmacon and the like, and you should loosen the bandage.
And examine the wound every evening and morning until it heals, and you should position the limb so that the pus flows downwards easily. If many days pass and the wound does not unite and the pus does not stop, then know that there are small bone fragments there.
Then you should probe the wound; whatever of those fragments is loose, remove and extract it; and whatever of them is not loose and is injuring the limb and causing pain, then strive to cut and extract it in every way you can.
If the wound develops inflammation, gangrene, or another type of corruption and putrefaction, then you should counter each symptom with its corresponding treatment, which we have previously described in its chapter.
And what you should pay attention to and keep in mind is that if a large bone is fractured and projects from the limb, such as the thigh bone, the humerus, and similar large bones, do not attempt to pull or extract it. For death often results from this; rather, leave it until it putrefies, for perhaps it will fall out by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see a place for treatment; otherwise, leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
In the treatment of complications which occur after some fractures.
This complication often occurs after the healing of a fracture, especially those near the joints, and it disfigures the limb and may prevent the limb from its natural function.
Examine it; if the complication is fresh, use astringent medications such as aloes, frankincense, myrrh, gum tragacanth, acacia, and the like. Take some or all of these and mix them with an astringent syrup, egg white, or vinegar.
Apply it to the complication with a bandage and bind it tightly. Leave the bandage in place for many days, then remove it and reapply another until, God willing, the complication disappears, or fasten a tight lead plate upon it.
For lead has the property of removing all that is superfluous in the limbs. If the complication has hardened and become severe, and necessity dictates its removal, incise it from above and cut away the excess growth, or shave it off with some sharp instrument until it is gone, and then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In the treatment of a fracture if it has healed, and the limb remains thin and not as it was originally.
If a bone fracture has healed and the limb remains thin and weak, this is due to many causes. One of them is either from excessive loosening and binding of the bandages, or from excessive tightening of the bandages so that nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb, or from excessive and repeated rubbing, or from excessive movement at the wrong time, or from a deficiency of blood and weakness in the patient's body.
And the treatment of this is to nourish the patient and enrich his body so that the blood increases in it, and to use the bath and to bring him joy and happiness and the like; then to apply pitch to the limb so that the pitch draws much nourishment to it, or to repeatedly rub it with lukewarm water. until nourishment flows into it and it returns to its natural form.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
In the treatment of fractured bones if they are set crookedly and prevented from functioning properly.
If, after a limb has healed, a crookedness or a projection of the fractured bone or a complication appears, and because of that the appearance of the limb is spoiled. However, if the limb does not prevent its natural function, then one should not accept the opinion of those who claim that the limb should be broken from the head, as many ignorant physicians and bone-setters used to do in our country.
And this action is highly reprehensible, leading to great harm, the least of which is damage. But if the crookedness and complication are recent, then it should be treated with water in which emollient herbs have been cooked, such as mallow leaves and root, and rosemary, and the like.
And it is bandaged with emollient bandages such as well-made diachylon, or the sap of the mallow root is taken and beaten with chicken fat and sherbet oil and bandaged with it, or ripe figs are taken and pounded with pigeon droppings and similar medicines called anti-inflammatory.
And sometimes the complication resolves with constant gentle massage by hand, and the movement of the limb in every direction at all times is used. If the crookedness has become old and severe and hardened, and treatment with iron is necessary.
Then it should be incised above and the connection of the bone released, and the excess of the complication or bone cut with fine cuts, and gentleness should be used with effort and care, then the wound is treated as previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
A dislocation is the displacement of a joint from its place, hindering movement, disfiguring the limb, and causing the patient severe pain and suffering.  When a dislocation occurs, one should immediately attempt to reduce it.
One should not delay at all, for if delayed, the area will swell and make reduction of the dislocation difficult. Therefore, one should not delay, move, or stretch it while it is swollen, because this often causes the patient spasms and harmful pain.
But if this occurs, one should immediately bleed the patient, then wait until the swelling subsides somewhat, then bathe the limb in warm water and oil, and then gently reduce it, treating each limb as described in its proper place.
The chapters on dislocations are also arranged, as with fractures, from the top of the body to the bottom.
Chapter twenty-four.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The jaws rarely become dislocated, and their dislocation occurs in one of two ways: either they are slightly displaced and relax a little, or they are completely dislocated so that they relax completely. towards the chest, so that the saliva of the patient flows and he cannot restrain it, nor can he close his jaw, and his tongue stumbles in speech. But if its dislocation is slight, it often returns of its own accord very easily.
But if the dislocation is complete, it should be reduced quickly and not delayed at all.  A servant should hold the patient's head, and the physician should insert the thumb of one hand into the base of the jaw inside the mouth, if the jaw is dislocated on one side.
or he should insert both thumbs if the jaw is dislocated on both sides, and the rest of his fingers from outside to align them, and he should order the patient to relax his jaw and release it to go to every side, while the physician aligns the jaw and pushes it until it returns to its place.
If its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated, then apply a warm water and oil compress until it is easy to reduce them, and do not delay their reduction at all, as we said. When they have returned and become aligned and the patient's mouth is closed and they are not relaxed, then apply compresses of rags with a plaster made of wax, oil, and rose water.
Then bind it gently with a loose bandage, and let the patient sleep on his back with his head raised between two pillows, so that he does not move it to the right or left, and let him not strain to chew anything, but let his food be soft gruel.
Until the pain subsides and the jaw is set, let him eat whatever he wishes, and let him use it gently and not force the opening of his mouth when eating, drinking, or yawning, until the jaw is set and healed.
If the reduction of the jaws is difficult, if they are dislocated at some time and do not return to their places, fevers and persistent headaches often result from this, and the patient's abdomen may become distended, and he may vomit pure bile. If you see this, know that it is damaged.
And many die from this within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
In the reduction of the clavicle and the tip of the shoulder.
As for the clavicle, it does not dislocate from the inner side because of its connection to the chest. It may dislocate outwards, and this is apparent to the touch. Its reduction is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms.
Then press the place with your hand strongly, and it will return. Then apply a bandage and pads and tighten it. As for its end which is near the shoulder and connected to it, it does not dislocate except rarely. If it dislocates at some time, then it should be reduced and set as we have mentioned and as is possible for you.
Then apply to it a bandage and pads and tightening, and you should order the patient to observe rest and stillness until it heals. By this same treatment you reduce the tip of the shoulder if it also moves from its place.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
In the reduction of the shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder dislocates in three ways: one is that it dislocates towards the armpit downwards; the second is that it dislocates towards the chest; and sometimes it dislocates upwards towards the shoulder, and this happens rarely.
It does not dislocate to the back because of the shoulder blade, nor does it dislocate to the front because of the nerve. Most often it dislocates and goes downwards towards the armpit, especially in those whose flesh is little, because it goes out quickly in them and enters quickly.
But as for those whose flesh is abundant, it is otherwise, I mean that it goes out with difficulty and enters with difficulty. And sometimes a blow or a fall happens to some people, and the shoulder swells with a hot swelling, so they think that it has dislocated.  It is necessary to examine this until you ascertain its truth.
Then you proceed in its treatment.  The dislocation is recognized if it is downwards towards the armpit; if you compare the dislocated shoulder with the sound shoulder, you will find a clear difference between them, and you will find the head of the humerus in it to be twisted; and under the armpit, on palpation, the head of the humerus is like an egg.
And the patient is not able to raise his hand to his ear, nor to move it in all movements.  Similarly, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, you will find this apparent to the touch, not hidden.
And this dislocation is easy to reduce if it is recent or if the patient is a child.  Its reduction is that a servant raises his hand upwards, then you place the thumbs of your hands under his armpit and raise the joint forcefully upwards to its place, and the servant raises his hand and extends it upwards.
Then he lowers it downwards, and it returns quickly. If it does not return by what we have mentioned, and the dislocation has been for many days, then the patient should bathe in hot water and use things that relax and soften, such as boiling the root of mallow, fenugreek and myrtle in water and using it.
Then the patient lies on his back, and you place under his armpit a ball of wool that is moderate between soft and hard; then the physician places his heel on the ball and holds the patient's hand with his hand and extends it downwards.
And he pushes the ball with his foot, and it returns to its place. And if you wish, reduce it in this way: you bring a taller man than the patient and station him on the side, and he puts his shoulder under the patient's armpit and raises his armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. And another servant pulls the patient's hand downwards towards his stomach. If the patient is light, then something else should be hung on him to weigh him down, and the jaw will return immediately. And it may also be reduced in another way.
And that is to firmly place in the ground a long piece of wood, its head being round in shape like the pestle of a mortar, neither thick nor thin, then it is placed under the patient's armpit after placing a soft cloth on the head of the wood, and the patient standing along the length of the wood.
Then you extend his hand downwards from the other side, and also extend his body from the other side forcefully, and the joint will return to its place quickly. If its reduction is difficult with all that we have mentioned, then use this treatment: you take a piece of wood about two cubits long. And its width is the size of four fingers and its thickness the size of two fingers; it has a round head to facilitate its entry deep into the armpit in this manner:
Then you tie a soft cloth to the round head so that the wood does not hurt the patient; then it is placed under the armpit, and the whole hand and arm are extended along the wood downwards, and the wood is tied to the upper arm, forearm and hand.
Then the arm is placed on a horizontal beam, the hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is left hanging from the other side. Then the joint will enter immediately. If it enters in any way possible, then a moderately sized ball of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then the prepared bandage, made from mill dust, frankincense, and egg white, is applied to the whole shoulder as it rotates from above. Then the ball under the armpit is tightly bound, and the bandage is rotated from above, and his hand is suspended to his neck.
He is left without moving his hand for seven days, and the patient's food should be light so that the limb may strengthen, for this is quicker for healing. Then, after seven or five days, it is untied and exercised; if it is firm and does not relax, then it is healed.
If the joint dislocates repeatedly due to dampness or some other cause, then cauterization with three cautery irons should be used as described in the chapter on cauterization. If all this has been done, and the bandage is removed after seven days and the joint is not stable
then the bandage and binding are repeated many times, but it does not stabilize, and it falls and relaxes, and you cannot raise it upwards; then know that the nerve in the head of the humerus has been severed, stretched, or relaxed. Then know that the joint will never be stable in its place.
As for a dislocation which is towards the chest and breast, and backwards, its reduction is by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns.  All the binding and treatment are used until it heals. If, after healing, there is stiffness in the limb and slowness of movement
then the patient should use the bath repeatedly until that stiffness softens and returns to its original nature.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the treatment of dislocation of the elbow.
The elbow joint is difficult to dislocate and also difficult to reduce. It is dislocated in all directions, especially forwards or backwards.  Its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is visible and palpable, whichever way it is dislocated.
And if you compare the dislocated elbow with the sound one, it will be clearly evident to you by the depression of the joint, and he will not be able to bend his arm or touch his shoulder with it. And you should act quickly and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a hot inflammation occurs.
For if a hot inflammation occurs, reduction is difficult, and it may not heal at all, especially if the dislocation is backwards, for this is the worst of all types of dislocation, and the most painful, and often death ensues.
And its reduction, if it is of a kind that can be reduced, is that an assistant extends his hand with both hands and his arm extended, and the physician's hands are above and below the elbow, and he pushes the joint with the thumbs of both his hands or with the base of his palms until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is forward, it may be reduced by bending the hand sharply until the base of the hand strikes the corresponding shoulder. If the dislocation does not reduce, use very strong traction, that is, let two assistants extend the arm.
And let two assistants also hold the patient so that he does not move during traction. Then the arm is rotated in every direction after wrapping a folded cloth or wide bandage around his hand. And when the physician touches the joint with his hands, let him anoint them with oil so that it helps in easily slipping the joint.
Then he pushes the joint strongly until it returns. After its return, it is necessary to apply a bandage containing astringents and drying agents with egg white, and it is tightened firmly, and the arm is suspended from the patient's neck and left for several days.
Then it is untied. If the joint is stable in its place, untie the bandage and leave it. But if you see that the joint is not firm, reapply the bandage and leave it for several days until it becomes firm, then untie it. If swelling occurs after the reduction of the joint and its untying,
and difficulty in movement occurs, use moist applications in the bath and gentle massage and manipulation until it softens, or place a poultice of fat mutton on the joint, then bandage it and leave it on for a day and night, then remove it and put him in the bath. When he sweats, massage the joint moderately.
Then reapply the poultice once, twice, and three times with a bath until it softens. And if you wish to apply a moist, light poultice of cow dung with fat and tighten it, do that several times, for it will soften and return to its former state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
In the treatment of the fractured wrist.
The wrist often becomes dislocated, and its reduction is easier than that of other joints, except that it is necessary to hasten its reduction at the time it is dislocated, before the area becomes inflamed or a hot swelling appears.
To reduce the dislocation, place the patient's wrist on a board, and have an assistant extend his hand.  The physician should place his hand on the prominence of the joint and push until it returns. However, it is necessary to observe whether the dislocation has shifted to the palm of the hand.
If the dislocation is prominent towards the back of the hand, let the patient place the palm of his hand on the board during extension and reduction. If the dislocation is prominent towards the back of the hand, let him place the back of his hand on the board so that the physician's hand falls on the prominence of the joint itself.
