If it returns immediately, well and good; otherwise, bind it with a bandage that soothes the swelling.
Leave it and do not manipulate it again, for it will not tolerate it, and you will not be able to reduce it after several days have passed, except that the joint will remain crooked and will not harm the patient in any way, unless the hand becomes weak and unable to grasp anything.
Then you will know that the nerve has been severed or crushed; there is no remedy for this except cauterization, which may or may not be effective. When the wrist has been reduced, apply the bandage we described, then tighten it and leave it for five days.
Then unbind it and exercise the hand. If its movement is difficult and there is any stiffness, soften it with warm water and massage repeatedly until it softens.
Chapter twenty-nine.
On the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Fingers may be dislocated in any direction. If a finger is dislocated towards the back or palm of the hand, extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns. Then bandage the tip of the finger and suspend it towards the direction in which it was dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it is straight that day. If it is nighttime, bandage it in the same way; keep releasing it during the day and exercising it with movement, and bandaging it at night. Do this for several days until it strengthens.
Do the same if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand; bandage it towards that same direction and do as you did before until it heals.  Do likewise if it is dislocated in any other direction.
Chapter Thirty.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
If a vertebra of the back or neck is completely dislocated, or if many vertebrae are displaced from their positions, there is no treatment, for death swiftly overtakes the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's excrement is passed involuntarily, and he cannot retain it; and often some of his limbs become relaxed, either his legs or his arms, or one of them. But if only one vertebra is displaced from its position, it often returns to its place.
And its displacement is in four directions. That which is displaced backwards is called a hump. As for its treatment, you should observe: if the hump has occurred from childhood, there is no treatment for it, and there is absolutely no cure for it. But if it has occurred from a fall or a blow or the like,
the ancients have mentioned it with various treatments in lengthy descriptions, most of which are of no benefit. I have abridged this, taking what is sufficient from the much they have presented, by approaching the meaning and explaining it, and I have depicted the instrument differently from what they have explained and described.
I say that the hump which occurs in the front of the chest cannot be helped, and there is no cure for it; the same applies to that which occurs to the sides. Only that which occurs in the back is treated, specifically with what I describe, which is that you extend the patient on his face.
on a level bench near a wall, and spread a damp mat underneath so that his chest is not harmed. Then you place a vertical piece of wood, planted in a hole in the ground at the end of the bench towards his head, and another piece of wood towards his feet at the other end of the bench.
And a servant holds the wood, and let it not be secured in the hole, and another servant holds the other in that manner. Then wrap around the chest of the patient and under his armpits with a soft, tight cloth, and extend the end of the cloth to the wood at his head and tie it there.
Then tighten with another cloth above his hips and above his knees and at his ankles. Then gather all the bandages and tie them to the other piece of wood at his feet. Then each servant pulls on his side of the wood with the bandage, and the two pieces of wood do not move from their planted positions.
except that they are not fixed, as we said. And the physician places his hands on the hump forcefully until it returns, or he places a board on it, then he leans on the board with his feet until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three cubits.
And dig in the wall, which we said should be near the patient, a place into which the end of the board enters. Then place the middle of the board on the hump, and the physician places his feet on the other end and pulls strongly until he presses the hump.
And it returns to its place. And if you wish to make it with a screw that is twisted by hand, it is that you plant in the ground at the head of the patient at the end of the shop two pieces of wood whose length is a cubit, and at his feet at the end of the shop also two pieces of wood.
And let the distance between each piece of wood be a cubit. And a hole is made in each piece of wood through which the screw passes. And all the wood is firmly secured in the ground so that it does not move at all. And insert a round stick, which is the screw in which the bandage is twisted, into the holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its end is a hole in which a stick a cubit long is secured by what is twisted. And in the other two pieces of wood, likewise. Then tighten the bandage that you tightened in the chest of the patient in the screw that is at his head, and the bandages that you tightened in his legs in the screw that is at his feet.
Then a servant stands at each screw, twisting his hand with the instrument with which he twists the screw, and the physician straightens the hump as we have explained. And this is an image of the screw, the shop, and the patient:
Then, after the vertebra returns and the place is straightened, it is necessary to apply a drying compress with egg white, then the pledget, then place on top of the compress a splint of a board whose width is three fingers or so, and its length is as much as it takes the place of the hump.
And on some of the sound vertebrae. Then it is bound with the appropriate bandage, and the patient uses light food until he recovers. If some protrusion remains in the place at the end of the healing, then it is necessary to use medicines that relax and soften, along with the use of the board which we described, for a long time.
A lead plate may be used for this. And a protrusion may appear at the end of the vertebrae of the back, and it may be thought to be a dislocation, and it is an extra bone that has protruded. Do not treat it with this treatment, for death may result.
Chapter Thirty-One.
In the treatment of the dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and they are not subject to what happens to the other joints, of slight displacement and depression. The hip joint is dislocated in four ways.
That is, it is dislocated inwards, outwards, forwards, and backwards. Most often it is dislocated inwards, and rarely forwards or backwards. The sign of its dislocation inwards is that if you compare the sound leg of the patient with the affected one, it will be longer.
And the knee will be more bent than the sound one, and the patient will not be able to bend his leg at the groin, and the place next to the groin will be swollen, evidently because the head of the femur has gone there.
And the sign of that which is dislocated outwards is that its symptoms are the opposite of these symptoms. And the sign of that which is dislocated forwards is that he extends his leg completely, but he cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he tries to walk, he cannot do so forwards.
And he retains his urine, and his groin is swollen, and when walking, his weight is on his heel. And the sign of that which is dislocated backwards is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he bend it before bending the groin, and his leg is also shorter than the other.
And the groin is relaxed, and the head of the femur is clearly at the position of the loin. As for the reduction of the types of this dislocation, you should look; if the dislocation is old, having persisted with its sufferer and no attempt at reduction having been made, and it remains in its state, then there is absolutely no treatment for it.
Then you should not attempt it. But as for one whose dislocation is recent, whatever of the four types of dislocation it may be, hasten to twist the joint and stretch it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and to the left, for perhaps...
it will return and will not need other treatment. If it does not return in this way, then you should prepare a strong and able assistant who will stretch his leg from below, either with his hands or with a bandage tied around his leg above the knee, and another assistant will stretch him from above.
by putting his hands under his armpits, then tightening a soft bandage around the root of the thigh, and another third assistant holding the end of the bandage, and the stretching will be either from the front, from the side of the groin to the clavicle, or from behind to the side of the back.
And their stretching will all be at once, until the patient is raised with his body from the ground and remains suspended. For this type of stretching is a common type for the four types. If the dislocation returns by what we have said, otherwise, each type necessarily needs what I mention of specific treatment.
As for its specific reduction, if the dislocation is inwards, the patient should lie on his sound side, then a bandage should be applied to the root of the thigh between the head of the thigh and the place under the groin.
Then the bandage is stretched upwards from the groin to the upper body towards the clavicle, then another strong and able assistant takes it with his arms and embraces the thick part of the affected thigh and stretches it outwards with a strong pull, for then it will return to its place.
And this type is easier than the other types of treatment by which this member is reduced.  If it is difficult for you and it does not respond to this type of treatment at all, then you should tie the patient's legs together with a strong, soft bandage at the heels and at the knees.
And the distance between each [bandage] and its neighbour will be the length of four fingers, and the affected leg will be extended more than the other by the length of two fingers. Then the patient will be suspended by the head from a wooden beam which is in the house, and his distance from the ground will be the length of two arms.
Then you order a strong boy to embrace the head of the thigh, and another boy will hold onto the patient, and the other boy embracing the thigh will push with force, then the joint will return to its place quickly. As for its specific reduction if the dislocation is outwards.
Then the patient should lie on the bench, as we described for the patient with kyphosis, and a bandage is tightened on his affected leg in particular and on his chest. Then two pieces of wood are placed, one at his feet and the other at his head, then an extra piece of wood is placed in the middle of the bench, very firmly secured.
A wet cloth has been wrapped around it so that it does not harm the patient; the wood is to be placed between his thighs so that he does not be pulled downwards when extended. Then each servant pulls towards his side, and the physician sets the hip with his hands; if it responds to reduction.
Otherwise, place the board on him and press it down as we mentioned in the case of kyphosis, except that the patient should lie on his sound side. And if the dislocation is forward, then the patient's leg should be extended once while he is in this same position on the bench.
The physician places the palm of his right hand on the affected hip, then presses it with the other hand, and with that, he extends the pressure downwards towards the knee. If the dislocation is backward, then the patient should not be extended downwards.
And it is raised from the ground, but rather it should be placed on something solid, just as it should also be if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we mentioned of his lying on the bench on his face, and the bandages tightened.
As we said before, and the pressure with the board should also be applied to the place where the joint has gone.  If the reduction of the hip joint is complete, as it should be, and the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you  which is that you extend the patient's legs.
Then if you see them even, and the patient can flex and extend the leg without difficulty, know that the limb has been reduced as it should be. Then, bind the thighs together and apply the bandage, binding it firmly so that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain still for three or four days, then unbind the bandage and compare the leg with the other. If you see them equal in size, know that the reduction has held, and release the patient to walk.  If you see any laxness in it,
then re-bandage it and tighten it as you did at first, and also leave it for three days, then unbind it, and he should walk slowly on it for days until it strengthens, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
In the treatment of the reduction of the knee.
The knee dislocates in three ways: inwards, outwards, and downwards, that is, backwards; it does not dislocate forwards at all.  The sign of its dislocation is that you order the patient to bring his leg to his thigh.
If it does not adhere to the thigh, then know that the knee is dislocated. The treatment of all types of its dislocation is that you make the patient sit with his legs extended, if he is able to do so, and a servant sits behind him to hold his waist and twist it slightly backwards, then you sit on his thighs.
And you place your back against his face and put his leg between your legs, then you grasp his knee with your hands and interlock your fingers on his knee, then you press with your hands strongly on the sides of his knee, and another servant extends his leg until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh smoothly and without constraint. Then bandage it and bind the leg to the thigh, and bind them both together with a bandage for three or four days, then release them. And he should only do a little walking for a few days until it strengthens.
If it is difficult for you to return it by what we have described, then use strong traction with the bandages which I have previously described in the treatment of the hip, until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the treatment of a dislocated heel.
The heel may be slightly displaced, or it may be completely dislocated.  Its dislocation is either inwards or outwards, and the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen and prominent in the direction in which it was dislocated.
As for the treatment of its slight displacement, it is easy to return it; this is done by gently extending it with the hands and straightening it until it returns. As for its treatment if it is completely dislocated, then it is necessary that you make the patient sit, and a strong servant should hold him from behind his back at his waist.
Then you grasp his foot with your right hand from above, and with your left hand from below the foot at the place of the heel; then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then with the left, twice; then extend it with the left and push the front of the foot with the right towards the leg without force.
Do this twice as we have described, then push the front of the foot to the leg on the third time while you pull at the heel. If it returns in one or two times in this manner and you see the foot straight, then it is done; otherwise repeat the procedure, for it will return.
If it refuses to return by what we have described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground and fix a strong, firmly secured peg in the ground to be placed between his thighs, having wrapped a cloth around it lest it injure the patient; then a servant should hold his thigh.
Then another servant extends the leg, either with his hands or with a rope which he ties around the neck of the leg; then each servant extends in opposition to the other, the peg remaining between the patient's thighs, holding him so that his body is not drawn downwards during extension; then the physician straightens the joint with his hands.
And another servant holds the sound leg below, for the joint returns quickly; when the joint returns and you see that its return is correct, then apply the bandage and splint, and bind it tightly with bandages, and bind the foot with the bandage below; and it is necessary to protect the nerve which is above the heel from behind.
lest the bandage be tight upon it and injure it; then leave it for two or three days. If the bandage loosens, then tighten it, and then loosen it on the third or fourth day; and the patient must abstain from walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this time, you cannot be sure that the joint will not be dislocated again and spoiled, and will not then accept treatment. If a hot swelling appears, then it is necessary that you use for its alleviation what we have previously described in another place concerning treatment and cleaning, until it disappears.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
In the treatment of the dislocation of the toes of the foot.
It is necessary to set what is dislocated with a slight extension without force; and this is not difficult, but easy. If the dislocation is in some of the joints of the instep, then it is necessary that the patient place his foot on a level surface of the ground.
Or on a board while he is standing like one walking, then you stand and place your foot on what is dislocated from those joints, then press it down with your foot firmly until it returns and you see that it has become straight and no prominence appears in the place.
Then place under the sole of his foot a board which takes the whole foot, which has two ends, then bind it tightly and firmly for three days, then unbind it and keep him from walking for many days until it strengthens and you are safe from a relapse, God willing.
Chapter thirty-five.
On the types of dislocation which occurs with a wound or with a fracture or with both together.
When something of that happens and its treatment and setting are neglected, it often results in death.
Therefore, no one should undertake the treatment of such a thing except one who is skillful in the art, long experienced, gentle, compassionate, deliberate, not rash or reckless, and that he should use in the beginning only those medicines which calm hot swellings and reassure the patient.
For the power of God, except that for which I hoped for safety from harm with mildness of the disease and some hope appeared to you in it, then return it from its place at the first, before the hot swelling occurs.
If the member returns as you wished, then use the treatment which calms hot swellings and treat the wound with what is suitable for it of drying ointments; if the dislocation is with a fracture and there occur in the bone splinters which are separated, then remove them. And observe in that what we mentioned in the simple diseases which preceded in their places.
And strive your best and refrain yourself from entering the path of risk as my advice to you preceded; that is more enduring for your reputation and safer for your honor, God willing.
